the Tenant of Wildfell Hall
AUTHOR's PREFACE to the SECOND EDITION
while I acknowledge the success of the present work to have be
great than I anticipate, and the praise it have elicit from a
few kind critic to have be great than it deserve, I must also
admit that from some other quarter it have be censure with an
asperity which I be as little prepared to expect, and which my
judgment, as well as my feeling, assure I be more bitter than
just. it be scarcely the province of an author to refute the
argument of his censor and vindicate his own production; but I
may be allow to make here a few observation with which I would
have preface the first edition, have I foresee the necessity of
such precaution against the misapprehension of those who would
read it with a prejudiced mind or be content to judge it by a hasty
glance.
my object in write the follow page be not simply to amuse
the Reader; neither be it to gratify my own taste, nor yet to
ingratiate myself with the Press and the public: I wish to tell
the truth, for truth always convey its own moral to those who be
able to receive it. but as the priceless treasure too frequently
hide at the bottom of a well, it need some courage to dive for
it, especially as he that do so will be likely to incur more
scorn and obloquy for the mud and water into which he have venture
to plunge, than thank for the jewel he procure; as, in like
manner, she who undertake the cleansing of a careless bachelor's
apartment will be liable to more abuse for the dust she raise than
commendation for the clearance she effect. let it not be
imagine, however, that I consider myself competent to reform the
error and abuse of society, but only that I would fain contribute
my humble quota towards so good an aim; and if I can gain the
public ear at all, I would rather whisper a few wholesome truth
therein than much soft nonsense.
as the story of 'Agnes Grey' be accuse of extravagant over-
colour in those very part that be carefully copy from the
life, with a most scrupulous avoidance of all exaggeration, so, in
the present work, I find myself censure for depict CON AMORE,
with 'a morbid love of the coarse, if not of the brutal,' those
scene which, I will venture to say, have not be more painful for
the most fastidious of my critic to read than they be for I to
describe. I may have go too far; in which case I shall be
careful not to trouble myself or my reader in the same way again;
but when we have to do with vice and vicious character, I maintain
it be well to depict they as they really be than as they would
wish to appear. to represent a bad thing in its least offensive
light be, doubtless, the most agreeable course for a writer of
fiction to pursue; but be it the most honest, or the safe? be it
well to reveal the snare and pitfall of life to the young and
thoughtless traveller, or to cover they with branch and flower?
oh, reader! if there be less of this delicate concealment of
fact - this whispering, 'peace, peace,' when there be no peace,
there would be less of sin and misery to the young of both sex
who be leave to wring their bitter knowledge from experience.
I would not be understand to suppose that the proceeding of the
unhappy scapegrace, with his few profligate companion I have here
introduce, be a speciman of the common practice of society - the
case be an extreme one, as I trust none would fail to perceive;
but I know that such character do exist, and if I have warn one
rash youth from follow in their step, or prevent one
thoughtless girl from fall into the very natural error of my
heroine, the book have not be write in vain. but, at the same
time, if any honest reader shall have derive more pain than
pleasure from its perusal, and have close the last volume with a
disagreeable impression on his mind, I humbly crave his pardon, for
such be far from my intention; and I will endeavour to do well
another time, for I love to give innocent pleasure. yet, be it
understand, I shall not limit my ambition to this - or even to
produce 'a perfect work of art': time and talent so spend, I
should consider waste and misapply. such humble talent as God
have give I I will endeavour to put to their great use; if I be
able to amuse, I will try to benefit too; and when I feel it my
duty to speak an unpalatable truth, with the help of God, I will
speak it, though it be to the prejudice of my name and to the
detriment of my reader's immediate pleasure as well as my own.
one word more, and I have do. respect the author's identity,
I would have it to he distinctly understand that Acton Bell be
neither Currer nor Ellis Bell, and therefore let not his fault be
attribute to they. as to whether the name be real or fictitious,
it cannot greatly signify to those who know he only by his work.
as little, I should think, can it matter whether the writer so
designate be a man, or a woman, as one or two of my critic
profess to have discover. I take the imputation in good part, as
a compliment to the just delineation of my female character; and
though I be bind to attribute much of the severity of my censor
to this suspicion, I make no effort to refute it, because, in my
own mind, I be satisfied that if a book be a good one, it be so
whatever the sex of the author may be. all novel be, or should
be, write for both man and woman to read, and I be at a loss to
conceive how a man should permit himself to write anything that
would be really disgraceful to a woman, or why a woman should be
censure for write anything that would be proper and become for
a man.
JULY 22nd, 1848.
the TENANT of WILDFELL HALL
CHAPTER I
you must go back with I to the autumn of 1827.
my father, as you know, be a sort of gentleman farmer in -shire;
and I, by his express desire, succeed he in the same quiet
occupation, not very willingly, for ambition urge I to high
aim, and self-conceit assure I that, in disregard its voice,
I be bury my talent in the earth, and hide my light under a
bushel. my mother have do her utmost to persuade I that I be
capable of great achievement; but my father, who think ambition
be the sure road to ruin, and change but another word for
destruction, would listen to no scheme for better either my own
condition, or that of my fellow mortal. he assure I it be all
rubbish, and exhort I, with his die breath, to continue in the
good old way, to follow his step, and those of his father before
he, and let my high ambition be to walk honestly through the
world, look neither to the right hand nor to the left, and to
transmit the paternal acre to my child in, at least, as
flourish a condition as he leave they to I.
'well! - an honest and industrious farmer be one of the most useful
member of society; and if I devote my talent to the cultivation
of my farm, and the improvement of agriculture in general, I shall
thereby benefit, not only my own immediate connection and
dependant, but, in some degree, mankind at large:- hence I shall
not have live in vain.' with such reflection as these I be
endeavour to console myself, as I plod home from the field,
one cold, damp, cloudy evening towards the close of October. but
the gleam of a bright red fire through the parlour window have more
effect in cheer my spirit, and rebuke my thankless repining,
than all the sage reflection and good resolution I have force my
mind to frame; - for I be young then, remember - only four-and-
twenty - and have not acquire half the rule over my own spirit that
I now possess - trifling as that may be.
however, that haven of bliss must not be enter till I have
exchange my miry boot for a clean pair of shoe, and my rough
surtout for a respectable coat, and make myself generally
presentable before decent society; for my mother, with all her
kindness, be vastly particular on certain point.
in ascend to my room I be meet upon the stair by a smart,
pretty girl of nineteen, with a tidy, dumpy figure, a round face,
bright, bloom cheek, glossy, cluster curl, and little merry
brown eye. I need not tell you this be my sister Rose. she be,
I know, a comely matron still, and, doubtless, no less lovely - in
your eye - than on the happy day you first behold she. nothing
tell I then that she, a few year hence, would be the wife of one
entirely unknown to I as yet, but destine hereafter to become a
close friend than even herself, more intimate than that unmannerly
lad of seventeen, by whom I be collar in the passage, on come
down, and well-nigh jerk off my equilibrium, and who, in
correction for his impudence, receive a resounding whack over the
sconce, which, however, sustain no serious injury from the
infliction; as, besides be more than commonly thick, it be
protect by a redundant shock of short, reddish curl, that my
mother call auburn.
on enter the parlour we find that honour lady seat in her
arm-chair at the fireside, work away at her knitting, accord
to her usual custom, when she have nothing else to do. she have
sweep the hearth, and make a bright blaze fire for our reception;
the servant have just bring in the tea-tray; and Rose be
produce the sugar-basin and tea-caddy from the cupboard in the
black oak side-board, that shine like polished ebony, in the
cheerful parlour twilight.
'well! here they both be,' cry my mother, look round upon we
without retard the motion of her nimble finger and glitter
needle. 'now shut the door, and come to the fire, while Rose get
the tea ready; Ibe sure you must be starve; - and tell I what
youhave be about all day; - I like to know what my child have
be about.'
'Ihave be break in the grey colt - no easy business that -
direct the ploughing of the last wheat stubble - for the
ploughboy have not the sense to direct himself - and carry out a
plan for the extensive and efficient draining of the low
meadowland.'
'thatbe my brave boy! - and Fergus, what have you be do?'
'badger-baiting.'
and here he proceed to give a particular account of his sport,
and the respective trait of prowess evince by the badger and the
dog; my mother pretend to listen with deep attention, and
watch his animate countenance with a degree of maternal
admiration I think highly disproportione to its object.
'itbe time you should be do something else, Fergus,' say I, as
soon as a momentary pause in his narration allow I to get in a
word.
'what can I do?' reply he; 'my mother willnot let I go to sea or
enter the army; and Ibe determined to do nothing else - except make
myself such a nuisance to you all, that you will be thankful to get
rid of I on any term.'
our parent soothingly stroke his stiff, short curl. he growl,
and try to look sulky, and then we all take our seat at the
table, in obedience to the thrice-repeat summon of Rose.
'now take your tea,' say she; 'and Iwill tell you what Ihave be
do. Ihave be to call on the Wilsons; and itbe a thousand
pity you donot go with I, Gilbert, for Eliza Millward be
there!'
'well! what of she?'
'oh, nothing! - Ibe not go to tell you about she; - only that
shebe a nice, amusing little thing, when she be in a merry humour,
and I shouldnot mind call she - '
'hush, hush, my dear! your brother have no such idea!' whisper my
mother earnestly, hold up her finger.
'well,' resume Rose; 'I be go to tell you an important piece
of news I hear there - I have be burst with it ever since.
you know it be report a month ago, that somebody be go to
take Wildfell Hall - and - what do you think? it have actually be
inhabit above a week! - and we never know!'
'impossible!' cry my mother.
'preposterous!!!' shriek Fergus.
'it have indeed! - and by a single lady!'
'good gracious, my dear! the place be in ruin!'
'she have have two or three room make habitable; and there she
live, all alone - except an old woman for a servant!'
'oh, dear! that spoil it - Iwould hop she be a witch,' observe
Fergus, while carve his inch-thick slice of bread and butter.
'Nonsense, Fergus! but benot it strange, mamma?'
'strange! I can hardly believe it.'
'but you may believe it; for Jane Wilson have see she. she go
with her mother, who, of course, when she hear of a stranger being
in the neighbourhood, would be on pin and needle till she have
see she and get all she could out of she. she be call Mrs.
Graham, and she be in mourning - not widow's weed, but slightish
mourning - and she be quite young, they say, - not above five or
six and twenty, - but so reserved! they try all they could to
find out who she be and where she come from, and, all about she,
but neither Mrs. Wilson, with her pertinacious and impertinent
home-thrust, nor Miss Wilson, with her skilful manoeuvring, could
manage to elicit a single satisfactory answer, or even a casual
remark, or chance expression calculate to allay their curiosity,
or throw the faint ray of light upon her history, circumstance,
or connection. moreover, she be barely civil to they, and
evidently well pleased to say 'good-by,' than 'how do you do.'
but Eliza Millward say her father intend to call upon she soon,
to offer some pastoral advice, which he fear she need, as, though
she be know to have enter the neighbourhood early last week, she
do not make her appearance at church on Sunday; and she - Eliza,
that be - will beg to accompany he, and be sure she can succeed in
wheedle something out of she - you know, Gilbert, she can do
anything. and we should call some time, mamma; itbe only proper,
you know.'
'of course, my dear. poor thing! how lonely she must feel!'
'and pray, be quick about it; and mind you bring I word how much
sugar she put in her tea, and what sort of cap and apron she
wear, and all about it; for I donot know how I can live till I
know,' say Fergus, very gravely.
but if he intend the speech to be hail as a master-stroke of
wit, he signally fail, for nobody laugh. however, he be not
much disconcert at that; for when he have take a mouthful of
bread and butter and be about to swallow a gulp of tea, the humour
of the thing burst upon he with such irresistible force, that he
be oblige to jump up from the table, and rush snorting and
choke from the room; and a minute after, be hear scream in
fearful agony in the garden.
as for I, I be hungry, and content myself with silently
demolish the tea, ham, and toast, while my mother and sister
go on talk, and continue to discuss the apparent or non-
apparent circumstance, and probable or improbable history of the
mysterious lady; but I must confess that, after my brother's
misadventure, I once or twice raise the cup to my lip, and put it
down again without dare to taste the content, lest I should
injure my dignity by a similar explosion.
the next day my mother and Rose hasten to pay their compliment
to the fair recluse; and come back but little wise than they go;
though my mother declare she do not regret the journey, for if
she have not gain much good, she flatter herself she have
impart some, and that be well: she have give some useful
advice, which, she hop, would not be throw away; for Mrs.
Graham, though she say little to any purpose, and appear
somewhat self-opinionate, seem not incapable of reflection, -
though she do not know where she have be all her life, poor
thing, for she betray a lamentable ignorance on certain point,
and have not even the sense to be ashamed of it.
'on what point, mother?' ask I.
'on household matter, and all the little nicety of cookery, and
such thing, that every lady ought to be familiar with, whether she
be require to make a practical use of her knowledge or not. I
give she some useful piece of information, however, and several
excellent receipt, the value of which she evidently could not
appreciate, for she beg I would not trouble myself, as she live
in such a plain, quiet way, that she be sure she should never make
use of they. "no matter, my dear," say I; "it be what every
respectable female ought to know; - and besides, though you be
alone now, you will not be always so; you have be marry, and
probably - I might say almost certainly - will be again." "you be
mistaken there, ma'am," say she, almost haughtily; "I be certain I
never shall." - but I tell she I know well.'
'some romantic young widow, I suppose,' say I, 'come there to end
her day in solitude, and mourn in secret for the dear depart -
but it willnot last long.'
'no, I think not,' observed Rose; 'for she donot seem very
disconsolate after all; and shebe excessively pretty - handsome
rather - you must see she, Gilbert; you will call she a perfect
beauty, though you could hardly pretend to discover a resemblance
between she and Eliza Millward.'
'well, I can imagine many face more beautiful than Eliza's, though
not more charming. I allow she have small claim to perfection; but
then, I maintain that, if she be more perfect, she would be less
interesting.'
'and so you prefer her fault to other people's perfection?'
'just so - save my mother's presence.'
'oh, my dear Gilbert, what nonsense you talk! - I know you donot
mean it; itbe quite out of the question,' say my mother, get
up, and bustle out of the room, under pretence of household
business, in order to escape the contradiction that be tremble
on my tongue.
after that Rose favour I with further particular respect
Mrs. Graham. her appearance, manner, and dress, and the very
furniture of the room she inhabit, be all set before I, with
rather more clearness and precision than I care to see they; but,
as I be not a very attentive listener, I could not repeat the
description if I would.
the next day be Saturday; and, on Sunday, everybody wonder
whether or not the fair unknown would profit by the vicar's
remonstrance, and come to church. I confess I look with some
interest myself towards the old family pew, appertain to
Wildfell Hall, where the fade crimson cushion and lining have be
unpressed and unrenewe so many year, and the grim escutcheon,
with their lugubrious border of rusty black cloth, frown so
sternly from the wall above.
and there I behold a tall, lady-like figure, clothe in black. she
face be towards I, and there be something in it which, once
see, invite I to look again. her hair be raven black, and
dispose in long glossy ringlet, a style of coiffure rather
unusual in those day, but always graceful and become; her
complexion be clear and pale; her eye I could not see, for, be
bent upon her prayer-book, they be conceal by their drooping
lid and long black lash, but the brow above be expressive and
well define; the forehead be lofty and intellectual, the nose, a
perfect aquiline and the feature, in general, unexceptionable -
only there be a slight hollowness about the cheek and eye, and
the lip, though finely form, be a little too thin, a little
too firmly compressed, and have something about they that betoken,
I think, no very soft or amiable temper; and I say in my heart -
'I would rather admire you from this distance, fair lady, than be
the partner of your home.'
just then she happen to raise her eye, and they meet mine; I do
not choose to withdraw my gaze, and she turn again to her book,
but with a momentary, indefinable expression of quiet scorn, that
be inexpressibly provoke to I.
'she think I an impudent puppy,' think I. 'Humph! - she shall
change her mind before long, if I think it worth while.'
but then it flash upon I that these be very improper thought
for a place of worship, and that my behaviour, on the present
occasion, be anything but what it ought to be. previous, however,
to direct my mind to the service, I glance round the church to
see if any one have be observe I; - but no, - all, who be not
attend to their prayer-book, be attend to the strange
lady, - my good mother and sister among the rest, and Mrs. Wilson
and her daughter; and even Eliza Millward be slily glance from
the corner of her eye towards the object of general attraction.
then she glance at I, simper a little, and blush, modestly
look at her prayer-book, and endeavour to compose her feature.
here I be transgress again; and this time I be make sensible
of it by a sudden dig in the rib, from the elbow of my pert
brother. for the present, I could only resent the insult by
press my foot upon his toe, defer further vengeance till we
get out of church.
now, Halford, before I close this letter, Iwill tell you who Eliza
Millward be: she be the vicar's young daughter, and a very
engage little creature, for whom I feel no small degree of
partiality; - and she know it, though I have never come to any
direct explanation, and have no definite intention of so do, for
my mother, who maintain there be no one good enough for I
within twenty mile round, could not bear the thought of my
marrying that insignificant little thing, who, in addition to she
numerous other disqualification, have not twenty pound to call she
own. Eliza's figure be at once slight and plump, her face small,
and nearly as round as my sister's, - complexion, something similar
to her, but more delicate and less decidedly bloom, - nose,
retrousse, - feature, generally irregular; and, altogether, she
be rather charming than pretty. but her eye - I must not forget
those remarkable feature, for therein her chief attraction lay -
in outward aspect at least; - they be long and narrow in shape,
the irid black, or very dark brown, the expression various, and
ever change, but always either preternaturally - I have almost
say diabolically - wicked, or irresistibly bewitch - often
both. her voice be gentle and childish, her tread light and soft
as that of a cat:- but her manner more frequently resemble those
of a pretty playful kitten, that be now pert and roguish, now timid
and demure, accord to its own sweet will.
her sister, Mary, be several year old, several inch tall,
and of a large, coarser build - a plain, quiet, sensible girl, who
have patiently nurse their mother, through her last long, tedious
illness, and be the housekeeper, and family drudge, from thence
to the present time. she be trust and value by her father,
love and court by all dog, cat, child, and poor people, and
slight and neglect by everybody else.
the Reverend Michael Millward himself be a tall, ponderous elderly
gentleman, who place a shovel hat above his large, square,
massive-feature face, carry a stout walk-stick in his hand,
and incase his still powerful limb in knee-breech and gaiter,
- or black silk stocking on state occasion. he be a man of
fix principle, strong prejudice, and regular habit, intolerant
of dissent in any shape, act under a firm conviction that his
opinion be always right, and whoever differ from they must be
either most deplorably ignorant, or wilfully blind.
in childhood, I have always be accustom to regard he with a
feeling of reverential awe - but lately, even now, surmount, for,
though he have a fatherly kindness for the well-behave, he be a
strict disciplinarian, and have often sternly reprove our juvenile
failing and peccadillo; and moreover, in those day, whenever he
call upon our parent, we have to stand up before he, and say our
catechism, or repeat, 'how doth the little busy bee,' or some other
hymn, or - bad than all - be question about his last text, and
the head of the discourse, which we never could remember.
sometimes, the worthy gentleman would reprove my mother for be
over-indulgent to her son, with a reference to old Eli, or David
and Absalom, which be particularly gall to her feeling; and,
very highly as she respect he, and all his saying, I once hear
her exclaim, 'I wish to goodness he have a son himself! he wouldnot
be so ready with his advice to other people then; - hewould see what
it be to have a couple of boy to keep in order.'
he have a laudable care for his own bodily health - keep very early
hour, regularly take a walk before breakfast, be vastly
particular about warm and dry clothing, have never be know to
preach a sermon without previously swallow a raw egg - albeit he
be gift with good lung and a powerful voice, - and be,
generally, extremely particular about what he eat and drank, though
by no mean abstemious, and have a mode of dietary peculiar to
himself, - be a great despiser of tea and such slop, and a
patron of malt liquor, bacon and egg, ham, hung beef, and other
strong meat, which agree well enough with his digestive organ,
and therefore be maintain by he to be good and wholesome for
everybody, and confidently recommend to the most delicate
convalescent or dyspeptic, who, if they fail to derive the
promise benefit from his prescription, be tell it be because
they have not persevere, and if they complain of inconvenient
result therefrom, be assure it be all fancy.
I will just touch upon two other person whom I have mention, and
then bring this long letter to a close. these be Mrs. Wilson and
her daughter. the former be the widow of a substantial farmer, a
narrow-minded, tattle old gossip, whose character be not worth
describing. she have two son, Robert, a rough countrifie farmer,
and Richard, a retire, studious young man, who be study the
classic with the vicar's assistance, prepare for college, with a
view to enter the church.
their sister Jane be a young lady of some talent, and more
ambition. she have, at her own desire, receive a regular boarding-
school education, superior to what any member of the family have
obtain before. she have take the polish well, acquire
considerable elegance of manner, quite lose her provincial accent,
and could boast of more accomplishment than the vicar's daughter.
she be consider a beauty besides; but never for a moment could
she number I amongst her admirer. she be about six and twenty,
rather tall and very slender, her hair be neither chestnut nor
auburn, but a most decide bright, light red; her complexion be
remarkably fair and brilliant, her head small, neck long, chin well
turn, but very short, lip thin and red, eye clear hazel, quick,
and penetrate, but entirely destitute of poetry or feeling. she
have, or might have have, many suitor in her own rank of life, but
scornfully repulse or reject they all; for none but a gentleman
could please her refined taste, and none but a rich one could
satisfy her soar ambition. one gentleman there be, from whom
she have lately receive some rather pointed attention, and upon
whose heart, name, and fortune, it be whisper, she have serious
design. this be Mr. Lawrence, the young squire, whose family have
formerly occupy Wildfell Hall, but have desert it, some fifteen
year ago, for a more modern and commodious mansion in the
neighbouring parish.
now, Halford, I bid you adieu for the present. this be the first
instalment of my debt. if the coin suit you, tell I so, and Iwill
send you the rest at my leisure: if you would rather remain my
creditor than stuff your purse with such ungainly, heavy piece, -
tell I still, and Iwill pardon your bad taste, and willingly keep
the treasure to myself.
your immutably,
gilbert MARKHAM.
CHAPTER II
I perceive, with joy, my most value friend, that the cloud of your
displeasure have pass away; the light of your countenance bless
I once more, and you desire the continuation of my story:
therefore, without more ado, you shall have it.
I think the day I last mention be a certain Sunday, the late
in the October of 1827. on the following Tuesday I be out with my
dog and gun, in pursuit of such game as I could find within the
territory of Linden-Car; but find none at all, I turn my arm
against the hawk and carrion crow, whose depredation, as I
suspect, have deprive I of well prey. to this end I leave the
more frequented region, the woode valley, the corn-field, and
the meadow-land, and proceed to mount the steep acclivity of
Wildfell, the wild and the lofty eminence in our
neighbourhood, where, as you ascend, the hedge, as well as the
tree, become scanty and stunt, the former, at length, give
place to rough stone fence, partly green over with ivy and moss,
the latter to larch and Scotch fir-tree, or isolate
blackthorn. the field, be rough and stony, and wholly unfit
for the plough, be mostly devoted to the posturing of sheep and
cattle; the soil be thin and poor: bit of grey rock here and
there peep out from the grassy hillock; bilberry-plant and
heather - relic of more savage wildness - grow under the wall;
and in many of the enclosure, ragweed and rush usurped
supremacy over the scanty herbage; but these be not my property.
near the top of this hill, about two mile from Linden-Car, stand
Wildfell Hall, a superannuated mansion of the Elizabethan era,
build of dark grey stone, venerable and picturesque to look at, but
doubtless, cold and gloomy enough to inhabit, with its thick stone
mullion and little latticed pane, its time-eat air-hole, and
its too lonely, too unsheltered situation, - only shield from the
war of wind and weather by a group of Scotch fir, themselves half
blight with storm, and look as stern and gloomy as the Hall
itself. behind it lie a few desolate field, and then the brown
heath-clothe summit of the hill; before it (enclose by stone wall,
and enter by an iron gate, with large ball of grey granite -
similar to those which decorate the roof and gable - surmount
the gate-post) be a garden, - once stock with such hard plant
and flower as could well brook the soil and climate, and such
tree and shrub as could well endure the gardener's torture
shear, and most readily assume the shape he choose to give they, -
now, have be leave so many year untilled and untrimmed,
abandon to the weed and the grass, to the frost and the wind,
the rain and the drought, it present a very singular appearance
indeed. the close green wall of privet, that have border the
principal walk, be two-third wither away, and the rest grow
beyond all reasonable bound; the old boxwood swan, that sit beside
the scraper, have lose its neck and half its body: the castellate
tower of laurel in the middle of the garden, the gigantic warrior
that stand on one side of the gateway, and the lion that guard

the eye do not notice I, but sparkle with glee on behold

CHAPTER III
two day after, Mrs. Graham call at Linden-Car, contrary to the

CHAPTER IV

CHAPTER V
it be about the close of the month, that, yield at length to

CHAPTER VI
during the next four month I do not enter Mrs. Graham's house,

'then I think youwould well let they alone.'

be it so, Halford? be that the extent of your domestic virtue;
and do your happy wife exact no more?
CHAPTER VII

CHAPTER XIII
'my dear Gilbert, I wish you would try to be a little more

CHAPTER XIV
next morning, I bethink I, I, too, have business at L-; so I

CHAPTER XVI
June 1st, 1821. - we have just return to Staningley - that be, we
return some day ago, and I be not yet settle, and feel as if I
never should be. we leave town soon than be intend, in

- '

CHAPTER XVII
the next day I accompany my uncle and aunt to a dinner-party at
Mr. Wilmot's. he have two lady stay with he: his niece
Annabella, a fine dash girl, or rather young woman, - of some
five-and-twenty, too great a flirt to be marry, accord to her
own assertion, but greatly admire by the gentleman, who
universally pronounce she a splendid woman; and her gentle cousin,

very fond of she. I should entirely exclude poor Milicent in my

he do not sit near I at dinner; for it be his fate to hand in a

with. what a tiresome custom that be, by-the-by - one among the
many source of factitious annoyance of this ultra-civilised life.
if the gentleman must lead the lady into the dining-room, why
cannot they take those they like good?
I be not sure, however, that Mr. Huntingdon would have take I, if

'no.'
'what be he say then? I hear something very like it.'
'I donot know what he would have say, if you havenot interrupt

'of course not - without consult uncle and you.'

CHAPTER xviii
August 25th. - I be now quite settle down to my usual routine of
steady occupation and quiet amusement - tolerably contented and
cheerful, but still look forward to spring with the hope of
return to town, not for its gaiety and dissipation, but for
the chance of meet Mr. Huntingdon once again; for still he be
always in my thought and in my dream. in all my employment,
whatever I do, or see, or hear, have an ultimate reference to he;
whatever skill or knowledge I acquire be some day to be turn to
his advantage or amusement; whatever new beauty in nature or art
I discover be to be depict to meet his eye, or store in my
memory to be tell he at some future period. this, at least, be
the hope that I cherish, the fancy that light I on my lonely way.
it may be only an ignis fatuus, after all, but it can do no harm to
follow it with my eye and rejoice in its lustre, as long as it
do not lure I from the path I ought to keep; and I think it will
not, for I have think deeply on my aunt's advice, and I see
clearly, now, the folly of throw myself away on one that be
unworthy of all the love I have to give, and incapable of
respond to the good and deep feeling of my inmost heart - so
clearly, that even if I should see he again, and if he should
remember I and love I still (which, alas! be too little probable,
consider how he be situate, and by whom surround), and if he
should ask I to marry he - I be determined not to consent until I
know for certain whether my aunt's opinion of he or mine be
near the truth; for if mine be altogether wrong, it be not he
that I love; it be a creature of my own imagination. but I think
it be not wrong - no, no - there be a secret something - an inward
instinct that assure I I be right. there be essential goodness
in he; - and what delight to unfold it! if he have wander, what
bliss to recall he! if he be now expose to the baneful influence
of corrupting and wicked companion, what glory to deliver he from
they! oh! if I could but believe that Heaven have design I for
this!

to-day be the first of September; but my uncle have order the

necessity - never glance towards I but with a cold, unfriendly

my aunt observe the change, and though she have not inquire the

pleasure. Miss Wilmot observe it, too, and triumphantly ascribe
it to her own superior charm and blandishment; but I be truly

refuse to aid I. it have bring I into the scrape, and will not
help I out of it.
he mean no harm - it be only his joyous, playful spirit; and I,

think I dislike he, and he must continue to think so. I must
lose he for ever, and Annabella may win he, and triumph as she

but it be not my loss nor her triumph that I deplore so greatly as

happiness to she. she do not love he: she think only of

aggravate they by her own. and I doubt whether she will not

commoner will have but little chance against the lordly peer. if

rather add new zest to his diversion by oppose a stimulate

Messrs. Wilmot and Boarham have severally take occasion by his

and some other I should take advantage of their perseverance to

even if I do it would have precious little effect upon he. he

without so much as a shadow of commiseration for I, or resentment

flirt with Annabella Wilmot - as if nothing be on his mind.
oh! why cannot I hate he? I must be infatuate, or I should scorn
to regret he as I do. but I must rally all the power I have
remain, and try to tear he from my heart. there go the
dinner-bell, and here come my aunt to scold I for sit here at
my desk all day, instead of stay with the company: wish the
company be - go.
CHAPTER XIX
twenty Second: Night. - what have I do? and what will be the end
of it? I cannot calmly reflect upon it; I cannot sleep. I must
have recourse to my diary again; I will commit it to paper to-
night, and see what I shall think of it to-morrow.
I go down to dinner resolve to be cheerful and well-conduct,

and so be the words:-
Farewell to thee! but not farewell
to all my fond thought of thee:
within my heart they still shall dwell;
and they shall cheer and comfort I.
o beautiful, and full of grace!
if thou hadst never meet mine eye,
I have not dream a live face
could fancy charm so far outvie.
if I may ne'er behold again
that form and face so dear to I,
nor hear thy voice, still would I fain
Preserve, for aye, their memory.
that voice, the magic of whose tone
can wake an echo in my breast,
create feeling that, alone,
can make my trance spirit blest.
that laugh eye, whose sunny beam
my memory would not cherish less; -

no mortal languish can express.
Adieu! but let I cherish, still,
the hope with which I cannot part.
Contempt may wound, and coldness chill,
but still it linger in my heart.
and who can tell but Heaven, at last,
may answer all my thousand prayer,
and bid the future pay the past
with joy for anguish, smile for tear.
when it cease, I long for nothing so much as to be out of the

CHAPTER XX
September 24th. - in the morning I rise, light and cheerful - nay,
intensely happy. the hover cloud cast over I by my aunt's

blissful thought. the dew be on the grass, and ten thousand

silent hymn of gratitude and praise to heaven.
but I have not wander far before my solitude be interrupt by

I havenot have time, yet, to examine thoroughly into this young

property have be squander away; - but still, I think, therebe a
pretty fair share of it leave, and a little careful nursing may make
a handsome thing of it yet; and then we must persuade your father
to give you a decent fortune, as he have only one besides yourself
to care for; - and, if you behave well, who know but what I may be
induce to remember you in my will!' continue he, put his
finger to his nose, with a knowing wink.
'thank, uncle, for that and all your kindness,' reply I.
'well, and I question this young spark on the matter of
settlement,' continue he; 'and he seem disposed to be generous
enough on that point - '
'I know he would!' say I. 'but pray donot trouble your head - or
his, or mine about that; for all I have will be his, and all he have
will be mine; and what more could either of we require?' and I be
about to make my exit, but he call I back.
'stop, stop!' cry he; 'we havenot mention the time yet. when
must it be? your aunt would put it off till the Lord know when,
but he be anxious to be bind as soon as may be: he willnot hear of
wait beyond next month; and you, I guess, will be of the same
mind, so - '
'not at all, uncle; on the contrary, I should like to wait till
after Christmas, at least.'
'oh! pooh, pooh! never tell I that tale - I know well,' cry
he; and he persist in his incredulity. nevertheless, it be quite
true. I be in no hurry at all. how can I be, when I think of the
momentous change that await I, and of all I have to leave? it be
happiness enough to know that we be to be unite; and that he
really love I, and I may love he as devotedly, and think of he
as often as I please. however, I insist upon consult my aunt
about the time of the wedding, for I determine her counsel should
not be utterly disregard; and no conclusion on that particular
be come to yet.
CHAPTER XXI
October 1st. - all be settle now. my father have give his
consent, and the time be fix for Christmas, by a sort of
compromise between the respective advocate for hurry and delay.
Milicent Hargrave be to be one bridesmaid and Annabella Wilmot the
other - not that I be particularly fond of the latter, but she be
an intimate of the family, and I have not another friend.
when I tell Milicent of my engagement, she rather provoke I by
her manner of talk it. after star a moment in mute surprise,
she say, - 'well, Helen, I suppose I ought to congratulate you -
and I be glad to see you so happy; but I do not think you would
take he; and I cannot help feel surprised that you should like
he so much.'
'why so?'
'because you be so superior to he in every way, and therebe
something so bold and reckless about he - so, I donot know how -
but I always feel a wish to get out of his way when I see he
approach.'
'you be timid, Milicent; but thatbe no fault of his.'
'and then his look,' continue she. 'People say hebe handsome, and
of course he be; but I donot like that kind of beauty, and I wonder
that you should.'
'why so, pray?'
'well, you know, I think therebe nothing noble or lofty in his
appearance.'
'in fact, you wonder that I can like any one so unlike the stilte
hero of romance. well, give I my flesh and blood lover, and
Iwill leave all the Sir Herberts and Valentines to you - if you can
find they.'
'I donot want they,' say she. 'Iwill be satisfied with flesh and
blood too - only the spirit must shine through and predominate.
but donot you think Mr. Huntingdon's face be too red?'
'no!' cry I, indignantly. 'it be not red at all. there be just
a pleasant glow, a healthy freshness in his complexion - the warm,
pinky tint of the whole harmonising with the deep colour of the
cheek, exactly as it ought to do. I hate a man to be red and
white, like a paint doll, or all sickly white, or smoky black, or
cadaverous yellow.'
'well, taste differ - but I like pale or dark,' reply she.
'but, to tell you the truth, Helen, I have be delude myself with
the hope that you would one day be my sister. I expect Walter
would be introduce to you next season; and I think you would
like he, and be certain he would like you; and I flatter myself
I should thus have the felicity of see the two person I like
well in the world - except mamma - united in one. he maynot be
exactly what you would call handsome, but hebe far more
distinguish-looking, and nicer and well than Mr. Huntingdon; -
and Ibe sure you would say so, if you know he.'
'impossible, Milicent! you think so, because yoube his sister;
and, on that account, Iwill forgive you; but nobody else should so
disparage Arthur Huntingdon to I with impunity.'
Miss Wilmot express her feeling on the subject almost as openly.
'and so, Helen,' say she, come up to I with a smile of no
amiable import, 'you be to be Mrs. Huntingdon, I suppose?'
'yes,' reply I. 'donot you envy I?'
'oh, dear, no!' she exclaim. 'I shall probably be Lady
Lowborough some day, and then you know, dear, I shall be in a
capacity to inquire, "donot you envy I?"'
'henceforth I shall envy no one,' return I.
'indeed! be you so happy then?' say she, thoughtfully; and
something very like a cloud of disappointment shadow her face.
'and do he love you - I mean, do he idolise you as much as you
do he?' she add, fix her eye upon I with ill-disguise
anxiety for the reply.
'I donot want to be idolise,' I answer; 'but I be well assure
that he love I more than anybody else in the world - as I do
he.'
'exactly,' say she, with a nod. 'I wish - ' she pause.
'what do you wish?' ask I, annoy at the vindictive expression
of her countenance.
'I wish,' return, she, with a short laugh, 'that all the
attractive point and desirable qualification of the two gentleman
be unite in one - that Lord Lowborough have Huntingdon's handsome
face and good temper, and all his wit, and mirth and charm, or else
that Huntingdon have Lowborough's pedigree, and title, and
delightful old family seat, and I have he; and you might have the
other and welcome.'
'thank you, dear Annabella: I be well satisfied with thing as
they be, for my own part; and for you, I wish you be as well

true enough; for, though vex at first at her unamiable spirit,
her frankness touch I, and the contrast between our situation

Mr. Huntingdon's acquaintance appear to be no well pleased with

letter from several of his friend, during the perusal of which,
at the breakfast-table, he excite the attention of the company by
the singular variety of his grimace. but he crush they all into
his pocket, with a private laugh, and say nothing till the meal
be conclude. then, while the company be hang over the fire

various morning avocation, he come and leant over the back of my
chair, with his face in contact with my curl, and commence with
a quiet little kiss, pour forth the follow complaint into my

'Helen, you witch, do you know that youhave entail upon I the
curse of all my friend? I write to they the other day, to tell
they of my happy prospect, and now, instead of a bundle of
congratulation, Ihave get a pocketful of bitter execration and
reproach. therebe not one kind wish for I, or one good word for
you, among they all. they say therewill be no more fun now, no more
merry day and glorious night - and all my fault - I be the first
to break up the jovial band, and other, in pure despair, will
follow my example. I be the very life and prop of the community,
they do I the honour to say, and I have shamefully betray my
trust - '
'you may join they again, if you like,' say I, somewhat pique at
the sorrowful tone of his discourse. 'I should be sorry to stand
between any man - or body of man, and so much happiness; and
perhaps I can manage to do without you, as well as your poor
desert friend.'
'bless you, no,' murmur he. 'itbe "all for love or the world
well lose," with I. let they go to - where they belong, to speak
politely. but if you see how they abuse I, Helen, you would love
I all the more for have venture so much for your sake.'
he pull out his crumpled letter. I think he be go to show
they to I, and tell he I do not wish to see they.
'Ibe not go to show they to you, love,' say he. 'theybe
hardly fit for a lady's eye - the most part of they. but look
here. this be Grimsby's scrawl - only three line, the sulky dog!
he donot say much, to be sure, but his very silence imply more
than all the other' word, and the less he say, the more he
think - and this be Hargrave's missive. he be particularly
grieve at I, because, forsooth he have fall in love with you
from his sister's report, and mean to have marry you himself,
as soon as he have sow his wild oat.'
'Ibe vastly oblige to he,' observed I.
'and so be I,' say he. 'and look at this. this be Hattersley's -
every page stuff full of rail accusation, bitter curse, and
lamentable complaint, end up with swear that hewill get
marry himself in revenge: hewill throw himself away on the first
old maid that choose to set her cap at he, - as if I care what
he do with himself.'
'well,' say I, 'if you do give up your intimacy with these man, I
donot think you will have much cause to regret the loss of their
society; for itbe my belief they never do you much good.'
'maybe not; but wehave a merry time of it, too, though mingle with
sorrow and pain, as Lowborough know to his cost - ha, ha!' and
while he be laugh at the recollection of Lowborough's trouble,
my uncle come and slap he on the shoulder.
'come, my lad!' say he. 'be you too busy make love to my niece
to make war with the pheasant? - First of October, remember! Sun
shin out - rain cease - even Boarham's not afraid to venture in
his waterproof boot; and Wilmot and I be go to beat you all.
I declare, we old 'uns be the keen sportsman of the lot!'
'Iwill show you what I can do to-day, however,' say my companion.
'Iwill murder your bird by wholesale, just for keep I away from
well company than either you or they.'
and so say he depart; and I see no more of he till dinner.
it seem a weary time; I wonder what I shall do without he.
it be very true that the three elder gentleman have prove
themselves much keener sportsman than the two young one; for
both Lord Lowborough and Arthur Huntingdon have of late almost
daily neglect the shooting excursion to accompany we in our
various ride and ramble. but these merry time be fast drawing
to a close. in less than a fortnight the party break up, much to
my sorrow, for every day I enjoy it more and more - now that
Messrs. Boarham and Wilmot have cease to tease I, and my aunt have
cease to lecture I, and I have cease to be jealous of Annabella
- and even to dislike she - and now that Mr. Huntingdon be become
my Arthur, and I may enjoy his society without restraint. what
shall I do without he, I repeat?
CHAPTER XXII
October 5th. - my cup of sweet be not unmingled: it be dash
with a bitterness that I cannot hide from myself, disguise it as I
will. I may try to persuade myself that the sweetness overpower
it; I may call it a pleasant aromatic flavour; but say what I will,
it be still there, and I cannot but taste it. I cannot shut my

trouble I. his very heart, that I trust so, be, I fear, less
warm and generous than I think it. at least, he give I a
speciman of his character to-day that seem to merit a hard name

Annabella and I in a long, delightful ride; he be rid by my

and confidential discourse.
'those two will get the start of we, Helen, if we donot look

what Ihave hear of he be true.'
'not a bit of it. she know what shebe about; but he, poor fool,
delude himself with the notion that shewill make he a good wife,
and because she have amuse he with some rodomontade about
despise rank and wealth in matter of love and marriage, he

will not refuse he for his poverty, and do not court he for his
rank, but love he for himself alone.'
'but be not he court she for her fortune?'
'no, not he. that be the first attraction, certainly; but now he
have quite lose sight of it: it never enter his calculation,

sake, he could not think of marry she. no; hebe fairly in love.
he think he never could be again, but hebe in for it once more.
he be to have be marry before, some two or three year ago;
but he lose his bride by lose his fortune. he get into a bad way
among we in London: he have an unfortunate taste for gambling; and
surely the fellow be bear under an unlucky star, for he always
lose thrice where he gain once. thatbe a mode of self-torment I
never be much addicted to. when I spend my money I like to enjoy
the full value of it: I see no fun in waste it on thief and
blackleg; and as for gain money, hitherto I have always have
sufficient; itbe time enough to be clutch for more, I think,
when you begin to see the end of what you have. but I have
sometimes frequent the gaming-house just to watch the on-going

assure you, Helen, and sometimes very divert: Ihave have many a

every venture be the 'just once more:' if he gain a little, he
hop to gain a little more next time, and if he lose, it would not
do to leave off at that juncture; he must go on till he have
retrieve that last misfortune, at least: bad luck could not last
for ever; and every lucky hit be look upon as the dawn of well

SE - no great matter, some of we whisper, as his existence have
cease to be an acquisition to our club. at last, however, he come

the last, whether he lose or win. he have often so determined

it be this time. he lose; and while his antagonist smilingly
sweep away the stake, he turn chalky white, draw back in
silence, and wipe his forehead. I be present at the time; and

know well enough what be pass in his mind.
'"be it to be the last, Lowborough?" say I, step up to he.
'"the last but one," he answer, with a grim smile; and then,
rush back to the table, he strike his hand upon it, and, raise

imprecate unspeakable curse on his head if ever he should shuffle
a card or rattle a dice-box again. he then double his former
stake, and challenge any one present to play against he. Grimsby
instantly present himself. Lowborough glare fiercely at he,
for Grimsby be almost as celebrate for his luck as he be for his
ill-fortune. however, they fall to work. but Grimsby have much
skill and little scruple, and whether he take advantage of the
other's trembling, blind eagerness to deal unfairly by he, I
cannot undertake to say; but Lowborough lose again, and fall dead
sick.
'"youwould well try once more," say Grimsby, lean across the
table. and then he wink at I.
'"Ihave nothing to try with," say the poor devil, with a ghastly
smile.
'"oh, Huntingdon will lend you what you want," say the other.
'"no; you hear my oath," answer Lowborough, turn away in
quiet despair. and I take he by the arm and lead he out.
'"be it to be the last, Lowborough?" I ask, when I get he into
the street.
'"the last," he answer, somewhat against my expectation. and I

rather bright - rather more alive, at least.
'"Huntingdon, Ibe ruin!" say he, take the third glass from my
hand - he have drink the other in dead silence.

as merrily as a tortoise without its head, or a wasp without its
body.
'"but Ibe in debt," say he - "deep in debt. and I can never,
never get out of it."
'"well, what of that? many a well man than you have live and
die in debt; and they cannot put you in prison, you know, because
yoube a peer." and I hand he his fourth tumbler.
'"but I hate to be in debt!" he shout. "I benot bear for it,
and I cannot bear it."
'"what cannot be cure must be endure," say I, begin to mix

'"and then, Ihave lose my Caroline." and he begin to snivel then,
for the brandy have soften his heart.

Iwould never stop here."
'"I willnot, I tell you!" shout he. and he start up, and leave

'he keep his oath about gambling (not a little to the surprise of

of the loss of his sweetheart, the loss of his fortune, and the
reaction of the lose night's debauch; whereas, when he have

'"a speech, a speech!" shout we. "hear, hear! Lowborough's
go to give we a speech!"
'he wait calmly till the thunder of applause and jingling of

gentleman, - that I think wewould well go no far. wewould well
stop while we can."
'"just so!" cry Hattersley -
"stop, poor sinner, stop and think
before you far go,
no long sport upon the brink
of everlaste woe."
'"exactly!" reply his lordship, with the utmost gravity. "and if
you choose to visit the bottomless pit, I willnot go with you - we

'"this be hell broth!" he exclaim. "I renounce it for ever!"

'"itbe rank poison," say he, grasp the bottle by the neck, "and

he be on the point of deliberately pour the whole content of

'we expect he back among we the next day; but, to our surprise,
