he be a model of moderation and prudence - something too much so
for the taste of our wild community; but, somehow, Lowborough have
not the gift of moderation: if he stumble a little to one side,
he must go down before he could right himself: if he overshot the
mark one night, the effect of it render he so miserable the
next day that he must repeat the offence to mend it; and so on from
day to day, till his clamorous conscience bring he to a stand.
and then, in his sober moment, he so bother his friend with his
remorse, and his terror and woe, that they be oblige, in self-
defence, to get he to drown his sorrow in wine, or any more
potent beverage that come to hand; and when his first scruple of
conscience be overcome, he would need no more persuading, he
would often grow desperate, and be as great a blackguard as any of
they could desire - but only to lament his own unutterable
wickedness and degradation the more when the fit be over.
'at last, one day when he and I be alone together, after
ponder awhile in one of his gloomy, abstract mood, with his
arm fold and his head sink on his breast, he suddenly wake up,
and vehemently grasp my arm, say, -
'"Huntingdon, this willnot do! Ibe resolve to have do with it."
'"what, be you go to shoot yourself?" say I.
'"no; Ibe go to reform."
'"oh, thatbe nothing new! youhave be go to reform these twelve
month and more."
'"yes, but you wouldnot let I; and I be such a fool I couldnot
live without you. but now I see what it be that keep I back, and
whatbe want to save I; and Iwould compass sea and land to get it -
only Ibe afraid therebe no chance." and he sigh as if his heart
would break.
'"what be it, Lowborough?" say I, think he be fairly crack
at last.
'"a wife," he answer; "for I cannot live alone, because my own
mind distract I, and I cannot live with you, because you take the
devil's part against I."
'"who - I?"
'"yes - all of you do - and you more than any of they, you know.
but if I could get a wife, with fortune enough to pay off my debt
and set I straight in the world - "
'"to be sure," say I.
'"and sweetness and goodness enough," he continue, "to make home
tolerable, and to reconcile I to myself, I think I should do yet.
I shall never be in love again, thatbe certain; but perhaps that
would be no great matter, it would enable I to choose with my eye
open - and I should make a good husband in spite of it; but could
any one be in love with I? - thatbe the question. with your good
look and power of fascination" (he be pleased to say), "I might
hope; but as it be, Huntingdon, do you think anybody would take I
- ruin and wretche as I be?"
'"yes, certainly."
'"who?"
'"why, any neglect old maid, fast sinking in despair, would be
delighted to - "
'"no, no," say he - "it must be somebody that I can love."
'"why, you just say you never could be in love again!'
'"well, love be not the word - but somebody that I can like. Iwill
search all England through, at all event!" he cry, with a sudden
burst of hope, or desperation. "succeed or fail, it will be well
than rush headlong to destruction at that d-d club: so farewell
to it and you. whenever I meet you on honest ground or under a
christian roof, I shall be glad to see you; but never more shall
you entice I to that devil's den!"
'this be shameful language, but I shake hand with he, and we
part. he keep his word; and from that time forward he have be a
pattern of propriety, as far as I can tell; but till lately I have
not have very much to do with he. he occasionally seek my
company, but as frequently shrink from it, fear lest I should
wile he back to destruction, and I find his not very
entertaining, especially as he sometimes attempt to awaken my
conscience and draw I from the perdition he consider himself to
have escape; but when I do happen to meet he, I seldom fail to
ask after the progress of his matrimonial effort and research,
and, in general, he could give I but a poor account. the mother
be repel by his empty coffer and his reputation for gambling,
and the daughter by his cloudy brow and melancholy temper -
besides, he donot understand they; he want the spirit and
assurance to carry his point.
'I leave he at it when I go to the continent; and on my return,
at the year's end, I find he still a disconsolate bachelor -
though, certainly, look somewhat less like an unbl exile from
the tomb than before. the young lady have cease to be afraid of
he, and be begin to think he quite interesting; but the
mamma be still unrelenting. it be about this time, Helen, that
my good angel bring I into conjunction with you; and then I have
eye and ear for nobody else. but, meantime, Lowborough become
acquainted with our charming friend, Miss Wilmot - through the
intervention of his good angel, no doubt he would tell you, though
he do not dare to fix his hope on one so court and admire,
till after they be bring into close contact here at
Staningley, and she, in the absence of her other admirer,
indubitably court his notice and hold out every encouragement to
his timid advance. then, indeed, he begin to hope for a dawn of
bright day; and if, for a while, I darken his prospect by
stand between he and his sun - and so nearly plunge he again
into the abyss of despair - it only intensify his ardour and
strengthen his hope when I choose to abandon the field in the
pursuit of a bright treasure. in a word, as I tell you, he be
fairly besotte. at first, he could dimly perceive her fault, and
they give he considerable uneasiness; but now his passion and her
art together have blind he to everything but her perfection and
his amazing good fortune. last night he come to I brimful of his
new-find felicity:
'"Huntingdon, I be not a castaway!" say he, seize my hand and
squeeze it like a vice. "there be happiness in store for I yet
- even in this life - she love I!"
'"indeed!" say I. "have she tell you so?"
'"no, but I can no long doubt it. do you not see how pointedly
kind and affectionate she be? and she know the utmost extent of
my poverty, and care nothing about it! she know all the folly
and all the wickedness of my former life, and be not afraid to
trust I - and my rank and title be no allurement to she; for
they she utterly disregard. she be the most generous, high-minded
being that can be conceive of. she will save I, body and soul,
from destruction. already, she have ennoble I in my own
estimation, and make I three time well, wiser, great than I
be. oh! if I have but know she before, how much degradation and
misery I should have be spar! but what have I do to deserve
so magnificent a creature?"
'and the cream of the jest,' continue Mr. Huntingdon, laughing,
'be, that the artful minx love nothing about he but his title and
pedigree, and "that delightful old family seat."'
'how do you know?' say I.
'she tell I so herself; she say, "as for the man himself, I
thoroughly despise he; but then, I suppose, it be time to be
make my choice, and if I wait for some one capable of elicit
my esteem and affection, I should have to pass my life in single
blessedness, for I det you all!" ha, ha! I suspect she be
wrong there; but, however, it be evident she have no love for he,
poor fellow.'
'then you ought to tell he so.'
'what! and spoil all her plan and prospect, poor girl? no, no:
that would be a breach of confidence, wouldnot it, Helen? ha, ha!
besides, it would break his heart.' and he laugh again.
'well, Mr. Huntingdon, I donot know what you see so amazingly
divert in the matter; I see nothing to laugh at.'
'Ibe laugh at you, just now, love,' say he, redouble his
machination.
and leave he to enjoy his merriment alone, I touch Ruby with
the whip, and canter on to rejoin our companion; for we have be
walk our horse all this time, and be consequently a long way
behind. Arthur be soon at my side again; but not dispose to talk
to he, I break into a gallop. he do the same; and we do not
slacken our pace till we come up with Miss Wilmot and Lord
Lowborough, which be within half a mile of the park-gates. I
avoid all further conversation with he till we come to the end
of our ride, when I mean to jump off my horse and vanish into the
house, before he could offer his assistance; but while I be
disengage my habit from the crutch, he lift I off, and hold I
by both hand, assert that he would not let I go till I have
forgive he.
'I have nothing to forgive,' say I. 'you have not injure I.'
'no, darling - God forbid that I should! but you be angry because
it be to I that Annabella confess her lack of esteem for her
lover.'
'no, Arthur, it be not that that displease I: it be the whole
system of your conduct towards your friend, and if you wish I to
forget it, go now, and tell he what sort of a woman it be that he
adore so madly, and on whom he have hang his hope of future
happiness.'
'I tell you, Helen, it would break his heart - it would be the
death of he - besides be a scandalous trick to poor Annabella.
there be no help for he now; he be past pray for. besides, she
may keep up the deception to the end of the chapter; and then he
will be just as happy in the illusion as if it be reality; or
perhaps he will only discover his mistake when he have cease to
love she; and if not, it be much well that the truth should dawn
gradually upon he. so now, my angel, I hope I have make out a
clear case, and fully convince you that I cannot make the
atonement you require. what other requisition have you to make?
speak, and I will gladly obey.'
'I have none but this,' say I, as gravely as before: 'that, in
future, you will never make a jest of the suffering of other, and
always use your influence with your friend for their own advantage
against their evil propensity, instead of second their evil
propensity against themselves.'
'I will do my utmost,' say he, 'to remember and perform the
injunction of my angel monitress;' and after kiss both my
gloved hand, he let I go.
when I enter my room, I be surprised to see Annabella Wilmot
stand before my toilet-table, composedly survey her feature
in the glass, with one hand flirt her gold-mount whip, and the
other hold up her long habit.
'she certainly be a magnificent creature!' think I, as I behold
that tall, finely develop figure, and the reflection of the
handsome face in the mirror before I, with the glossy dark hair,
slightly and not ungracefully disorder by the breezy ride, the
rich brown complexion glow with exercise, and the black eye
sparkle with unwonted brilliance. on perceive I, she turn
round, exclaim, with a laugh that savour more of malice than
of mirth, - 'why, Helen! what have you be do so long? I come
to tell you my good fortune,' she continue, regardless of Rachel's
presence. 'Lord Lowborough have propose, and I have be
graciously pleased to accept he. donot you envy I, dear?'
'no, love,' say I - 'or he either,' I mentally add. 'and do
you like he, Annabella?'
'like he! yes, to be sure - over head and ear in love!'
'well, I hope youwill make he a good wife.'
'thank you, my dear! and what besides do you hope?'
'I hope you will both love each other, and both be happy.'
'thank; and I hope you will make a very good wife to Mr.
Huntingdon!' say she, with a queenly bow, and retire.
'oh, Miss! how could you say so to she!' cry Rachel.
'say what?' reply I.
'why, that you hop she would make he a good wife. I never hear
such a thing!'
'because I do hope it, or rather, I wish it; shebe almost past
hope.'
'well,' say she, 'Ibe sure I hope hewill make she a good husband.
they tell queer thing about he downstairs. they be say - '
'I know, Rachel. Ihave hear all about he; but hebe reform now.
and they have no business to tell tale about their master.'
'no, mum - or else, they have say some thing about Mr. Huntingdon
too.'
'I willnot hear they, Rachel; they tell lie.'
'yes, mum,' say she, quietly, as she go on arrange my hair.
'do you believe they, Rachel?' I ask, after a short pause.
'no, Miss, not all. you know when a lot of servant get together
they like to talk about their better; and some, for a bit of
swagger, like to make it appear as though they know more than they
do, and to throw out hint and thing just to astonish the other.
but I think, if I be you, Miss Helen, Iwould look very well before I
leap. I do believe a young lady cannot be too careful who she
marry.'
'of course not,' say I; 'but be quick, will you, Rachel? I want
to be dress.'
and, indeed, I be anxious to be rid of the good woman, for I be
in such a melancholy frame I could hardly keep the tear out of my
eye while she dress I. it be not for Lord Lowborough - it be
not for Annabella - it be not for myself - it be for Arthur
Huntingdon that they rise.
* * * * *
13th. - they be go, and he be go. we be to be part for
more than two month, above ten week! a long, long time to live
and not to see he. but he have promise to write often, and make
I promise to write still oftener, because he will be busy settle
his affair, and I shall have nothing well to do. well, I think
I shall always have plenty to say. but oh! for the time when we
shall be always together, and can exchange our thought without the
intervention of these cold go-between, pen, ink, and paper!
22nd. - I have have several letter from Arthur already. they be
not long, but pass sweet, and just like himself, full of ardent
affection, and playful lively humour; but there be always a 'but'
in this imperfect world, and I do wish he would sometimes be
serious. I cannot get he to write or speak in real, solid
earnest. I donot much mind it now, but if it be always so, what
shall I do with the serious part of myself?
CHAPTER XXIII
Feb. 18, 1822. - early this morning Arthur mount his hunter and
set off in high glee to meet the - hound. he will be away all
day, and so I will amuse myself with my neglect diary, if I can
give that name to such an irregular composition. it be exactly
four month since I open it last.
I be marry now, and settle down as Mrs. Huntingdon of Grassdale
Manor. I have have eight week' experience of matrimony. and do I
regret the step I have take? no, though I must confess, in my
secret heart, that Arthur be not what I think he at first, and
if I have know he in the beginning as thoroughly as I do now, I
probably never should have love he, and if I love he first, and
then make the discovery, I fear I should have think it my duty
not to have marry he. to be sure I might have know he, for
every one be willing enough to tell I about he, and he himself
be no accomplished hypocrite, but I be wilfully blind; and now,
instead of regret that I do not discern his full character
before I be indissolubly bind to he, I be glad, for it have save
I a great deal of battle with my conscience, and a great deal of
consequent trouble and pain; and, whatever I ought to have do, my
duty now be plainly to love he and to cleave to he, and this just
tally with my inclination.
he be very fond of I, almost too fond. I could do with less
caressing and more rationality. I should like to be less of a pet
and more of a friend, if I might choose; but I willnot complain of
that: I be only afraid his affection lose in depth where it gain
in ardour. I sometimes liken it to a fire of dry twig and
branch compare with one of solid coal, very bright and hot; but
if it should burn itself out and leave nothing but ash behind,
what shall I do? but it willnot, it sha'n't, I be determine; and
surely I have power to keep it alive. so let I dismiss that
think at once. but Arthur be selfish; I be constrain to
acknowledge that; and, indeed, the admission give I less pain
than might be expect, for, since I love he so much, I can easily
forgive he for love himself: he like to be please, and it be
my delight to please he; and when I regret this tendency of his,
it be for his own sake, not for mine.
the first instance he give be on the occasion of our bridal tour.
he want to hurry it over, for all the continental scene be
already familiar to he: many have lose their interest in his eye,
and other have never have anything to lose. the consequence be,
that after a fly transit through part of France and part of
Italy, I come back nearly as ignorant as I go, have make no
acquaintance with person and manner, and very little with thing,
my head swarm with a motley confusion of object and scene;
some, it be true, leave a deep and more pleasing impression
than other, but these embitter by the recollection that my
emotion have not be share by my companion, but that, on the
contrary, when I have express a particular interest in anything
that I see or desire to see, it have be displease to he,
inasmuch as it prove that I could take delight in anything
disconnected with himself.
as for Paris, we only just touch at that, and he would not give
I time to see one-tenth of the beauty and interesting object of
Rome. he want to get I home, he say, to have I all to
himself, and to see I safely instal as the mistress of
Grassdale Manor, just as single-minded, as naive, and piquante as I
be; and as if I have be some frail butterfly, he express
himself fearful of rub the silver off my wing by bring I
into contact with society, especially that of Paris and Rome; and,
more-over, he do not scruple to tell I that there be lady in
both place that would tear his eye out if they happen to meet
he with I.
of course I be vex at all this; but still it be less the
disappointment to myself that annoy I, than the disappointment
in he, and the trouble I be at to frame excuse to my friend for
have see and observe so little, without impute one particle
of blame to my companion. but when we get home - to my new,
delightful home - I be so happy and he be so kind that I freely
forgave he all; and I be begin to think my lot too happy, and
my husband actually too good for I, if not too good for this
world, when, on the second Sunday after our arrival, he shock and
horrify I by another instance of his unreasonable exaction. we
be walk home from the morning service, for it be a fine
frosty day, and as we be so near the church, I have request the
carriage should not be use.
'Helen,' say he, with unusual gravity, 'I be not quite satisfied
with you.'
I desire to know what be wrong.
'but will you promise to reform if I tell you?'
'yes, if I can, and without offend a high authority.'
'ah! there it be, you see: you donot love I with all your heart.'
'I donot understand you, Arthur (at least I hope I donot): pray
tell I what I have do or say amiss.'
'it be nothing you have do or say; it be something that you be
- you be too religious. now I like a woman to be religious, and I
think your piety one of your great charm; but then, like all
other good thing, it may be carry too far. to my thinking, a
woman's religion ought not to lessen her devotion to her earthly
lord. she should have enough to purify and etherealise her soul,
but not enough to refine away her heart, and raise she above all
human sympathy.'
'and be I above all human sympathy?' say I.
'no, darling; but you be make more progress towards that saintly
condition than I like; for all these two hour I have be think
of you and want to catch your eye, and you be so absorb in
your devotion that you have not even a glance to spare for I - I
declare it be enough to make one jealous of one's Maker - which be
very wrong, you know; so donot excite such wicked passion again,
for my soul's sake.'
'I will give my whole heart and soul to my maker if I can,' I
answer, 'and not one atom more of it to you than he allow. what
be you, sir, that you should set yourself up as a god, and presume
to dispute possession of my heart with he to whom I owe all I have
and all I be, every blessing I ever do or ever can enjoy - and
yourself among the rest - if you be a blessing, which I be half
incline to doubt.'
'donot be so hard upon I, Helen; and donot pinch my arm so: you
be squeeze your finger into the bone.'
'Arthur,' continue I, relax my hold of his arm, 'you donot love
I half as much as I do you; and yet, if you love I far less than
you do, I would not complain, provide you love your maker more.
I should rejoice to see you at any time so deeply absorb in your
devotion that you have not a single thought to spare for I. but,
indeed, I should lose nothing by the change, for the more you love
your God the more deep and pure and true would be your love to I.'
at this he only laugh and kiss my hand, call I a sweet
enthusiast. then take off his hat, he add: 'but look here,
Helen - what can a man do with such a head as this?'
the head look right enough, but when he place my hand on the top
of it, it sink in a bed of curl, rather alarmingly low, especially
in the middle.
'you see I be not make to be a saint,' say he, laugh, 'if God
mean I to be religious, why donot he give I a proper organ of
veneration?'
'you be like the servant,' I reply, 'who, instead of employ
his one talent in his master's service, restore it to he
unimproved, allege, as an excuse, that he know he "to be a hard
man, reap where he have not sow, and gather where he have not
straw." of he to whom less be give, less will be require, but
our utmost exertion be require of we all. you be not without
the capacity of veneration, and faith and hope, and conscience and
reason, and every other requisite to a Christian's character, if
you choose to employ they; but all our talent increase in the
using, and every faculty, both good and bad, strengthen by
exercise: therefore, if you choose to use the bad, or those which
tend to evil, till they become your master, and neglect the good
till they dwindle away, you have only yourself to blame. but you
have talent, Arthur - natural endowment both of heart and mind
and temper, such as many a well Christian would be glad to
possess, if you would only employ they in God's service. I should
never expect to see you a devotee, but it be quite possible to be a
good Christian without cease to be a happy, merry-hearted man.'
'you speak like an oracle, Helen, and all you say be indisputably
true; but listen here: I be hungry, and I see before I a good
substantial dinner; I be tell that if I abstain from this to-day I
shall have a sumptuous feast to-morrow, consist of all manner of
dainty and delicacy. now, in the first place, I should be loth
to wait till to-morrow when I have the mean of appease my hunger
already before I: in the second place, the solid viand of to-day
be more to my taste than the dainty that be promise I; in the
third place, I donot see to-morrow's banquet, and how can I tell
that it be not all a fable, get up by the greasy-face fellow that
be advise I to abstain in order that he may have all the good
victual to himself? in the fourth place, this table must be spread
for somebody, and, as Solomon say, "who can eat, or who else can
hasten hereunto more than I?" and finally, with your leave, Iwill
sit down and satisfy my craving of to-day, and leave to-morrow to
shift for itself - who know but what I may secure both this and
that?'
'but you be not require to abstain from the substantial dinner of
to-day: you be only advise to partake of these coarser viand in
such moderation as not to incapacitate you from enjoy the
choicer banquet of to-morrow. if, regardless of that counsel, you
choose to make a beast of yourself now, and over-eat and over-drink
yourself till you turn the good victual into poison, who be to
blame if, hereafter, while you be suffer the torment of
yesterday's gluttony and drunkenness, you see more temperate man
sit down to enjoy themselves at that splendid entertainment
which you be unable to taste?'
'most true, my patron saint; but again, our friend Solomon say,
"there be nothing well for a man than to eat and to drink, and to
be merry."'
'and again,' return I, 'he say, "rejoice, o young man, in thy
youth; and walk in the way of thine heart, and in the sight of
thine eye: but know thou, that for all these thing God will
bring thee into judgment."'
'well, but, Helen, Ibe sure Ihave be very good these last few
week. what have you see amiss in I, and what would you have I
to do?'
'nothing more than you do, Arthur: your action be all right so
far; but I would have your thought change; I would have you to
fortify yourself against temptation, and not to call evil good, and
good evil; I should wish you to think more deeply, to look far,
and aim high than you do.'
CHAPTER XXIV
March 25th. - Arthur be get tired - not of I, I trust, but of
the idle, quiet life he lead - and no wonder, for he have so few
source of amusement: he never read anything but newspaper and
sporting magazine; and when he see I occupy with a book, he
willnot let I rest till I close it. in fine weather he generally
manage to get through the time pretty well, but on rainy day, of
which we have have a good many of late, it be quite painful to
witness his ennui. I do all I can to amuse he, but it be
impossible to get he to feel interested in what I most like to
talk about, while, on the other hand, he like to talk about thing
that cannot interest I - or even that annoy I - and these please
he - the most of all: for his favourite amusement be to sit or
loll beside I on the sofa, and tell I story of his former
amour, always turn upon the ruin of some confide girl or the
cozening of some unsuspecting husband; and when I express my horror
and indignation, he lay it all to the charge of jealousy, and
laugh till the tear run down his cheek. I use to fly into
passion or melt into tear at first, but see that his delight
increase in proportion to my anger and agitation, I have since
endeavour to suppress my feeling and receive his revelation in
the silence of calm contempt; but still he read the inward
struggle in my face, and misconstrue my bitterness of soul for his
unworthiness into the pang of wounded jealousy; and when he have
sufficiently divert himself with that, or fear my displeasure
will become too serious for his comfort, he try to kiss and
soothe I into smile again - never be his caress so little
welcome as then! this be double selfishness display to I and to
the victim of his former love. there be time when, with a
momentary pang - a flash of wild dismay, I ask myself, 'Helen, what
have you do?' but I rebuke the inward questioner, and repel the
obtrusive thought that crowd upon I; for be he ten time as
sensual and impenetrable to good and lofty thought, I well know I
have no right to complain. and I donot and willnot complain. I do
and will love he still; and I do not and will not regret that I
have link my fate with his.
April 4th. - we have have a downright quarrel. the particular be
as follow: Arthur have tell I, at different interval, the whole
story of his intrigue with Lady F-, which I would not believe
before. it be some consolation, however, to find that in this
instance the lady have be more to blame than he, for he be very
young at the time, and she have decidedly make the first advance,
if what he say be true. I hat she for it, for it seem as if
she have chiefly contribute to his corruption; and when he be
begin to talk about she the other day, I beg he would not
mention she, for I detest the very sound of her name.
'not because you love she, Arthur, mind, but because she injure
you and deceive her husband, and be altogether a very abominable
woman, whom you ought to be ashamed to mention.'
but he defend she by say that she have a doting old husband,
whom it be impossible to love.
'then why do she marry he?' say I.
'for his money,' be the reply.
'then that be another crime, and her solemn promise to love and
honour he be another, that only increase the enormity of the
last.'
'you be too severe upon the poor lady,' laugh he. 'but never
mind, Helen, I donot care for she now; and I never love any of
they half as much as I do you, so you neednot fear to be forsake
like they.'
'if you have tell I these thing before, Arthur, I never should
have give you the chance.'
'wouldnot you, my darling?'
'most certainly not!'
he laugh incredulously.
'I wish I could convince you of it now!' cry I, start up from
beside he: and for the first time in my life, and I hope the
last, I wish I have not marry he.
'Helen,' say he, more gravely, 'do you know that if I believe you
now I should be very angry? but thank heaven I donot. though you
stand there with your white face and flash eye, look at I
like a very tigress, I know the heart within you perhaps a trifle
well than you know it yourself.'
without another word I leave the room and lock myself up in my own
chamber. in about half an hour he come to the door, and first he
try the handle, then he knock.
'willnot you let I in, Helen?' say he.
'no; you have displease I,' I reply, 'and I donot want to see
your face or hear your voice again till the morning.'
he pause a moment as if dumfounde or uncertain how to answer such

it do not make I relent. I be determined to show he that my

tell she nothing of all this. soon after ten o'clock I hear

'be you cross still, Helen?' say he, approach as if to salute

coffee, observe that he be rather late.
he utter a low whistle and saunter away to the window, where he
stand for some minute look out upon the pleasing prospect of
sullen grey clouds, streaming rain, soak lawn, and dripping

'd-d cold.'

he make no answer, and the meal be conclude in silence. it be a

doubtless have order his horse and set off to some distant

would only minister to his self-conceit, increase his arrogance,
and quite destroy the lesson I want to give he.
he make a long stay in the dining-room after dinner, and, I fear,
take an unusual quantity of wine, but not enough to loosen his

and compose himself to sleep. but his favourite cocker, Dash,
that have be lie at my foot, take the liberty of jump upon
he and begin to lick his face. he strike it off with a smart

he wake up, about half an hour after, he call it to he again,
but Dash only look sheepish and wag the tip of his tail. he
call again more sharply, but Dash only cling the close to I,
and lick my hand, as if implore protection. enrage at this,
his master snatch up a heavy book and hurl it at his head. the
poor dog set up a piteous outcry, and run to the door. I let he
out, and then quietly take up the book.
'give that book to I,' say Arthur, in no very courteous tone. I
give it to he.
'why do you let the dog out?' he ask; 'you know I want he.'
'by what token?' I reply; 'by your throw the book at he? but
perhaps it be intend for I?'
'no; but I see youhave get a taste of it,' say he, look at my
hand, that have also be strike, and be rather severely graze.
I return to my reading, and he endeavour to occupy himself in

last his patience be tired out.
'what be that book, Helen?' he exclaim.
I tell he.
'be it interesting?'
'yes, very.'
I go on reading, or pretend to read, at least - I cannot say
there be much communication between my eye and my brain; for,

he continue lounge on the sofa, and alternately close his eye

take my candle and retire.
'Helen!' cry he, the moment I have leave the room. I turn back,
and stand await his command.
'what do you want, Arthur?' I say at length.
'nothing,' reply he. 'go!'
I go, but hear he mutter something as I be close the door,
I turn again. it sound very like 'confound slut,' but I be
quite willing it should be something else.
'be you speak, Arthur?' I ask.
'no,' be the answer, and I shut the door and depart. I see
nothing more of he till the follow morning at breakfast, when
he come down a full hour after the usual time.
'yoube very late,' be my morning's salutation.
'you neednot have wait for I,' be his; and he walk up to the
window again. it be just such weather as yesterday.
'oh, this confound rain!' he mutter. but, after studiously
regard it for a minute or two, a bright idea, seem to strike

return and take his seat at the table. the letter-bag be
already there, wait to be open. he unlock it and examine
the content, but say nothing about they.
'be there anything for I?' I ask.
'no.'
he open the newspaper and begin to read.
'youwould well take your coffee,' suggest I; 'it will be cold
again.'
'you may go,' say he, 'if youhave do; I donot want you.'
I rise and withdraw to the next room, wonder if we be to have

'I must not let he go to London, whatever come of it,' say I to

well, I will wait awhile, and see if he mention it.'
I wait most anxiously, from hour to hour; but not a word be

as on the previous day. at last I begin to think I must introduce
the subject myself, and be ponder how to bring it about, when
John unwittingly come to my relief with the following message from
the coachman:
'please, sir, Richard say one of the horse have get a very bad
cold, and he think, sir, if you could make it convenient to go the
day after to-morrow, instead of to-morrow, he could physic it to-
day, so as - '
'confound his impudence!' interject the master.
'please, sir, he say it would be a deal well if you could,'
persist John, 'for he hop therewill be a change in the weather
shortly, and he say itbe not likely, when a horse be so bad with a
cold, and physic and all - '
'devil take the horse!' cry the gentleman. 'well, tell he Iwill
think about it,' he add, after a moment's reflection. he cast a

some token of deep astonishment and alarm; but, be previously

'where do you want to go, Arthur?' say I.
'to London,' reply he, gravely.
'what for?' I ask.
'because I cannot be happy here.'
'why not?'
'because my wife donot love I.'
'she would love you with all her heart, if you deserve it.'
'what must I do to deserve it?'
this seem humble and earnest enough; and I be so much affected,
between sorrow and joy, that I be oblige to pause a few second
before I could steady my voice to reply.
'if she give you her heart,' say I, 'you must take it,
thankfully, and use it well, and not pull it in piece, and laugh
in her face, because she cannot snatch it away.'
he now turn round, and stand face I, with his back to the

this sound rather too arrogant, and the smile that accompany it

'heart-break!' he answer, with a rueful countenance, yet with a

he readily consent, but say there be no need of much

so we be to go to London on Monday, the day after to-morrow. it

sure it have do we both good: it have make I like Arthur a great
deal well, and make he behave a great deal well to I. he have

get he to regard such matter in the same light as I do. well! it

subject for a conjugal jest. he may see far some time. I
will put no limit to my hope; and, in spite of my aunt's
foreboding and my own unspoken fear, I trust we shall be happy
yet.
CHAPTER XXV
on the eighth of April we go to London, on the eighth of May I
return, in obedience to Arthur's wish; very much against my own,
because I leave he behind. if he have come with I, I should have
be very glad to get home again, for he lead I such a round of
restless dissipation while there, that, in that short space of
time, I be quite tired out. he seem bent upon display I to
his friend and acquaintance in particular, and the public in
general, on every possible occasion, and to the great possible
advantage. it be something to feel that he consider I a worthy
object of pride; but I pay dear for the gratification: for, in
the first place, to please he I have to violate my cherished
predilection, my almost root principle in favour of a plain,
dark, sober style of dress - I must sparkle in costly jewel and
deck myself out like a paint butterfly, just as I have, long
since, determine I would never do - and this be no trifling
sacrifice; in the second place, I be continually strain to
satisfy his sanguine expectation and do honour to his choice by my
general conduct and deportment, and fear to disappoint he by
some awkward misdemeanour, or some trait of inexperienced ignorance
about the custom of society, especially when I act the part of
hostess, which I be not unfrequently call upon to do; and, in
the third place, as I intimate before, I be weary of the throng
and bustle, the restless hurry and ceaseless change of a life so
alien to all my previous habit. at last, he suddenly discover
that the London air do not agree with I, and I be languish
for my country home, and must immediately return to Grassdale.
I laughingly assure he that the case be not so urgent as he
appear to think it, but I be quite willing to go home if he be.
he reply that he should be oblige to remain a week or two
long, as he have business that require his presence.
'then I will stay with you,' say I.
'but I cannot do with you, Helen,' be his answer: 'as long as you
stay I shall attend to you and neglect my business.'
'but I willnot let you,' I return; 'now that I know you have
business to attend to, I shall insist upon your attend to it,
and let I alone; and, to tell the truth, I shall be glad of a
little rest. I can take my ride and walk in the Park as usual;
and your business cannot occupy all your time: I shall see you at
meal-time, and in the evening at least, and that will be well
than be league away and never see you at all.'
'but, my love, I cannot let you stay. how can I settle my affair
when I know that you be here, neglect -?'
'I shall not feel myself neglect: while you be do your duty,
Arthur, I shall never complain of neglect. if you have tell I
before, that you have anything to do, it would have be half do
before this; and now you must make up for lost time by redouble
exertion. tell I what it be; and I will be your taskmaster,
instead of be a hindrance.'
'no, no,' persist the impracticable creature; 'you must go home,
Helen; I must have the satisfaction of know that you be safe
and well, though far away. your bright eye be fade, and that
tender, delicate bloom have quite desert your cheek.'
'that be only with too much gaiety and fatigue.'
'it be not, I tell you; it be the London air: you be pin for
the fresh breeze of your country home, and you shall feel they
before you be two day old. and remember your situation,
dear Helen; on your health, you know, depend the health, if not
the life, of our future hope.'
'then you really wish to get rid of I?'
'positively, I do; and I will take you down myself to Grassdale,
and then return. I shall not be absent above a week or fortnight
at most.'
'but if I must go, I will go alone: if you must stay, it be
needless to waste your time in the journey there and back.'
but he do not like the idea of send I alone.
'why, what helpless creature do you take I for,' I reply, 'that
you cannot trust I to go a hundred mile in our own carriage, with
our own footman and a maid to attend I? if you come with I I
shall assuredly keep you. but tell I, Arthur, what be this
tiresome business; and why do you never mention it before?'
'it be only a little business with my lawyer,' say he; and he tell
I something about a piece of property he want to sell, in order

account be a little confused, or I be rather dull of
comprehension, for I could not clearly understand how that should
keep he in town a fortnight after I. still less can I now

deceive I, or deceive himself. his excuse be vague and
insufficient. I cannot doubt that he have get among his former
companion again. oh, why do I leave he! I wish - I do
intensely wish he would return!
June 29th. - no Arthur yet; and for many day I have be look
and long in vain for a letter. his letter, when they come, be
kind, if fair word and endear epithet can give they a claim to
the title - but very short, and full of trivial excuse and
promise that I cannot trust; and yet how anxiously I look forward
to they I how eagerly I open and devour one of those little,
hastily-scribble return for the three or four long letter,
hitherto unanswered, he have have from I!
oh, it be cruel to leave I so long alone! he know I have no one
but Rachel to speak to, for we have no neighbour here, except the
Hargraves, whose residence I can dimly descry from these upper
window embosome among those low, woody hill beyond the Dale. I
be glad when I learn that Milicent be so near we; and her
company would be a soothe solace to I now; but she be still in
town with her mother; there be no one at the Grove but little
Esther and her french governess, for Walter be always away. I see
that paragon of manly perfection in London: he seem scarcely to
merit the eulogium of his mother and sister, though he certainly
appear more conversable and agreeable than Lord Lowborough, more

judge fit to introduce to I. - oh, Arthur, why willnot you come?
why willnot you write to I at least? you talk about my health:
how can you expect I to gather bloom and vigour here, pin in

July 3rd. - my last bitter letter have wring from he an answer at
last, and a rather long one than usual; but still I donot know
what to make of it. he playfully abuse I for the gall and

'your little friend Milicent be likely, before long, to follow your

say he have plenty of the needful himself, or should have when his
old governor choose to quit the stage. so you see, Helen, I have
manage pretty well, both for your friend and mine.'
Poor Milicent! but I cannot imagine she will ever be lead to accept
such a suitor - one so repugnant to all her idea of a man to be
honour and love.
5th. - alas! I be mistaken. I have get a long letter from she
this morning, tell I she be already engage, and expect to be
marry before the close of the month.
'I hardly know what to say about it,' she write, 'or what to
think. to tell you the truth, Helen, I donot like the thought of
it at all. if I be to be Mr. Hattersley's wife, I must try to love
he; and I do try with all my might; but I have make very little
progress yet; and the bad symptom of the case be, that the

quite as good as Mr. Huntingdon, if not well; and yet you love

this, but I donot know he. I know only the exterior, and what, I
trust, be the bad part of he.'
she conclude with 'good-by, dear Helen. I be wait anxiously
for your advice - but mind you let it be all on the right side.'
alas! poor Milicent, what encouragement can I give you? or what
advice - except that it be well to make a bold stand now, though
at the expense of disappointing and anger both mother and
brother and lover, than to devote your whole life, hereafter, to
misery and vain regret?
Saturday, 13th. - the week be over, and he be not come. all the
sweet summer be pass away without one breath of pleasure to I
or benefit to he. and I have all along be look forward to
this season with the fond, delusive hope that we should enjoy it so
sweetly together; and that, with God's help and my exertion, it
would be the mean of elevate his mind, and refine his taste to
a due appreciation of the salutary and pure delight of nature, and
peace, and holy love. but now - at evening, when I see the round
red sun sink quietly down behind those woody hill, leave they
sleep in a warm, red, golden haze, I only think another lovely
day be lose to he and I; and at morning, when rouse by the
flutter and chirp of the sparrow, and the gleeful twitter of the
swallow - all intent upon feed their young, and full of life
and joy in their own little frame - I open the window to inhale
the balmy, soul-revive air, and look out upon the lovely
landscape, laugh in dew and sunshine - I too often shame that
glorious scene with tear of thankless misery, because he cannot
feel its freshen influence; and when I wander in the ancient
wood, and meet the little wild flower smile in my path, or sit
in the shadow of our noble ash-tree by the water-side, with their
branch gently sway in the light summer breeze that murmur
through their feathery foliage - my ear full of that low music
mingle with the dreamy hum of insect, my eye abstractedly gaze
on the glassy surface of the little lake before I, with the tree
that crowd about its bank, some gracefully bend to kiss its
water, some rear their stately head high above, but stretch
their wide arm over its margin, all faithfully mirror far, far
down in its glassy depth - though sometimes the image be
partially break by the sport of aquatic insect, and sometimes,
for a moment, the whole be shiver into trembling fragment by a
transient breeze that sweep the surface too roughly - still I have
no pleasure; for the great the happiness that nature set before
I, the more I lament that he be not here to taste it: the great
the bliss we might enjoy together, the more I feel our present
wretchedness apart (yes, our; he must be wretche, though he may
not know it); and the more my sens be pleased, the more my heart
be oppress; for he keep it with he confine amid the dust and
smoke of London - perhaps shut up within the wall of his own
abominable club.
but most of all, at night, when I enter my lonely chamber, and look
out upon the summer moon, 'sweet regent of the sky,' float above
I in the 'black blue vault of heaven,' shed a flood of silver
radiance over park, and wood, and water, so pure, so peaceful, so
divine - and think, where be he now? - what be he do at this
moment? wholly unconscious of this heavenly scene - perhaps
revel with his boon companion, perhaps - God help I, it be
too - too much!
23rd. - thank heaven, he be come at last! but how alter! flushed
and feverish, listless and languid, his beauty strangely
diminish, his vigour and vivacity quite depart. I have not
upbraid he by word or look; I have not even ask he what he
have be do. I have not the heart to do it, for I think he be
ashamed of himself-he must be so indeed, and such inquiry could
not fail to be painful to both. my forbearance please he -
touch he even, I be inclined to think. he say he be glad to be
home again, and God know how glad I be to get he back, even as he
be. he lie on the sofa, nearly all day long; and I play and sing
to he for hour together. I write his letter for he, and get
he everything he want; and sometimes I read to he, and sometimes
I talk, and sometimes only sit by he and soothe he with silent
caress. I know he do not deserve it; and I fear I be spoil
he; but this once, I will forgive he, freely and entirely. I
will shame he into virtue if I can, and I will never let he leave
I again.
he be pleased with my attention - it may be, grateful for they.
he like to have I near he: and though he be peevish and testy
with his servant and his dog, he be gentle and kind to I. what
he would be, if I do not so watchfully anticipate his want, and
so carefully avoid, or immediately desist from do anything that
have a tendency to irritate or disturb he, with however little
reason, I cannot tell. how intensely I wish he be worthy of all
this care! last night, as I sit beside he, with his head in my
lap, pass my finger through his beautiful curl, this thought
make my eye overflow with sorrowful tear - as it often do; but
this time, a tear fall on his face and make he look up. he
smile, but not insultingly.
'Dear Helen!' he say - 'why do you cry? you know that I love you'
(and he press my hand to his feverish lip), 'and what more could
you desire?'
'only, Arthur, that you would love yourself as truly and as
faithfully as you be love by I.'
'that would be hard, indeed!' he reply, tenderly squeeze my
hand.
August 24th. - Arthur be himself again, as lusty and reckless, as
light of heart and head as ever, and as restless and hard to amuse
as a spoilt child, and almost as full of mischief too, especially
when wet weather keep he within door. I wish he have something
to do, some useful trade, or profession, or employment - anything
to occupy his head or his hand for a few hour a day, and give he
something besides his own pleasure to think about. if he would
play the country gentleman and attend to the farm - but that he
know nothing about, and willnot give his mind to consider, - or if
he would take up with some literary study, or learn to draw or to
play - as he be so fond of music, I often try to persuade he to
learn the piano, but he be far too idle for such an undertaking:
he have no more idea of exert himself to overcome obstacle than
he have of restrain his natural appetite; and these two thing
be the ruin of he. I lie they both to the charge of his harsh
yet careless father, and his madly indulgent mother. - if ever I be
a mother I will zealously strive against this crime of over-
indulgence. I can hardly give it a milder name when I think of the
evil it bring.
happily, it will soon be the shoot season, and then, if the
weather permit, he will find occupation enough in the pursuit and
destruction of the partridge and pheasant: we have no grouse, or
he might have be similarly occupy at this moment, instead of
lie under the acacia-tree pull poor dash's ear. but he say
it be dull work shooting alone; he must have a friend or two to
help he.
'let they be tolerably decent then, Arthur,' say I. the word
'friend' in his mouth make I shudder: I know it be some of his
'friend' that induce he to stay behind I in London, and keep
he away so long: indeed, from what he have unguardedly tell I, or
hint from time to time, I cannot doubt that he frequently show
they my letter, to let they see how fondly his wife watch over
his interest, and how keenly she regret his absence; and that
they induce he to remain week after week, and to plunge into all
manner of excess, to avoid be laugh at for a wife-ride
fool, and, perhaps, to show how far he could venture to go without
danger of shake the fond creature's devoted attachment. it be a
hateful idea, but I cannot believe it be a false one.
'well,' reply he, 'I think of Lord Lowborough for one; but
there be no possibility of get he without his well half, our
mutual friend, Annabella; so we must ask they both. yoube not
afraid of she, be you, Helen?' he ask, with a mischievous
twinkle in his eye.
'of course not,' I answer: 'why should I? and who besides?'
'hargrave for one. he will be glad to come, though his own place
be so near, for he have little enough land of his own to shoot over,
and we can extend our depredation into it, if we like; and he be
thoroughly respectable, you know, Helen - quite a lady's man: and
I think, Grimsby for another: hebe a decent, quiet fellow enough.
youwill not object to Grimsby?'
'I hate he: but, however, if you wish it, Iwill try to endure his
presence for a while.'
'all a prejudice, Helen, a mere woman's antipathy.'
'no; I have solid ground for my dislike. and be that all?'
'why, yes, I think so. Hattersley will be too busy billing and
cooing, with his bride to have much time to spare for gun and dog
at present,' he reply. and that remind I, that I have have
several letter from Milicent since her marriage, and that she
either be, or pretend to be, quite reconcile to her lot. she
profess to have discover numberless virtue and perfection in
her husband, some of which, I fear, less partial eye would fail to
distinguish, though they seek they carefully with tear; and now
that she be accustomed to his loud voice, and abrupt, uncourteous
manner, she affirm she find no difficulty in love he as a
wife should do, and beg I will burn that letter wherein she speak
so unadvisedly against he. so that I trust she may yet be happy;
but, if she be, it will be entirely the reward of her own goodness
of heart; for have she choose to consider herself the victim of
fate, or of her mother's worldly wisdom, she might have be
thoroughly miserable; and if, for duty's sake, she have not make
every effort to love her husband, she would, doubtless, have hat
he to the end of her day.
CHAPTER XXVI
Sept. 23rd. - our guest arrive about three week ago. Lord and
Lady Lowborough have now be marry above eight month; and I
will do the lady the credit to say that her husband be quite an
altered man; his look, his spirit, and his temper, be all
perceptibly change for the well since I last see he. but there
be room for improvement still. he be not always cheerful, nor
always content, and she often complain of his ill-humour, which,
however, of all person, she ought to be the last to accuse he of,
as he never display it against she, except for such conduct as
would provoke a saint. he adore she still, and would go to the
world's end to please she. she know her power, and she use it
too; but well know that to wheedle and coax be safe than to
command, she judiciously temper her despotism with flattery and
blandishment enough to make he deem himself a favoured and a
happy man.
but she have a way of torment he, in which I be a fellow-
sufferer, or might be, if I choose to regard myself as such. this
be by openly, but not too glaringly, coquet with Mr.
Huntingdon, who be quite willing to be her partner in the game; but
I donot care for it, because, with he, I know there be nothing but
personal vanity, and a mischievous desire to excite my jealousy,
and, perhaps, to torment his friend; and she, no doubt, be actuate
by much the same motif; only, there be more of malice and less of
playfulness in her manoeuvre. it be obviously, therefore, my
interest to disappoint they both, as far as I be concern, by
preserve a cheerful, undisturbed serenity throughout; and,
accordingly, I endeavour to show the full confidence in my
husband, and the great indifference to the art of my attractive
guest. I have never reproach the former but once, and that be
for laugh at Lord Lowborough's depressed and anxious countenance
one evening, when they have both be particularly provoking; and
then, indeed, I say a good deal on the subject, and rebuke he
sternly enough; but he only laugh, and say, - 'you can feel for
he, Helen, cannot you?'
'I can feel for anyone that be unjustly treat,' I reply, 'and I
can feel for those that injure they too.'
'why, Helen, you be as jealous as he be!' cry he, laugh still
more; and I find it impossible to convince he of his mistake.
so, from that time, I have carefully refrain from any notice of
the subject whatever, and leave Lord Lowborough to take care of
himself. he either have not the sense or the power to follow my
example, though he do try to conceal his uneasiness as well as he
can; but still, it will appear in his face, and his ill-humour will
peep out at interval, though not in the expression of open
resentment - they never go far enough for that. but I confess I do
feel jealous at time, most painfully, bitterly so; when she sing
and play to he, and he hang over the instrument, and dwell upon
her voice with no affected interest; for then I know he be really
delighted, and I have no power to awaken similar fervour. I can
amuse and please he with my simple song, but not delight he
thus.
28th. - yesterday, we all go to the Grove, Mr. Hargrave's much-
neglect home. his mother frequently ask we over, that she may
have the pleasure of her dear Walter's company; and this time she
have invite we to a dinner-party, and get together as many of the
country gentry as be within reach to meet we. the entertainment
be very well get up; but I could not help think about the cost
of it all the time. I donot like Mrs. Hargrave; she be a hard,
pretentious, worldly-minded woman. she have money enough to live
very comfortably, if she only know how to use it judiciously, and
have teach her son to do the same; but she be ever strain to
keep up appearance, with that despicable pride that shun the
semblance of poverty as of a shameful crime. she grind her
dependent, pinch her servant, and deprive even her daughter
and herself of the real comfort of life, because she will not
consent to yield the palm in outward show to those who have three
time her wealth; and, above all, because she be determined she
cherish son shall be enable to 'hold up his head with the
high gentleman in the land.' this same son, I imagine, be a man
of expensive habit, no reckless spendthrift and no abandon
sensualist, but one who like to have 'everything handsome about
he,' and to go to a certain length in youthful indulgence, not so
much to gratify his own taste as to maintain his reputation as a
man of fashion in the world, and a respectable fellow among his own
lawless companion; while he be too selfish to consider how many
comfort might be obtain for his fond mother and sister with the
money he thus waste upon himself: as long as they can contrive to
make a respectable appearance once a year, when they come to town,
he give himself little concern about their private stinting and
struggle at home. this be a harsh judgment to form of 'dear,
noble-minded, generous-hearted Walter,' but I fear it be too just.
Mrs. Hargrave's anxiety to make good match for her daughter be
partly the cause, and partly the result, of these error: by
make a figure in the world, and show they off to advantage,
she hop to obtain well chance for they; and by thus live
beyond her legitimate mean, and lavish so much on their
brother, she render they portionless, and make they burden on
her hand. poor Milicent, I fear, have already fall a sacrifice
to the manoeuvring of this mistaken mother, who congratulate
herself on have so satisfactorily discharge her maternal duty,
and hop to do as well for Esther. but Esther be a child as yet,
a little merry romp of fourteen: as honest-hearted, and as
guileless and simple as her sister, but with a fearless spirit of
her own, that I fancy her mother will find some difficulty in
bend to her purpose.
CHAPTER XXVII
October 9th. - it be on the night of the 4th, a little after tea,
that Annabella have be sing and playing, with Arthur as usual
at her side: she have end her song, but still she sit at the
instrument; and he stand lean on the back of her chair,
converse in scarcely audible tone, with his face in very close
proximity with her. I look at Lord Lowborough. he be at the
other end of the room, talk with Messrs. Hargrave and Grimsby;
but I see he dart towards his lady and his host a quick, impatient
glance, expressive of intense disquietude, at which Grimsby smile.
determine to interrupt the TETE-e-tete, I rise, and, select a
piece of music from the music stand, step up to the piano,
intend to ask the lady to play it; but I stand transfix and
speechless on see she seat there, listen, with what seem
an exultant smile on her flushed face to his soft murmuring, with
her hand quietly surrender to his clasp. the blood rush first
to my heart, and then to my head; for there be more than this:
almost at the moment of my approach, he cast a hurried glance over
his shoulder towards the other occupant of the room, and then
ardently press the unresiste hand to his lip. on raise his
eye, he behold I, and drop they again, confound and
dismayed. she see I too, and confront I with a look of hard
defiance. I lay the music on the piano, and retire. I feel ill;
but I do not leave the room: happily, it be get late, and
could not be long before the company disperse.
I go to the fire, and leant my head against the chimney-piece.
in a minute or two, some one ask I if I feel unwell. I do not
answer; indeed, at the time, I know not what be say; but I
mechanically look up, and see Mr. Hargrave stand beside I on
the rug.
'shall I get you a glass of wine?' say he.
'no, thank you,' I reply; and, turn from he, I look round.
Lady Lowborough be beside her husband, bend over he as he sit,
with her hand on his shoulder, softly talk and smile in his
face; and Arthur be at the table, turn over a book of
engraving. I seat myself in the near chair; and Mr.
Hargrave, find his service be not desire, judiciously
withdraw. shortly after, the company break up, and, as the guest
be retire to their room, Arthur approach I, smile with
the utmost assurance.
'be you very angry, Helen?' murmur he.
'this be no jest, Arthur,' say I, seriously, but as calmly as I
could - 'unless you think it a jest to lose my affection for ever.'
'what! so bitter?' he exclaim, laughingly, clasp my hand
between both his; but I snatch it away, in indignation - almost
in disgust, for he be obviously affect with wine.
'then I must go down on my knee,' say he; and kneeling before I,
with clasped hand, uplifted in mock humiliation, he continue
imploringly - 'forgive I, Helen - dear Helen, forgive I, and Iwill
never do it again!' and, bury his face in his handkerchief, he
affect to sob aloud.
leave he thus employ, I take my candle, and, slip quietly
from the room, hasten up-stair as fast as I could. but he soon
discover that I have leave he, and, rush up after I, catch I
in his arm, just as I have enter the chamber, and be about to
shut the door in his face.
'no, no, by heaven, you sha'n't escape I so!' he cry. then,
alarm at my agitation, he beg I not to put myself in such a
passion, tell I I be white in the face, and should kill myself
if I do so.
'let I go, then,' I murmur; and immediately he release I - and
it be well he do, for I be really in a passion. I sink into the
easy-chair and endeavour to compose myself, for I want to speak
to he calmly. he stand beside I, but do not venture to touch I
or to speak for a few second; then, approach a little nearer,
he drop on one knee - not in mock humility, but to bring himself
nearer my level, and lean his hand on the arm of the chair, he
begin in a low voice: 'it be all nonsense, Helen - a jest, a mere
nothing - not worth a thought. will you never learn,' he continue
more boldly, 'that you have nothing to fear from I? that I love
you wholly and entirely? - or if,' he add with a lurking smile,
'I ever give a thought to another, you may well spare it, for those
fancy be here and go like a flash of lightning, while my love
for you burn on steadily, and for ever, like the sun. you little
exorbitant tyrant, will not that -?'
'be quiet a moment, will you, Arthur?' say I, 'and listen to I -
and donot think Ibe in a jealous fury: I be perfectly calm. feel
my hand.' and I gravely extend it towards he - but close it
upon his with an energy that seem to disprove the assertion, and
make he smile. 'you neednot smile, sir,' say I, still tighten
my grasp, and look steadfastly on he till he almost quail
before I. 'you may think it all very fine, Mr. Huntingdon, to
amuse yourself with rouse my jealousy; but take care you donot
rouse my hate instead. and when you have once extinguish my
love, you will find it no easy matter to kindle it again.'
'well, Helen, I willnot repeat the offence. but I mean nothing by
it, I assure you. I have take too much wine, and I be scarcely
myself at the time.'
'you often take too much; and that be another practice I detest.'
he look up astonish at my warmth. 'yes,' I continue; 'I never
mention it before, because I be ashamed to do so; but now Iwill
tell you that it distress I, and may disgust I, if you go on
and suffer the habit to grow upon you, as it will if you donot
check it in time. but the whole system of your conduct to Lady
Lowborough be not referable to wine; and this night you know
perfectly well what you be do.'
'well, Ibe sorry for it,' reply he, with more of sulkiness than
contrition: 'what more would you have?'
'you be sorry that I see you, no doubt,' I answer coldly.
'if you have not see I,' he mutter, fix his eye on the
carpet, 'it would have do no harm.'
my heart feel ready to burst; but I resolutely swallow back my
emotion, and answer calmly,
'you think not?'
'no,' reply he, boldly. 'after all, what have I do? itbe
nothing - except as you choose to make it a subject of accusation
and distress.'
'what would Lord Lowborough, your friend, think, if he know all? or
what would you yourself think, if he or any other have act the
same part to I, throughout, as you have to Annabella?'
'I would blow his brain out.'
'well, then, Arthur, how can you call it nothing - an offence for
which you would think yourself justify in blow another man's
brain out? be it nothing to trifle with your friend's feeling
and mine - to endeavour to steal a woman's affection from her
husband - what he value more than his gold, and therefore what it
be more dishonest to take? be the marriage vow a jest; and be it
nothing to make it your sport to break they, and to tempt another
to do the same? can I love a man that do such thing, and coolly
maintain it be nothing?'
'you be break your marriage vow yourself,' say he,
indignantly rise and pace to and fro. 'you promise to honour
and obey I, and now you attempt to hector over I, and threaten
and accuse I, and call I bad than a highwayman. if it be not
for your situation, Helen, I would not submit to it so tamely. I
willnot be dictate to by a woman, though she be my wife.'
'what will you do then? will you go on till I hate you, and then
accuse I of break my vow?'
he be silent a. moment, and then reply: 'you never will hate
I.' return and resume his former position at my foot, he
repeat more vehemently - 'you cannot hate I as long as I love
you.'
'but how can I believe that you love I, if you continue to act in
this way? just imagine yourself in my place: would you think I
love you, if I do so? would you believe my protestation, and
honour and trust I under such circumstance? '
'the case be different,' he reply. 'it be a woman's nature to
be constant - to love one and one only, blindly, tenderly, and for
ever - bless they, dear creature! and you above they all; but you
must have some commiseration for we, Helen; you must give we a
little more licence, for, as Shakespeare have it -
however we do praise ourselves,
our fancy be more giddy and unfirm,
More longing, wavering, soon lose and win
than woman's be.'
'do you mean by that, that your fancy be lose to I, and win by
Lady Lowborough?'
'No! heaven be my witness that I think her mere dust and ash in
comparison with you, and shall continue to think so, unless you
drive I from you by too much severity. she be a daughter of
earth; you be an angel of heaven; only be not too austere in your
divinity, and remember that I be a poor, fallible mortal. come
now, Helen; willnot you forgive I?' he say, gently take my hand,
and look up with an innocent smile.
'if I do, you will repeat the offence.'
'I swear by - '
'donot swear; Iwill believe your word as well as your oath. I wish
I could have confidence in either.'
'try I, then, Helen: only trust and pardon I this once, and you
shall see! come, I be in hell's torment till you speak the word.'
I do not speak it, but I put my hand on his shoulder and kiss
his forehead, and then burst into tear. he embrace I tenderly;
and we have be good friend ever since. he have be decently
temperate at table, and well-conduct towards Lady Lowborough.
the first day he hold himself aloof from she, as far as he could
without any flagrant breach of hospitality: since that he have be
friendly and civil, but nothing more - in my presence, at least,
nor, I think, at any other time; for she seem haughty and
displease, and Lord Lowborough be manifestly more cheerful, and
more cordial towards his host than before. but I shall be glad
when they be go, for I have so little love for Annabella that it
be quite a task to be civil to she, and as she be the only woman
here besides myself, we be necessarily throw so much together.
next time Mrs. Hargrave call I shall hail her advent as quite a
relief. I have a good mind to ask Arthur's leave to invite the old
lady to stay with we till our guest depart. I think I will. she
will take it as a kind attention, and, though I have little relish
for her society, she will be truly welcome as a third to stand
between Lady Lowborough and I.
the first time the latter and I be alone together, after that
unhappy evening, be an hour or two after breakfast on the
follow day, when the gentleman be go out, after the usual
time spend in the writing of letter, the reading of newspaper,
and desultory conversation. we sit silent for two or three
minute. she be busy with her work, and I be run over the
column of a paper from which I have extract all the pith some
twenty minute before. it be a moment of painful embarrassment to
I, and I think it must be infinitely more so to she; but it
seem I be mistaken. she be the first to speak; and, smile
with the cool assurance, she begin, -
'your husband be merry last night, Helen: be he often so?'
my blood boil in my face; but it be well she should seem to
attribute his conduct to this than to anything else.
'no,' reply I, 'and never will be so again, I trust.'
'you give he a curtain lecture, do you?'
'no! but I tell he I dislike such conduct, and he promise I not
to repeat it.'
'I think he look rather subdue this morning,' she continue;
'and you, Helen? youhave be weep, I see - thatbe our grand
resource, you know. but donot it make your eye smart? and do
you always find it to answer?'
'I never cry for effect; nor can I conceive how any one can.'
'well, I donot know: I never have occasion to try it; but I think
if Lowborough be to commit such impropriety, Iwould make he cry.
I donot wonder at your be angry, for Ibe sure Iwould give my
husband a lesson he would not soon forget for a light offence
than that. but then he never will do anything of the kind; for I
keep he in too good order for that.'
'be you sure you donot arrogate too much of the credit to
yourself. Lord Lowborough be quite as remarkable for his
abstemiousness for some time before you marry he, as he be now,
I have hear.'
'oh, about the wine you mean - yes, hebe safe enough for that. and
as to look askance to another woman, hebe safe enough for that
too, while I live, for he worship the very ground I tread on.'
'indeed! and be you sure you deserve it?'
'why, as to that, I cannot say: you know webe all fallible
creature, Helen; we none of we deserve to be worship. but be
you sure your darling Huntingdon deserve all the love you give to
he?'
I know not what to answer to this. I be burn with anger; but I
suppress all outward manifestation of it, and only bit my lip
and pretend to arrange my work.
'at any rate,' resume she, pursue her advantage, 'you can
console yourself with the assurance that you be worthy of all the
love he give to you.'
'you flatter I,' say I; 'but, at least, I can try to be worthy of
it.' and then I turn the conversation.
CHAPTER XXVIII
December 25th. - last Christmas I be a bride, with a heart
overflow with present bliss, and full of ardent hope for the
future, though not unmingled with foreboding fear. now I be a
wife: my bliss be sober, but not destroy; my hope diminish,
but not depart; my fear increase, but not yet thoroughly
confirm; and, thank heaven, I be a mother too. God have send I a
soul to educate for heaven, and give I a new and calmer bliss, and
strong hope to comfort I.
Dec. 25th, 1823. - another year be go. my little Arthur live
and thrive. he be healthy, but not robust, full of gentle
playfulness and vivacity, already affectionate, and susceptible of
passion and emotion it will be long ere he can find word to
express. he have win his father's heart at last; and now my
constant terror be, lest he should be ruin by that father's
thoughtless indulgence. but I must beware of my own weakness too,
for I never know till now how strong be a parent's temptation to
spoil an only child.
I have need of consolation in my son, for (to this silent paper I
may confess it) I have but little in my husband. I love he still;
and he love I, in his own way - but oh, how different from the
love I could have give, and once have hop to receive! how little
real sympathy there exist between we; how many of my thought and
feeling be gloomily cloister within my own mind; how much of my
high and well self be indeed unmarried - doom either to
harden and sour in the sunless shade of solitude, or to quite
degenerate and fall away for lack of nutriment in this unwholesome
soil! but, I repeat, I have no right to complain; only let I
state the truth - some of the truth, at least, - and see hereafter
if any dark truth will blot these page. we have now be full
two year unite; the 'romance' of our attachment must be wear
away. surely I have now get down to the low gradation in
Arthur's affection, and discover all the evil of his nature: if
there be any further change, it must be for the well, as we
become still more accustomed to each other; surely we shall find no
low depth than this. and, if so, I can bear it well - as well,
at least, as I have bear it hitherto.
Arthur be not what be commonly call a bad man: he have many good
quality; but he be a man without self-restraint or lofty
aspiration, a lover of pleasure, give up to animal enjoyment:
he be not a bad husband, but his notion of matrimonial duty and
comfort be not my notion. judge from appearance, his idea of
a wife be a thing to love one devotedly, and to stay at home to
wait upon her husband, and amuse he and minister to his comfort in
every possible way, while he choose to stay with she; and, when he
be absent, to attend to his interest, domestic or otherwise, and
patiently wait his return, no matter how he may be occupy in the
meantime.
early in spring he announce his intention of go to London: his
affair there demand his attendance, he say, and he could refuse
it no long. he express his regret at have to leave I, but
hop I would amuse myself with the baby till he return.
'but why leave I?' I say. 'I can go with you: I can be ready at
any time.'
'you would not take that child to town?'
'yes; why not?'
the thing be absurd: the air of the town would be certain to
disagree with he, and with I as a nurse; the late hour and
London habit would not suit I under such circumstance; and
altogether he assure I that it would be excessively troublesome,
injurious, and unsafe. I over-rule his objection as well as I
could, for I tremble at the thought of his go alone, and would
sacrifice almost anything for myself, much even for my child, to
prevent it; but at length he tell I, plainly, and somewhat
testily, that he could not do with I: he be wear out with the
baby's restless night, and must have some repose. I propose
separate apartment; but it would not do.
'the truth be, Arthur,' I say at last, 'you be weary of my
company, and determine not to have I with you. you might as well
have say so at once.'
he deny it; but I immediately leave the room, and fly to the
nursery, to hide my feeling, if I could not soothe they, there.
I be too much hurt to express any further dissatisfaction with his
plan, or at all to refer to the subject again, except for the
necessary arrangement concern his departure and the conduct of
affair during his absence, till the day before he go, when I
earnestly exhort he to take care of himself and keep out of the

'I donot wish to keep you a prisoner at home,' I reply; 'I should

'Pooh, pooh, you silly girl! do you think I cannot take care of

'you donot last time. but this time, Arthur,' I add, earnestly,

he promise fair, but in such a manner as we seek to soothe a

more gentle in his reply, and promise to return; but I have
learn, at last, to disregard his promise.
CHAPTER XXIX
those be four miserable month, alternate between intense

CHAPTER XXX
on the follow morning I receive a few line from he myself,

by coax, and daring, and determination, I succeed in
preserve he from absolute bondage to that detestable propensity,
so insidious in its advance, so inexorable in its tyranny, so
disastrous in its effect.
and here I must not forget that I be not a little indebted to his
friend Mr. Hargrave. about that time he frequently call at
Grassdale, and often din with we, on which occasion I fear
Arthur would willingly have cast prudence and decorum to the wind,
and make 'a night of it,' as often as his friend would have

have choose to comply, he might, in a night or two, have ruin the

and indeed I know not whether, at the time, it be not for he
rather than myself that I blush; for, since he and I be one, I
so identify myself with he, that I feel his degradation, his
failing, and transgression as my own: I blush for he, I fear

myself; but I cannot act for he; and hence I must be, and I be,

partaker in his sin. thing that formerly shock and disgust

and God's word declare they to be so; but I be gradually lose
that instinctive horror and repulsion which be give I by
nature, or instill into I by the precept and example of my
aunt. perhaps then I be too severe in my judgment, for I
abhor the sinner as well as the sin; now I flatter myself I be
more charitable and considerate; but be I not become more
indifferent and insensate too? fool that I be, to dream that I
have strength and purity enough to save myself and he! such vain
presumption would be rightly serve, if I should perish with he in
the gulf from which I seek to save he! yet, God preserve I
from it, and he too! yes, poor Arthur, I will still hope and pray
for you; and though I write as if you be some abandon wretch,
past hope and past reprieve, it be only my anxious fear, my strong
desire that make I do so; one who love you less would be less
bitter, less dissatisfied.
his conduct have, of late, be what the world call irreproachable;
but then I know his heart be still unchanged; and I know that
spring be approach, and deeply dread the consequence.
as he begin to recover the tone and vigour of his exhausted frame,

about the third week in August, Arthur set out for Scotland, and
Mr. Hargrave accompany he thither, to my private satisfaction.
shortly after, I, with little Arthur and Rachel, go to
Staningley, my dear old home, which, as well as my dear old friend
its inhabitant, I see again with mingled feeling of pleasure and
pain so intimately blend that I could scarcely distinguish the
one from the other, or tell to which to attribute the various
tear, and smile, and sigh awaken by those old familiar scene,
and tone, and face.
Arthur do not come home till several week after my return to

ground be not harden by frost; so that he be not wholly dependent

approach; and, I repeat, I dread the consequence of its
arrival. that sweet season, I once so joyously welcome as the
time of hope and gladness, awaken now far other anticipation by
its return.
CHAPTER XXXI
March 20th, 1824. the dreaded time be come, and Arthur be go, as

Sine as ye brew, my maiden fair,

yes; and I will drink it to the very dreg: and none but myself

you!' cry Hattersley, snatch up a footstool and hurt it at

latter still sit collapse and quake with feeble laughter, with
tear run down his face: a deplorable spectacle indeed.

have be do, and suspense, not know what might far happen,

write no more about that.
such disgraceful scene (or nearly such) have be repeat more

would do more harm than good; but I let he know that I intensely
dislike such exhibition; and each time he have promise they should

have comport herself with wonderful propriety towards he,
treat he with more uniform kindness and consideration than ever
I have observe she to do before. I date the time of this
improvement from the period when she cease to hope and strive for
Arthur's admiration.
CHAPTER XXXII
October 5th. - Esther Hargrave be get a fine girl. she be not
out of the school-room yet, but her mother frequently bring she

procure to rectify the pupil's natural quality), and, now and

lot in life, and so do she; but her speculation on the future

she be awaken, like I, to a sense of their delusive vanity.
it seem as if I should feel her disappointment, even more deeply

CHAPTER XXXIII
Seventh. - yes, I will hope! to-night I hear Grimsby and
Hattersley grumble together about the inhospitality of their
host. they do not know I be near, for I happen to be stand
behind the curtain in the bow of the window, watch the moon

wonder why Arthur be so sentimental as to stand without,
lean against the outer pillar of the portico, apparently

youwill see.'

Hargrave and Annabella, from different motif and in different

and be in perfect good-humour, though not excited by wine. so

9th. - yesterday, when Rachel come to dress I for dinner, I see

and she go on dress my hair, in a hurried way, quite unlike

'oh, Walter, how you talk!' cry Milicent; 'she have far more

'yes, as a chess-player.'

as the usual hour of retirement approach I dry my eye, and

'no.'

'very good.'

notwithstanding this resolution, I find my bed so intolerable
