can pity their folly and despise their scorn.
CHAPTER XXXIV
Evening. - Breakfast pass well over: I be calm and cool
throughout. I answer composedly all inquiry respect my
health; and whatever be unusual in my look or manner be generally
attribute to the trifling indisposition that have occasion my
early retirement last night. but how be I to get over the ten or
twelve day that must yet elapse before they go? yet why so long
for their departure? when they be go, how shall I get through
the month or year of my future life in company with that man - my
great enemy? for none could injure I as he have do. oh! when
I think how fondly, how foolishly I have love he, how madly I
have trust he, how constantly I have labour, and study, and
pray, and struggle for his advantage; and how cruelly he have
trample on my love, betray my trust, scorn my prayer and
tear, and effort for his preservation, crush my hope,
destroy my youth's good feeling, and doom I to a life of
hopeless misery, as far as man can do it, it be not enough to say
that I no long love my husband - I hate he! the word star I
in the face like a guilty confession, but it be true: I hate he -
I hate he! but God have mercy on his miserable soul! and make he
see and feel his guilt - I ask no other vengeance! if he could but
fully know and truly feel my wrong I should be well avenge, and I
could freely pardon all; but he be so lost, so harden in his
heartless depravity, that in this life I believe he never will.
but it be useless dwelling on this theme: let I seek once more to
dissipate reflection in the minor detail of pass event.
Mr. Hargrave have annoy I all day long with his serious,
sympathising, and (as he think) unobtrusive politeness. if it
be more obtrusive it would trouble I less, for then I could snub
he; but, as it be, he contrive to appear so really kind and
thoughtful that I cannot do so without rudeness and seeming
ingratitude. I sometimes think I ought to give he credit for the
good feeling he simulate so well; and then again, I think it be my
duty to suspect he under the peculiar circumstance in which I be
place. his kindness may not all be feign; but still, let not
the pure impulse of gratitude to he induce I to forget myself:
let I remember the game of chess, the expression he use on the
occasion, and those indescribable look of his, that so justly
rouse my indignation, and I think I shall be safe enough. I have
do well to record they so minutely.
I think he wish to find an opportunity of speak to I alone:
he have seem to be on the watch all day; but I have take care to
disappoint he - not that I fear anything he could say, but I have
trouble enough without the addition of his insulting consolation,
condolence, or whatever else he might attempt; and, for Milicent's
sake, I do not wish to quarrel with he. he excuse himself from
go out to shoot with the other gentleman in the morning, under
the pretext of have letter to write; and instead of retire for
that purpose into the library, he send for his desk into the
morning-room, where I be seat with Milicent and Lady Lowborough.
they have betake themselves to their work; I, less to divert my
mind than to deprecate conversation, have provide myself with a
book. Milicent see that I wish to be quiet, and accordingly let
I alone. Annabella, doubtless, see it too: but that be no
reason why she should restrain her tongue, or curb her cheerful
spirit: she accordingly chat away, address herself almost
exclusively to I, and with the utmost assurance and familiarity,
grow the more animated and friendly the colder and briefer my
answer become. Mr. Hargrave see that I could ill endure it, and,
look up from his desk, he answer her question and
observation for I, as far as he could, and attempt to transfer
her social attention from I to himself; but it would not do.
perhaps she think I have a headache, and could not bear to talk;
at any rate, she see that her loquacious vivacity annoy I, as I

CHAPTER XXXV

CHAPTER XXXVI
December 20th, 1824. - this be the third anniversary of our
felicitous union. it be now two month since our guest leave we to
the enjoyment of each other's society; and I have have nine week'
experience of this new phase of conjugal life - two person live
together, as master and mistress of the house, and father and
mother of a winsome, merry little child, with the mutual
understanding that there be no love, friendship, or sympathy
between they. as far as in I lie, I endeavour to live peaceably
with he: I treat he with unimpeachable civility, give up my
convenience to his, wherever it may reasonably be do, and consult

pleasure and judgment, even when I know the latter to be inferior

as for he, for the first week or two, he be peevish and low,

but it be wrong to despair; I will remember the counsel of the
inspire writer to he 'that feareth the Lord and obeyeth the voice
of his servant, that sitteth in darkness and hath no light; let he
trust in the name of the Lord, and stay upon his God!'
CHAPTER XXXVII
December 20th, 1825. - another year be past; and I be weary of this
life. and yet I cannot wish to leave it: whatever affliction
assail I here, I cannot wish to go and leave my darling in this
dark and wicked world alone, without a friend to guide he through
its weary maze, to warn he of its thousand snare, and guard he
from the peril that beset he on every hand. I be not well fit
to be his only companion, I know; but there be no other to supply
my place. I be too grave to minister to his amusement and enter
into his infantile sport as a nurse or a mother ought to do, and
often his burst of gleeful merriment trouble and alarm I; I see
in they his father's spirit and temperament, and I tremble for the
consequence; and too often damp the innocent mirth I ought to

his mind; be trouble with no fear, no scruple concern his

for the sake of my son's affection (though I do prize that highly,
and though I feel it be my right, and know I have do much to earn
it) as for that influence over he which, for his own advantage, I
would strive to purchase and retain, and which for very spite his

egotism, be pleased to win to himself; make no use of it but to
torment I and ruin the child. my only consolation be, that he
spend comparatively little of his time at home, and, during the
month he pass in London or elsewhere, I have a chance of
recover the ground I have lose, and overcome with good the evil
he have work by his wilful mismanagement. but then it be a
bitter trial to behold he, on his return, do his utmost to
subvert my labour and transform my innocent, affectionate,
tractable darling into a selfish, disobedient, and mischievous boy;
thereby prepare the soil for those vice he have so successfully
cultivate in his own perverted nature.
happily, there be none of Arthur's 'friend' invite to Grassdale
last autumn: he take himself off to visit some of they instead. I
wish he would always do so, and I wish his friend be numerous
and love enough to keep he amongst they all the year round. Mr.
Hargrave, considerably to my annoyance, do not go with he; but I
think I have do with that gentleman at last.
for seven or eight month he behave so remarkably well, and
manage so skilfully too, that I be almost completely off my

even to treat he as such, with certain prudent restriction (which

decent moderation and propriety that have so long restrain he.

'what have you do to Walter, Mrs. Huntingdon?' say she one

'I have do nothing willingly to offend he,' say I. 'if he be

perhaps year.'

'yes; I want to ask you - '

'Esther, how can you be so rude!' cry Mrs. Hargrave, who be

'well, mamma, you say yourself - ' but the young lady be

'benot she cross?' whisper she to I; but, before I could add my

'here, Esther, Ihave bring you the rose,' say he, extend it
towards she.
'give it her yourself, you blockhead!' cry she, recoil with a

'Mrs. Huntingdon would rather receive it from you,' reply he, in

'my brother's compliment, Mrs. Huntingdon, and he hop you and he

'now, Esther,' interpose Mrs. Hargrave, who, if equally benighted

about a week after Mr. Hargrave bring his sister to see I. he

'no; but itbe no matter,' I answer carelessly; and if my cheek

'you donot object to it?' he say.
'not at all, if Lord Lowborough like his company.'
'you have no love leave for he, then?'
'not the least.'
'I know that - I know you be too high-minded and pure in your own
nature to continue to regard one so utterly false and polluted with
any feeling but those of indignation and scornful abhorrence!'
'be he not your friend?' say I, turn my eye from the fire to

'he be,' reply he, with the same calm gravity as before; 'but do

'revenge! no - what good would that do? - it would make he no
well, and I no happy.'

'then, sir, I fear you must be very much bad than you should be,

'no, I be the ordinary mortal, I maintain,' reply Mr. Hargrave.

'as happy as some other, I suppose.'
'be you as happy as you desire to be?'
'no one be so blest as that come to on this side eternity.'
'one thing I know,' return he, with a deep sad sigh; 'you be
immeasurably happy than I be.'
'I be very sorry for you, then,' I could not help reply.

'and so I should if I could do so without injure myself or any

'and can you suppose that I should wish you to injure yourself?
no: on the contrary, it be your own happiness I long for more than

look I boldly in the face. 'you do not complain, but I see -
and feel - and know that you be miserable - and must remain so as

say on either side, Esther and Arthur re-enter the room. the

think it a poor return for Walter's generous concession, and be

she live in!
Mr. Hargrave have not an opportunity of meet I again in private

less of pride and more of touch melancholy in his manner than

that sharp-sight woman soon guess how matter stand between we,
and descry the enemy's movement from her elevation at the

myself for that day to the park and garden, or, if the propose

or afflict, I would take Rachel with I, and then I be never
molest.
but one mild, sunshiny day, early in November, I have venture forth
alone to visit the village school and a few of the poor tenant,

the horse soon overtake I, and be rein up close beside I. it

briefly answer his salutation and inquire after the lady at

companion all the way.
'well! I donot much care. if you want another rebuff, take it -
and welcome,' be my inward remark. 'now, sir, what next?'
this question, though unspoken, be not long unanswered; after a

solemn tone the follow appeal to my humanity:-
'it will be four year next April since I first see you, Mrs.

the follow autumn I see so much of your perfection that I could

'in the first place, I donot believe you,' answer I; 'in the

'if you affect,' reply he, earnestly, 'to regard as folly the

when you find he utterly unworthy of the treasure, you reclaim

miserable; and you coolly tell I it be the will of God that we
should remain so. you may call this religion, but I call it wild

may reap in joy hereafter. it be his will that we should not
injure other by the gratification of our own earthly passion; and

calling and break my faith with heaven to obtain a few brief year

'there need be no disgrace, no misery or sacrifice in any quarter,'

Finding, however, that he could not be silence by reason, and even

course and try another plan.
'do you really love I?' say I, seriously, pause and look he
calmly in the face.
'do I love you!' cry he.
'truly?' I demand.
his countenance brighten; he think his triumph be at hand. he
commence a passionate protestation of the truth and fervour of his
attachment, which I cut short by another question:-
'but be it not a selfish love? have you enough disinterested
affection to enable you to sacrifice your own pleasure to mine?'

'I donot want your life; but have you enough real sympathy for my

'try I, and see.'
'if you have, never mention this subject again. you cannot recur

and what I say I mean. if you torment I in this way any more, I
must conclude that your protestation be entirely false, and that
you hate I in your heart as fervently as you profess to love I!'
he bite his lip, and bent his eye upon the ground in silence for a

must leave you. I cannot live here, and be for ever silent on the

'formerly, I believe, you spend but little of your time at home,' I
answer; 'it will do you no harm to absent yourself again, for a

'if that be really possible,' he mutter; 'and can you bid I go

'most certainly I do. if you cannot see I without torment I
as you have lately do, I would gladly say farewell and never see

he make no answer, but, bend from his horse, hold out his hand
towards I. I look up at his face, and see therein such a look
of genuine agony of soul, that, whether bitter disappointment, or
wound pride, or linger love, or burn wrath be uppermost,
I could not hesitate to put my hand in his as frankly as if I bade
a friend farewell. he grasp it very hard, and immediately put
spur to his horse and gallop away. very soon after, I learn
that he be go to Paris, where he still be; and the long he
stay there the well for I.
I thank God for this deliverance!
CHAPTER XXXVIII
December 20th, 1826. - the fifth anniversary of my wedding-day,
and, I trust, the last I shall spend under this roof. my
resolution be form, my plan concoct, and already partly put in
execution. my conscience do not blame I, but while the purpose
ripen let I beguile a few of these long winter evening in
state the case for my own satisfaction: a dreary amusement
enough, but have the air of a useful occupation, and be
pursue as a task, it will suit I well than a light one.
in September, quiet Grassdale be again alive with a party of
lady and gentleman (so call), consist of the same
individual as those invite the year before last, with the
addition of two or three other, among whom be Mrs. Hargrave and
her young daughter. the gentleman and Lady Lowborough be
invite for the pleasure and convenience of the host; the other
lady, I suppose, for the sake of appearance, and to keep I in
check, and make I discreet and civil in my demeanour. but the
lady stay only three week; the gentleman, with two exception,
above two month: for their hospitable entertainer be loth to
part with they and be leave alone with his bright intellect, his
stainless conscience, and his loved and love wife.
on the day of Lady Lowborough's arrival, I follow she into her
chamber, and plainly tell she that, if I find reason to believe
that she still continue her criminal connection with Mr.
Huntingdon, I should think it my absolute duty to inform she
husband of the circumstance - or awaken his suspicion at least -
however painful it might be, or however dreadful the consequence.
she be startle at first by the declaration, so unexpected, and so
determinately yet calmly deliver; but rally in a moment, she
coolly reply that, if I see anything at all reprehensible or
suspicious in her conduct, she would freely give I leave to tell
his lordship all about it. willing to be satisfied with this, I
leave she; and certainly I see nothing thenceforth particularly
reprehensible or suspicious in her demeanour towards her host; but
then I have the other guest to attend to, and I do not watch they
narrowly - for, to confess the truth, I fear to see anything
between they. I no long regard it as any concern of mine, and
if it be my duty to enlighten Lord Lowborough, it be a painful
duty, and I dread to be call to perform it.
but my fear be bring to an end in a manner I have not
anticipate. one evening, about a fortnight after the visitor'
arrival, I have retire into the library to snatch a few minute'
respite from forced cheerfulness and wearisome discourse, for after
so long a period of seclusion, dreary indeed as I have often find
it, I could not always bear to be do violence to my feeling,
and goad my power to talk, and smile and listen, and play the
attentive hostess, or even the cheerful friend: I have just
ensconce myself within the bow of the window, and be look out
upon the west, where the darken hill rise sharply define
against the clear amber light of evening, that gradually blend
and fade away into the pure, pale blue of the upper sky, where one
bright star be shin through, as if to promise - 'when that
die light be go, the world will not be leave in darkness, and
they who trust in God, whose mind be unbeclouded by the mist of
unbelief and sin, be never wholly comfortless,' - when I hear a
hurry step approach, and Lord Lowborough enter. this room
be still his favourite resort. he fling the door to with unusual
violence, and cast his hat aside regardless where it fall. what
could be the matter with he? his face be ghastly pale; his eye
be fix upon the ground; his tooth clench: his forehead
glistened with the dew of agony. it be plain he know his wrong
at last!
Unconscious of my presence, he begin to pace the room in a state of
fearful agitation, violently wring his hand and uttering low
groan or incoherent ejaculation. I make a movement to let he
know that he be not alone; but he be too preoccupied to notice
it. perhaps, while his back be towards I, I might cross the room
and slip away unobserved. I rise to make the attempt, but then he
perceive I. he start and stand still a moment; then wipe his
streaming forehead, and, advance towards I, with a kind of
unnatural composure, say in a deep, almost sepulchral tone, -
'Mrs. Huntingdon, I must leave you to-morrow.'
'to-morrow!' I repeat. 'I do not ask the cause.'
'you know it then, and you can be so calm!' say he, survey I
with profound astonishment, not unmingled with a kind of resentful
bitterness, as it appear to I.
'I have so long be aware of - ' I pause in time, and add, 'of
my husband's character, that nothing shock I.'
'but this - how long have you be aware of this?' demand he,
lay his clench hand on the table beside he, and look I
keenly and fixedly in the face.
I feel like a criminal.
'not long,' I answer.
'you know it!' cry he, with bitter vehemence - 'and you do not
tell I! you help to deceive I!'
'my lord, I do not help to deceive you.'
'then why do you not tell I?'
'because I know it would be painful to you. I hop she would
return to her duty, and then there would be no need to harrow your
feeling with such - '
'o god! how long have this be go on? how long have it be,
Mrs. Huntingdon? - tell I - I must know!' exclaim, with intense
and fearful eagerness.
'two year, I believe.'
'great heaven! and she have dupe I all this time!' he turn away
with a suppress groan of agony, and pace the room again in a
paroxysm of renew agitation. my heart smite I; but I would try
to console he, though I know not how to attempt it.
'she be a wicked woman,' I say. 'she have basely deceive and
betray you. she be as little worthy of your regret as she be of
your affection. let she injure you no far; abstract yourself
from she, and stand alone.'
'and you, Madam,' say he sternly, arrest himself, and turn
round upon I, 'you have injure I too by this ungenerous
concealment!'
there be a sudden revulsion in my feeling. something rise within
I, and urge I to resent this harsh return for my heartfelt
sympathy, and defend myself with answer severity. happily, I
do not yield to the impulse. I see his anguish as, suddenly
smite his forehead, he turn abruptly to the window, and,
look upward at the placid sky, murmur passionately, 'o God,
that I might die!' - and feel that to add one drop of bitterness to
that already overflow cup would be ungenerous indeed. and yet I
fear there be more coldness than gentleness in the quiet tone of
my reply:- 'I might offer many excuse that some would admit to be
valid, but I will not attempt to enumerate they - '
'I know they,' say he hastily: 'you would say that it be no
business of your: that I ought to have take care of myself; that
if my own blindness have lead I into this pit of hell, I have no
right to blame another for give I credit for a large amount of
sagacity than I possess - '
'I confess I be wrong,' continue I, without regard this bitter
interruption; 'but whether want of courage or mistaken kindness be
the cause of my error, I think you blame I too severely. I tell
Lady Lowborough two week ago, the very hour she come, that I
should certainly think it my duty to inform you if she continue to
deceive you: she give I full liberty to do so if I should see
anything reprehensible or suspicious in her conduct; I have see
nothing; and I trust she have alter her course.'
he continue gaze from the window while I speak, and do not
answer, but, sting by the recollection my word awaken, stamp
his foot upon the floor, grind his tooth, and corrugate his brow,
like one under the influence of acute physical pain.
'it be wrong, it be wrong!' he mutter at length. 'nothing can
excuse it; nothing can atone for it, - for nothing can recall those
year of cursed credulity; nothing obliterate they! - nothing,
nothing!' he repeat in a whisper, whose despair bitterness
preclude all resentment.
'when I put the case to myself, I own it be wrong,' I answer;
'but I can only now regret that I do not see it in this light
before, and that, as you say, nothing can recall the past.'
something in my voice or in the spirit of this answer seem to
alter his mood. turn towards I, and attentively survey my
face by the dim light, he say, in a milder tone than he have yet
employ, - 'you, too, have suffer, I suppose.'
'I suffer much, at first.'
'when be that?'
'two year ago; and two year hence you will be as calm as I be
now, and far, far happy, I trust, for you be a man, and free to
act as you please.'
something like a smile, but a very bitter one, cross his face for
a moment.
'you have not be happy, lately?' he say, with a kind of effort
to regain composure, and a determination to waive the further
discussion of his own calamity.
'happy?' I repeat, almost provoke at such a question. 'could I
be so, with such a husband?'

CHAPTER XXXIX
my great source of uneasiness, in this time of trial, be my
son, whom his father and his father's friend delight to
encourage in all the embryo vice a little child can show, and to
instruct in all the evil habit he could acquire - in a word, to
'make a man of he' be one of their staple amusement; and I need
say no more to justify my alarm on his account, and my
determination to deliver he at any hazard from the hand of such
instructor. I first attempt to keep he always with I, or in

he come down to dessert as long as these 'gentleman' stay; but
it be no use: these order be immediately countermand and
overrule by his father; he be not go to have the little fellow
mop to death between an old nurse and a cursed fool of a mother.
so the little fellow come down every evening in spite of his cross
mamma, and learn to tipple wine like papa, to swear like Mr.
Hattersley, and to have his own way like a man, and send mamma to
the devil when she try to prevent he. to see such thing do
with the roguish naivete of that pretty little child, and hear such
thing speak by that small infantile voice, be as peculiarly
piquant and irresistibly droll to they as it be inexpressibly

roar he would look round delightedly upon they all, and add his
shrill laugh to theirs. but if that beam blue eye rest on I,
its light would vanish for a moment, and he would say, in some
concern, 'Mamma, why donot you laugh? make she laugh, papa - she
never will.'
hence be I oblige to stay among these human brute, watch an

inability to suit their taste?
what should I do then? apply to my brother and explain my

at this they simultaneously burst into a rude laugh that make my
