'where be he? Iwill ask he myself!' say I, advance towards
they.
suppress a new burst of merriment, Hattersley point to the
outer door. it be half open. his brother-in-law be stand on
the front without.
'Mr. Hargrave, will you please to step this way?' say I.
he turn and look at I in grave surprise.
'step this way, if you please!' I repeat, in so determined a
manner that he could not, or do not choose to resist its
authority. somewhat reluctantly he ascend the step and advance
a pace or two into the hall.
'and tell those gentleman,' I continue - 'these man, whether or
not I yield to your solicitation.'
'I donot understand you, Mrs. Huntingdon.'
'you do understand I, sir; and I charge you, upon your honour as a
gentleman (if you have any), to answer truly. do I, or do I
not?'
'no,' mutter he, turn away.
'speak up, sir; they cannot hear you. do I grant your request?
'you do not.'
'no, Iwill be swear she donot,' say Hattersley, 'or hewould never
look so black.'
'Ibe willing to grant you the satisfaction of a gentleman,
Huntingdon,' say Mr. Hargrave, calmly address his host, but
with a bitter sneer upon his countenance.
'go to the deuce!' reply the latter, with an impatient jerk of
the head. Hargrave withdraw with a look of cold disdain, say, -
'you know where to find I, should you feel disposed to send a
friend.'
muttered oath and curse be all the answer this intimation
obtain.
'now, Huntingdon, you see!' say Hattersley. 'clear as the day.'
'I donot care what he see,' say I, 'or what he imagine; but you,
Mr. Hattersley, when you hear my name belie and slander, will
you defend it?'
'I will.'
I instantly depart and shut myself into the library. what could
possess I to make such a request of such a man I cannot tell; but
drown man catch at straw: they have drive I desperate between
they; I hardly know what I say. there be no other to preserve my
name from be blacken and asperse among this nest of boon
companion, and through they, perhaps, into the world; and beside
my abandon wretch of a husband, the base, malignant Grimsby, and
the false villain Hargrave, this boorish ruffian, coarse and brutal
as he be, shine like a glow-worm in the dark, among its fellow
worm.
what a scene be this! could I ever have imagine that I should be
doom to bear such insult under my own roof - to hear such thing
speak in my presence; nay, speak to I and of I; and by those
who arrogate to themselves the name of gentleman? and could I
have imagine that I should have be able to endure it as calmly,
and to repel their insult as firmly and as boldly as I have do?
a hardness such as this be teach by rough experience and despair
alone.
such thought as these chase one another through my mind, as I
pace to and fro the room, and long - oh, how I long - to take
my child and leave they now, without an hour's delay! but it could
not be; there be work before I: hard work, that must be do.
'then let I do it,' say I, 'and lose not a moment in vain
repining and idle chafing against my fate, and those who
influence it.'
and conquer my agitation with a powerful effort, I immediately
resume my task, and labour hard all day.
Mr. Hargrave do depart on the morrow; and I have never see he
since. the other stay on for two or three week long; but I
keep aloof from they as much as possible, and still continue my
labour, and have continue it, with almost unabated ardour, to the
present day. I soon acquaint Rachel with my design, confide
all my motif and intention to her ear, and, much to my agreeable
surprise, find little difficulty in persuade she to enter into
my view. she be a sober, cautious woman, but she so hat she
master, and so love her mistress and her nursling, that after
several ejaculation, a few faint objection, and many tear and
lamentation that I should be bring to such a pass, she applaud
my resolution and consent to aid I with all her might: on one
condition only: that she might share my exile: otherwise, she be
utterly inexorable, regard it as perfect madness for I and
Arthur to go alone. with touching generosity, she modestly offer
to aid I with her little hoard of saving, hop I would 'excuse
she for the liberty, but really, if I would do she the favour to
accept it as a loan, she would be very happy.' of course I could
not think of such a thing; but now, thank heaven, I have gather a
little hoard of my own, and my preparation be so far advanced
that I be look forward to a speedy emancipation. only let the
stormy severity of this winter weather be somewhat abate, and
then, some morning, Mr. Huntingdon will come down to a solitary
breakfast-table, and perhaps be clamour through the house for
his invisible wife and child, when they be some fifty mile on
their way to the western world, or it may be more: for we shall
leave he hour before the dawn, and it be not probable he will
discover the loss of both until the day be far advanced.
I be fully alive to the evil that may and must result upon the
step I be about to take; but I never waver in my resolution,
because I never forget my son. it be only this morning, while I
pursue my usual employment, he be sit at my foot, quietly
play with the shred of canvas I have throw upon the carpet; but
his mind be otherwise occupy, for, in a while, he look up
wistfully in my face, and gravely ask, - 'Mamma, why be you
wicked?'
'who tell you I be wicked, love?'
'Rachel.'
'no, Arthur, Rachel never say so, I be certain.'
'well, then, it be papa,' reply he, thoughtfully. then, after a
reflective pause, he add, 'at least, Iwill tell you how it be I
get to know: when Ibe with papa, if I say mamma want I, or mamma
say Ibe not to do something that he tell I to do, he always
say, "Mamma be damn," and Rachel say itbe only wicked people
that be damned. so, mamma, thatbe why I think you must be wicked:
and I wish you wouldnot.'
'my dear child, I be not. those be bad word, and wicked people
often say they of other well than themselves. those word
cannot make people be damn, nor show that they deserve it. God
will judge we by our own thought and deed, not by what other say
about we. and when you hear such word speak, Arthur, remember
never to repeat they: it be wicked to say such thing of other,
not to have they say against you.'
'then itbe papa thatbe wicked,' say he, ruefully.
'Papa be wrong to say such thing, and you will be very wrong to
imitate he now that you know well.'
'what be imitate?'
'to do as he do.'
'do he know well?'
'perhaps he do; but that be nothing to you.'
'if he donot, you ought to tell he, mamma.'
'I have tell he.'
the little moralist pause and ponder. I try in vain to divert
his mind from the subject.
'Ibe sorry papa's wicked,' say he mournfully, at length, 'for I
donot want he to go to hell.' and so say he burst into tear.
I console he with the hope that perhaps his papa would alter and
become good before he die -; but be it not time to deliver he
from such a parent?
CHAPTER XL
January 10th, 1827. - while write the above, yesterday evening, I
sit in the drawing-room. Mr. Huntingdon be present, but, as I
think, asleep on the sofa behind I. he have rise, however,
unknown to I, and, actuate by some base spirit of curiosity, be
look over my shoulder for I know not how long; for when I have
lay aside my pen, and be about to close the book, he suddenly
place his hand upon it, and say, - 'with your leave, my dear,
Iwill have a look at this,' forcibly wrest it from I, and,
draw a chair to the table, composedly sit down to examine it:
turn back leaf after leaf to find an explanation of what he have
read. unluckily for I, he be more sober that night than he
usually be at such an hour.
of course I do not leave he to pursue this occupation in quiet:
I make several attempt to snatch the book from his hand, but he
hold it too firmly for that; I upbraid he in bitterness and
scorn for his mean and dishonourable conduct, but that have no
effect upon he; and, finally, I extinguish both the candle, but
he only wheel round to the fire, and raise a blaze sufficient
for his purpose, calmly continue the investigation. I have
serious thought of get a pitcher of water and extinguish
that light too; but it be evident his curiosity be too keenly
excited to be quench by that, and the more I manifest my
anxiety to baffle his scrutiny, the great would be his
determination to persist in it besides it be too late.
'it seem very interesting, love,' say he, lift his head and
turn to where I stand, wring my hand in silent rage and
anguish; 'but itbe rather long; Iwill look at it some other time;
and meanwhile Iwill trouble you for your key, my dear.'
'what key?'
'the key of your cabinet, desk, drawer, and whatever else you
possess,' say he, rise and hold out his hand.
'Ihave not get they,' I reply. the key of my desk, in fact, be
at that moment in the lock, and the other be attach to it.
'then you must send for they,' say he; 'and if that old devil,
Rachel, donot immediately deliver they up, she tramp bag and
baggage tomorrow.'
'she donot know where they be,' I answer, quietly place my
hand upon they, and take they from the desk, as I think,
unobserve. 'I know, but I shall not give they up without a
reason.'
'and I know, too,' say he, suddenly seize my closed hand and
rudely abstract they from it. he then take up one of the
candle and relighte it by thrust it into the fire.
'now, then,' sneer he, 'we must have a confiscation of property.
but, first, let we take a peep into the studio.'
and put the key into his pocket, he walk into the library.
I follow, whether with the dim idea of prevent mischief, or
only to know the bad, I can hardly tell. my painting material
be lay together on the corner table, ready for to-morrow's use,
and only cover with a cloth. he soon spy they out, and put
down the candle, deliberately proceed to cast they into the fire:
palette, paint, bladder, pencil, brush, varnish: I see they
all consume: the palette-knife snap in two, the oil and
turpentine send hiss and roar up the chimney. he then ring
the bell.
'Benson, take those thing away,' say he, point to the easel,
canvas, and stretcher; 'and tell the housemaid she may kindle the
fire with they: your mistress willnot want they any more.'
Benson pause aghast and look at I.
'take they away, Benson,' say I; and his master mutter an oath.
'and this and all, sir?' say the astonished servant, refer to
the half-finished picture.
'that and all,' reply the master; and the thing be clear
away.
Mr. Huntingdon then go up-stair. I do not attempt to follow
he, but remain seat in the arm-chair, speechless, tearless,
and almost motionless, till he return about half-an-hour after,
and walk up to I, hold the candle in my face and peer into my
eye with look and laughter too insulting to be bear. with a
sudden stroke of my hand I dash the candle to the floor.
'Hal-lo!' mutter he, start back; 'shebe the very devil for
spite. do ever any mortal see such eye? - they shine in the dark
like a cat's. oh, yoube a sweet one!' so say, he gather up
the candle and the candlestick. the former be break as well as
extinguish, he ring for another.
'Benson, your mistress have break the candle; bring another.'
'you expose yourself finely,' observe I, as the man depart.
'I donot say Iwould break it, do I?' return he. he then throw my
key into my lap, say, - 'there! youwill find nothing go but
your money, and the jewel, and a few little trifle I think it
advisable to take into my own possession, lest your mercantile
spirit should be tempt to turn they into gold. Ihave leave you a
few sovereign in your purse, which I expect to last you through
the month; at all event, when you want more you will be so good as
to give I an account of how thatbe spend. I shall put you upon a
small monthly allowance, in future, for your own private expense;
and you neednot trouble yourself any more about my concern; I
shall look out for a steward, my dear - I willnot expose you to the
temptation. and as for the household matter, Mrs. Greaves must be
very particular in keep her account; we must go upon an
entirely new plan - '
'what great discovery have you make now, Mr. Huntingdon? have I
attempt to defraud you?'
'not in money matter, exactly, it seem; but itbe good to keep out
of the way of temptation.'
here Benson enter with the candle, and there follow a brief
interval of silence; I sit still in my chair, and he stand
with his back to the fire, silently triumph in my despair.
'and so,' say he at length, 'you think to disgrace I, do you,
by run away and turn artist, and support yourself by the
labour of your hand, forsooth? and you think to rob I of my
son, too, and bring he up to be a dirty Yankee tradesman, or a
low, beggarly painter?'
'yes, to obviate his become such a gentleman as his father.'
'itbe well you couldnot keep your own secret - ha, ha! itbe well
these woman must be blab. if they havenot a friend to talk to,
they must whisper their secret to the fish, or write they on the
sand, or something; and itbe well, too, I benot over full to-
night, now I think of it, or I might have snooze away and never
dream of look what my sweet lady be about; or I might have
lack the sense or the power to carry my point like a man, as I
have do.'
leave he to his self-congratulation, I rise to secure my
manuscript, for I now remember it have be leave upon the drawing-
room table, and I determine, if possible, to save myself the
humiliation of see it in his hand again. I could not bear the
idea of his amuse himself over my secret thought and
recollection; though, to be sure, he would find little good of
himself therein indite, except in the former part; and oh, I would
soon burn it all than he should read what I have write when I
be such a fool as to love he!
'and by-the-by,' cry he, as I be leave the room, 'youwould well
tell that d-d old sneak of a nurse to keep out of my way for a day
or two; Iwould pay she her wage and send she packing to-morrow, but I
know shewould do more mischief out of the house than in it.'
and as I depart, he go on curse and abuse my faithful
friend and servant with epithet I will not defile this paper with
repeat. I go to she as soon as I have put away my book, and
tell she how our project be defeat. she be as much distressed
and horrify as I be - and more so than I be that night, for I
be partly stun by the blow, and partly excited and support
against it by the bitterness of my wrath. but in the morning, when
I wake without that cheering hope that have be my secret comfort
and support so long, and all this day, when I have wander about
restless and objectless, shun my husband, shrink even from
my child, know that I be unfit to be his teacher or companion,
hop nothing for his future life, and fervently wish he have
never be bear, - I feel the full extent of my calamity, and I
feel it now. I know that day after day such feeling will return
upon I. I be a slave - a prisoner - but that be nothing; if it
be myself alone I would not complain, but I be forbid to
rescue my son from ruin, and what be once my only consolation be
become the crown source of my despair.
have I no faith in God? I try to look to he and raise my heart to
heaven, but it will cleave to the dust. I can only say, 'he hath
hedge I about, that I cannot get out: he hath make my chain
heavy. he hath fill I with bitterness - he hath make I drunken
with wormwood.' I forget to add, 'but though he cause grief, yet
will he have compassion accord to the multitude of his mercy.
for he doth not afflict willingly nor grieve the child of man.'
I ought to think of this; and if there be nothing but sorrow for I
in this world, what be the long life of misery to a whole
eternity of peace? and for my little Arthur - have he no friend but
I? who be it say, 'it be not the will of your Father which be
in heaven that one of these little one should perish?'
CHAPTER XLI
March 20th. - have now get rid of Mr. Huntingdon for a season, my
spirit begin to revive. he leave I early in February; and the
moment he be go, I breathe again, and feel my vital energy
return; not with the hope of escape - he have take care to leave I
no visible chance of that - but with a determination to make the
good of exist circumstance. here be Arthur leave to I at
last; and rouse from my despondent apathy, I exert all my
power to eradicate the weed that have be foster in his infant
mind, and sow again the good seed they have render unproductive.
thank heaven, it be not a barren or a stony soil; if weed spring
fast there, so do well plant. his apprehension be more quick,
his heart more overflowing with affection than ever his father's
could have be, and it be no hopeless task to bend he to
obedience and win he to love and know his own true friend, as long
as there be no one to counteract my effort.
I have much trouble at first in break he of those evil habit
his father have teach he to acquire, but already that difficulty
be nearly vanquish now: bad language seldom defile his mouth,
and I have succeed in give he an absolute disgust for all
intoxicate liquor, which I hope not even his father or his
father's friend will be able to overcome. he be inordinately
fond of they for so young a creature, and, remember my
unfortunate father as well as his, I dread the consequence of
such a taste. but if I have stint he, in his usual quantity of
wine, or forbid he to taste it altogether, that would only have
increase his partiality for it, and make he regard it as a
great treat than ever. I therefore give he quite as much as his
father be accustom to allow he; as much, indeed, as he desire
to have - but into every glass I surreptitiously introduce a small
quantity of tartar-emetic, just enough to produce inevitable nausea
and depression without positive sickness. find such
disagreeable consequence invariably to result from this
indulgence, he soon grow weary of it, but the more he shrink from
the daily treat the more I press it upon he, till his reluctance
be strengthen to perfect abhorrence. when he be thoroughly
disgust with every kind of wine, I allow he, at his own
request, to try brandy-and-water, and then gin-and-water, for the
little toper be familiar with they all, and I be determined that
all should be equally hateful to he. this I have now effect;
and since he declare that the taste, the smell, the sight of any
one of they be sufficient to make he sick, I have give up tease
he about they, except now and then as object of terror in case
of misbehaviour. 'Arthur, if yoube not a good boy I shall give
you a glass of wine,' or 'now, Arthur, if you say that again you
shall have some brandy-and-water,' be as good as any other threat;
and once or twice, when he be sick, I have oblige the poor child
to swallow a little wine-and-water without the tartar-emetic, by
way of medicine; and this practice I intend to continue for some
time to come; not that I think it of any real service in a physical
sense, but because I be determined to enlist all the power of
association in my service; I wish this aversion to be so deeply
ground in his nature that nothing in after-life may be able to
overcome it.
thus, I flatter myself, I shall secure he from this one vice; and
for the rest, if on his father's return I find reason to apprehend
that my good lesson will be all destroy - if Mr. Huntingdon
commence again the game of teach the child to hate and despise
his mother, and emulate his father's wickedness - I will yet
deliver my son from his hand. I have devise another scheme that
might be resort to in such a case; and if I could but obtain my
brother's consent and assistance, I should not doubt of its
success. the old hall where he and I be bear, and where our
mother die, be not now inhabit, nor yet quite sink into decay,
as I believe. now, if I could persuade he to have one or two
room make habitable, and to let they to I as a stranger, I might
live there, with my child, under an assumed name, and still support
myself by my favourite art. he should lend I the money to begin
with, and I would pay he back, and live in lowly independence and
strict seclusion, for the house stand in a lonely place, and the
neighbourhood be thinly inhabit, and he himself should negotiate
the sale of my picture for I. I have arrange the whole plan in
my head: and all I want be to persuade Frederick to be of the same
mind as myself. he be come to see I soon, and then I will make
the proposal to he, have first enlighten he upon my
circumstance sufficiently to excuse the project.
already, I believe, he know much more of my situation than I have
tell he. I can tell this by the air of tender sadness pervade
his letter; and by the fact of his so seldom mention my
husband, and generally evince a kind of covert bitterness when he
do refer to he; as well as by the circumstance of his never
come to see I when Mr. Huntingdon be at home. but he have never
openly express any disapprobation of he or sympathy for I; he
have never ask any question, or say anything to invite my
confidence. have he do so, I should probably have have but few
concealment from he. perhaps he feel hurt at my reserve. he be
a strange being; I wish we know each other well. he use to
spend a month at Staningley every year, before I be marry; but,
since our father's death, I have only see he once, when he come
for a few day while Mr. Huntingdon be away. he shall stay many
day this time, and there shall be more candour and cordiality
between we than ever there be before, since our early childhood.
my heart cling to he more than ever; and my soul be sick of
solitude.
April 16th. - he be come and go. he would not stay above a
fortnight. the time pass quickly, but very, very happily, and it
have do I good. I must have a bad disposition, for my
misfortune have sour and embitter I exceedingly: I be
begin insensibly to cherish very unamiable feeling against my
fellow-mortal, the male part of they especially; but it be a
comfort to see there be at least one among they worthy to be
trust and esteemed; and doubtless there be more, though I have
never know they, unless I except poor Lord Lowborough, and he be
bad enough in his day. but what would Frederick have be, if he
have live in the world, and mingle from his childhood with such
man as these of my acquaintance? and what will Arthur be, with all
his natural sweetness of disposition, if I do not save he from
that world and those companion? I mention my fear to
Frederick, and introduce the subject of my plan of rescue on the
evening after his arrival, when I present my little son to his
uncle.
'he be like you, Frederick,' say I, 'in some of his mood: I
sometimes think he resemble you more than his father; and I be
glad of it.'
'you flatter I, Helen,' reply he, stroke the child's soft,
wavy lock.
'no, you will think it no compliment when I tell you I would rather
have he to resemble Benson than his father.'
he slightly elevate his eyebrow, but say nothing.
'do you know what sort of man Mr. Huntingdon be?' say I.
'I think I have an idea.'
'have you so clear an idea that you can hear, without surprise or
disapproval, that I meditate escape with that child to some
secret asylum, where we can live in peace, and never see he
again?'
'be it really so?'
'if you have not,' continue I, 'Iwill tell you something more about
he'; and I give a sketch of his general conduct, and a more
particular account of his behaviour with regard to his child, and
explain my apprehension on the latter's account, and my
determination to deliver he from his father's influence.
Frederick be exceedingly indignant against Mr. Huntingdon, and
very much grieve for I; but still he look upon my project as
wild and impracticable. he deem my fear for Arthur
disproportione to the circumstance, and oppose so many
objection to my plan, and devise so many milder method for
ameliorate my condition, that I be oblige to enter into further
detail to convince he that my husband be utterly incorrigible,
and that nothing could persuade he to give up his son, whatever
become of I, he be as fully determine the child should not
leave he, as I be not to leave the child; and that, in fact,
nothing would answer but this, unless I flee the country, as I have
intend before. to obviate that, he at length consent to have
one wing of the old hall put into a habitable condition, as a place
of refuge against a time of need; but hop I would not take
advantage of it unless circumstance should render it really
necessary, which I be ready enough to promise: for though, for my
own sake, such a hermitage appear like paradise itself, compare
with my present situation, yet for my friend' sake, for Milicent
and Esther, my sister in heart and affection, for the poor tenant
of Grassdale, and, above all, for my aunt, I will stay if I
possibly can.
July 29th. - Mrs. Hargrave and her daughter be come back from
London. Esther be full of her first season in town; but she be
still heart-whole and unengaged. her mother seek out an
excellent match for she, and even bring the gentleman to lay his
heart and fortune at her foot; but Esther have the audacity to
refuse the noble gift. he be a man of good family and large
possession, but the naughty girl maintain he be old as Adam,
ugly as sin, and hateful as - one who shall be nameless.
'but, indeed, I have a hard time of it,' say she: 'mamma be very
greatly disappointed at the failure of her darling project, and
very, very angry at my obstinate resistance to her will, and be so
still; but I cannot help it. and Walter, too, be so seriously
displeased at my perversity and absurd caprice, as he call it,
that I fear he will never forgive I - I do not think he could be
so unkind as he have lately show himself. but Milicent beg I
not to yield, and Ibe sure, Mrs. Huntingdon, if you have see the
man they want to palm upon I, you would have advise I not to
take he too.'
'I should have do so whether I have see he or not,' say I; 'it
be enough that you dislike he.'
'I know you would say so; though mamma affirm you would be quite
shocked at my undutiful conduct. you cannot imagine how she
lecture I: I be disobedient and ungrateful; I be thwart her
wish, wrong my brother, and make myself a burden on her
hand. I sometimes fear shewill overcome I after all. I have a
strong will, but so have she, and when she say such bitter thing,
it provoke I to such a pass that I feel inclined to do as she
bid I, and then break my heart and say, "there, mamma, itbe all
your fault!"'
'pray donot!' say I. 'obedience from such a motive would be
positive wickedness, and certain to bring the punishment it
deserve. stand firm, and your mamma will soon relinquish her
persecution; and the gentleman himself will cease to pester you
with his address if he find they steadily reject.'
'oh, no! mamma will weary all about she before she tire herself
with her exertion; and as for Mr. Oldfield, she have give he to
understand that I have refuse his offer, not from any dislike of
his person, but merely because I be giddy and young, and cannot at
present reconcile myself to the thought of marriage under any
circumstance: but by next season, she have no doubt, I shall have
more sense, and hop my girlish fancy will be wear away. so she
have bring I home, to school I into a proper sense of my duty,
against the time come round again. indeed, I believe she will not
put herself to the expense of take I up to London again, unless
I surrender: she cannot afford to take I to town for pleasure and
nonsense, she say, and it be not every rich gentleman that will
consent to take I without a fortune, whatever exalt idea I may
have of my own attraction.'
'well, Esther, I pity you; but still, I repeat, stand firm. you
might as well sell yourself to slavery at once, as marry a man you
dislike. if your mother and brother be unkind to you, you may
leave they, but remember you be bind to your husband for life.'
'but I cannot leave they unless I get marry, and I cannot get
married if nobody see I. I see one or two gentleman in London
that I might have like, but they be young son, and mamma
would not let I get to know they - one especially, who I believe
rather like I - but she throw every possible obstacle in the way
of our well acquaintance. benot it provoke?'
'I have no doubt you would feel it so, but it be possible that if
you marry he, you might have more reason to regret it hereafter
than if you marry Mr. Oldfield. when I tell you not to marry
without love, I do not advise you to marry for love alone: there
be many, many other thing to be consider. keep both heart and
hand in your own possession, till you see good reason to part with
they; and if such an occasion should never present itself, comfort
your mind with this reflection, that though in single life your
joy may not be very many, your sorrow, at least, will not be more
than you can bear. marriage may change your circumstance for the
well, but, in my private opinion, it be far more likely to
produce a contrary result.'
'so think Milicent; but allow I to say I think otherwise. if I
think myself doom to old-maidenhood, I should cease to value my
life. the thought of live on, year after year, at the Grove - a
hanger-on upon mamma and Walter, a mere cumberer of the ground (now
that I know in what light they would regard it), be perfectly
intolerable; I would rather run away with the butler.'
'your circumstance be peculiar, I allow; but have patience, love;
do nothing rashly. remember you be not yet nineteen, and many
year be yet to pass before any one can set you down as an old
maid: you cannot tell what Providence may have in store for you.
and meantime, remember you have a right to the protection and
support of your mother and brother, however they may seem to grudge
it.'
'you be so grave, Mrs. Huntingdon,' say Esther, after a pause.
'when Milicent utter the same discouraging sentiment concern
marriage, I ask if she be happy: she say she be; but I only
half believe she; and now I must put the same question to you.'
'it be a very impertinent question,' laugh I, 'from a young girl
to a married woman so many year her senior, and I shall not answer
it.'
'pardon I, dear madam,' say she, laughingly throw herself into
my arm, and kiss I with playful affection; but I feel a tear
on my neck, as she drop her head on my bosom and continue, with
an odd mixture of sadness and levity, timidity and audacity, - 'I
know you be not so happy as I mean to be, for you spend half your
life alone at Grassdale, while Mr. Huntingdon go about enjoy
himself where and how he please. I shall expect my husband to
have no pleasure but what he share with I; and if his great
pleasure of all be not the enjoyment of my company, why, it will be
the bad for he, thatbe all.'
'if such be your expectation of matrimony, Esther, you must,
indeed, be careful whom you marry - or rather, you must avoid it
altogether.'
CHAPTER XLII
September 1st. - no Mr. Huntingdon yet. perhaps he will stay among
his friend till Christmas; and then, next spring, he will be off
again. if he continue this plan, I shall be able to stay at
Grassdale well enough - that is, I shall be able to stay, and that
be enough; even an occasional bevy of friend at the shooting
season may be bear, if Arthur get so firmly attach to I, so
well establish in good sense and principle before they come that
I shall be able, by reason and affection, to keep he pure from
their contamination. vain hope, I fear! but still, till such a
time of trial come I will forbear to think of my quiet asylum in
the beloved old hall.
Mr. and Mrs. Hattersley have be stay at the Grove a fortnight:
and as Mr. Hargrave be still absent, and the weather be remarkably
fine, I never pass a day without see my two friend, Milicent
and Esther, either there or here. on one occasion, when Mr.
Hattersley have drive they over to Grassdale in the phaeton, with
little Helen and Ralph, and we be all enjoy ourselves in the
garden - I have a few minute' conversation with that gentleman,
while the lady be amuse themselves with the child.
'do you want to hear anything of your husband, Mrs. Huntingdon?'
say he.
'no, unless you can tell I when to expect he home.'
'I cannot. - you donot want he, do you?' say he, with a broad
grin.
'no.'
'well, I think yoube well without he, sure enough - for my
part, Ibe downright weary of he. I tell he Iwould leave he if he
donot mend his manner, and he wouldnot; so I leave he. you see,
Ibe a well man than you think I; and, whatbe more, I have
serious thought of wash my hand of he entirely, and the whole
set of them, and comport myself from this day forward with all
decency and sobriety, as a Christian and the father of a family
should do. what do you think of that?'
'it be a resolution you ought to have form long ago.'
'well, Ibe not thirty yet; it benot too late, be it?'
'no; it be never too late to reform, as long as you have the sense
to desire it, and the strength to execute your purpose.'
'well, to tell you the truth, Ihave think of it often and often
before; but hebe such devilish good company, be Huntingdon, after
all. you cannot imagine what a jovial good fellow he be when hebe
not fairly drunk, only just prim or half-seas-over. we all have
a bit of a liking for he at the bottom of our heart, though we
cannot respect he.'
'but should you wish yourself to be like he?'
'no, Iwould rather be like myself, bad as I be.'
'you cannot continue as bad as you be without get bad and
more brutalised every day, and therefore more like he.'
I could not help smile at the comical, half-angry, half-
confound look he put on at this rather unusual mode of address.
'never mind my plain speaking,' say I; 'it be from the good of
motif. but tell I, should you wish your son to be like Mr.
Huntingdon - or even like yourself?'
'hang it! no.'
'should you wish your daughter to despise you - or, at least, to
feel no vestige of respect for you, and no affection but what be
mingle with the bitter regret?'
'oh, no! I couldnot stand that.'
'and, finally, should you wish your wife to be ready to sink into
the earth when she hear you mention; and to loathe the very
sound of your voice, and shudder at your approach?'
'she never will; she like I all the same, whatever I do.'
'impossible, Mr. Hattersley! you mistake her quiet submission for
affection.'
'fire and fury - '
'now donot burst into a tempest at that. I donot mean to say she
do not love you - she do, I know, a great deal well than you
deserve; but I be quite sure, that if you behave well, she will
love you more, and if you behave bad, she will love you less and
less, till all be lose in fear, aversion, and bitterness of soul,
if not in secret hatred and contempt. but, drop the subject of
affection, should you wish to be the tyrant of her life - to take
away all the sunshine from her existence, and make she thoroughly
miserable?'
'of course not; and I donot, and Ibe not go to.'
'you have do more towards it than you suppose.'
'Pooh, pooh! shebe not the susceptible, anxious, worrite creature
you imagine: shebe a little meek, peaceable, affectionate body;
apt to be rather sulky at time, but quiet and cool in the main,
and ready to take thing as they come.'
'think of what she be five year ago, when you marry she, and
what she be now.'
'I know she be a little plump lassie then, with a pretty pink and
white face: now shebe a poor little bit of a creature, fade and
melt away like a snow-wreath. but hang it! - thatbe not my
fault.'
'what be the cause of it then? not year, for shebe only five-and-
twenty.'
'itbe her own delicate health, and confound it, madam! what would
you make of I? - and the child, to be sure, that worry she to
death between they.'
'no, Mr. Hattersley, the child give she more pleasure than pain:
they be fine, well-dispositione child - '
'I know they be - bless they!'
'then why lie the blame on they? - Iwill tell you what it be: itbe
silent fretting and constant anxiety on your account, mingle, I
suspect, with something of bodily fear on her own. when you behave
well, she can only rejoice with trembling; she have no security, no
confidence in your judgment or principle; but be continually
dread the close of such short-live felicity; when you behave
ill, her cause of terror and misery be more than any one can tell
but herself. in patient endurance of evil, she forget it be our
duty to admonish our neighbour of their transgression. since you
will mistake her silence for indifference, come with I, and Iwill
show you one or two of her letter - no breach of confidence, I
hope, since you be her other half.'
he follow I into the library. I seek out and put into his
hand two of Milicent's letter: one date from London, and
write during one of his wild season of reckless dissipation;
the other in the country, during a lucid interval. the former be
full of trouble and anguish; not accuse he, but deeply
regret his connection with his profligate companion, abuse
Mr. Grimsby and other, insinuate bitter thing against Mr.
Huntingdon, and most ingeniously throw the blame of her
husband's misconduct on to other man's shoulder. the latter be
full of hope and joy, yet with a trembling consciousness that this
happiness would not last; praise his goodness to the sky, but
with an evident, though but half-express wish, that it be base
on a surer foundation than the natural impulse of the heart, and a
half-prophetic dread of the fall of that house so found on the
sand, - which fall have shortly after take place, as Hattersley
must have be conscious while he read.
almost at the commencement of the first letter I have the unexpected
pleasure of see he blush; but he immediately turn his back to
I, and finish the perusal at the window. at the second, I see
he, once or twice, raise his hand, and hurriedly pass it across
his face. could it be to dash away a tear? when he have do,
there be an interval spend in clear his throat and star out
of the window, and then, after whistle a few bar of a favourite
air, he turn round, give I back the letter, and silently shake
I by the hand.
'Ihave be a cursed rascal, God know,' say he, as he give it a
hearty squeeze, 'but you see if I donot make amend for it - d-n I
if I donot!'
'donot curse yourself, Mr. Hattersley; if God have hear half your
invocation of that kind, you would have be in hell long before
now - and you cannot make amend for the past by do your duty
for the future, inasmuch as your duty be only what you owe to your
Maker, and you cannot do more than fulfil it: another must make
amend for your past delinquency. if you intend to reform,
invoke God's blessing, his mercy, and his aid; not his curse.'
'God help I, then - for Ibe sure I need it. where's Milicent?'
'shebe there, just come in with her sister.'
he step out at the glass door, and go to meet they. I
follow at a little distance. somewhat to his wife's
astonishment, he lift she off from the ground, and salute she
with a hearty kiss and a strong embrace; then place his two hand
on her shoulder, he give she, I suppose, a sketch of the great
thing he mean to do, for she suddenly throw her arm round he,
and burst into tear, exclaim, - 'do, do, Ralph - we shall be so
happy! how very, very good you be!'
'Nay, not I,' say he, turn her round, and push she towards
I. 'thank she; itbe her do.'
Milicent fly to thank I, overflow with gratitude. I
disclaim all title to it, tell she her husband be predispose
to amendment before I add my mite of exhortation and
encouragement, and that I have only do what she might, and ought
to have do herself.
'oh, no!' cry she; 'I couldnot have influence he, Ibe sure, by
anything that I could have say. I should only have bother he
by my clumsy effort at persuasion, if I have make the attempt.'
'you never try I, Milly,' say he.
shortly after they take their leave. they be now go on a visit
to Hattersley's father. after that they will repair to their
country home. I hope his good resolution will not fall through,
and poor Milicent will not be again disappointed. her last letter
be full of present bliss, and please anticipation for the
future; but no particular temptation have yet occur to put his
virtue to the test. henceforth, however, she will doubtless be
somewhat less timid and reserve, and he more kind and thoughtful.
- surely, then, her hope be not unfounded; and I have one bright
spot, at least, whereon to rest my thought.
CHAPTER XLIII
October 10th. - Mr. Huntingdon return about three week ago. his
appearance, his demeanour and conversation, and my feeling with
regard to he, I shall not trouble myself to describe. the day
after his arrival, however, he surprise I by the announcement of
an intention to procure a governess for little Arthur: I tell he
it be quite unnecessary, not to say ridiculous, at the present
season: I think I be fully competent to the task of teach
he myself - for some year to come, at least: the child's
education be the only pleasure and business of my life; and since
he have deprive I of every other occupation, he might surely leave
I that.
he say I be not fit to teach child, or to be with they: I have
already reduce the boy to little well than an automaton; I have
break his fine spirit with my rigid severity; and I should freeze
all the sunshine out of his heart, and make he as gloomy an
ascetic as myself, if I have the handling of he much long. and
poor Rachel, too, come in for her share of abuse, as usual; he
cannot endure Rachel, because he know she have a proper
appreciation of he.
I calmly defend our several qualification as nurse and
governess, and still resist the propose addition to our family;
but he cut I short by say it be no use bother about the
matter, for he have engage a governess already, and she be come
next week; so that all I have to do be to get thing ready for her
reception. this be a rather startling piece of intelligence. I
venture to inquire her name and address, by whom she have be
recommend, or how he have be lead to make choice of she.
'she be a very estimable, pious young person,' say he; 'you
neednot be afraid. her name be Myers, I believe; and she be
recommend to I by a respectable old dowager: a lady of high
repute in the religious world. I have not see she myself, and
therefore cannot give you a particular account of her person and
conversation, and so forth; but, if the old lady's eulogy be
correct, you will find she to possess all desirable qualification
for her position: an inordinate love of child among the rest.'
all this be gravely and quietly speak, but there be a laugh
demon in his half-avert eye that bode no good, I imagine.
however, I think of my asylum in -shire, and make no further
objection.
when Miss Myers arrive, I be not prepared to give she a very
cordial reception. her appearance be not particularly calculate
to produce a favourable impression at first sight, nor do she
manner and subsequent conduct, in any degree, remove the prejudice
I have already conceive against she. her attainment be limited,
her intellect noway above mediocrity. she have a fine voice, and
could sing like a nightingale, and accompany herself sufficiently
well on the piano; but these be her only accomplishment. there
be a look of guile and subtlety in her face, a sound of it in her
voice. she seem afraid of I, and would start if I suddenly
approach she. in her behaviour she be respectful and
complaisant, even to servility: she attempt to flatter and fawn
upon I at first, but I soon check that. her fondness for her
little pupil be overstrain, and I be oblige to remonstrate
with she on the subject of over-indulgence and injudicious praise;
but she could not gain his heart. her piety consist in an
occasional heaving of sigh, and uplifting of eye to the ceiling,
and the utterance of a few cannot phrase. she tell I she be a
clergyman's daughter, and have be leave an orphan from her
childhood, but have have the good fortune to obtain a situation in a
very pious family; and then she speak so gratefully of the kindness
she have experience from its different member, that I reproach
myself for my uncharitable thought and unfriendly conduct, and
relent for a time, but not for long: my cause of dislike be
too rational, my suspicion too well found for that; and I know
it be my duty to watch and scrutinize till those suspicion be
either satisfactorily remove or confirm.
I ask the name and residence of the kind and pious family. she
mention a common name, and an unknown and distant place of abode,
but tell I they be now on the continent, and their present
address be unknown to she. I never see she speak much to Mr.
Huntingdon; but he would frequently look into the school-room to
see how little Arthur get on with his new companion, when I be not
there. in the evening, she sit with we in the drawing-room, and
would sing and play to amuse he or we, as she pretend, and be
very attentive to his want, and watchful to anticipate they,
though she only talk to I; indeed, he be seldom in a condition
to be talk to. have she be other than she be, I should have
feel her presence a great relief to come between we thus, except,
indeed, that I should have be thoroughly ashamed for any decent
person to see he as he often be.
I do not mention my suspicion to Rachel; but she, have
sojourn for half a century in this land of sin and sorrow, have
learn to be suspicious herself. she tell I from the first she
be 'down of that new governess,' and I soon find she watch she
quite as narrowly as I do; and I be glad of it, for I long to
know the truth: the atmosphere of Grassdale seem to stifle I,
and I could only live by think of Wildfell Hall.
at last, one morning, she enter my chamber with such intelligence
that my resolution be take before she have cease to speak. while
she dress I I explain to she my intention and what assistance
I should require from she, and tell she which of my thing she be
to pack up, and what she be to leave behind for herself, as I have
no other mean of recompense she for this sudden dismissal after
her long and faithful service: a circumstance I most deeply
regret, but could not avoid.
'and what will you do, Rachel?' say I; 'will you go home, or seek
another place?'
'I have no home, ma'am, but with you,' she reply; 'and if I leave
you Iwill never go into place again as long as I live.'
'but I cannot afford to live like a lady now,' return I: 'I must
be my own maid and my child's nurse.'
'what signify!' reply she, in some excitement. 'youwill want
somebody to clean and wash, and cook, willnot you? I can do all
that; and never mind the wage: Ihave my bit o' saving yet, and
if you wouldnot take I I should have to find my own board and
lodge out of them somewhere, or else work among stranger: and
itbe what Ibe not use to: so you can please yourself, ma'am.'
her voice quaver as she speak, and the tear stand in her eye.
'I should like it above all thing, Rachel, and Iwould give you such
wage as I could afford: such as I should give to any servant-of-
all-work I might employ: but donot you see I should be drag
you down with I when you have do nothing to deserve it?'
'oh, fiddle!' ejaculate she.
'and, besides, my future way of living will be so widely different
to the past: so different to all you have be accustom to - '
'do you think, ma'am, I cannot bear what my missis can? surely Ibe
not so proud and so dainty as that come to; and my little master,
too, God bless he!'
'but Ibe young, Rachel; I sha'n't mind it; and Arthur be young too:
it will be nothing to he.'
'nor I either: Ibe not so old but what I can stand hard fare and
hard work, if itbe only to help and comfort they as Ihave love like
my own bairn: for all Ibe too old to bide the thought o' leave
them in trouble and danger, and go amongst stranger myself.'
'then you sha'n't, Rachel!' cry I, embrace my faithful friend.
'wewill all go together, and you shall see how the new life suit
you.'
'bless you, honey!' cry she, affectionately return my embrace.
'only let we get shut of this wicked house, and wewill do right
enough, youwill see.'
'so think I,' be my answer; and so that point be settle.
by that morning's post I despatch a few hasty line to Frederick,
beseech he to prepare my asylum for my immediate reception:
for I should probably come to claim it within a day after the
receipt of that note: and tell he, in few word, the cause of
my sudden resolution. I then write three letter of adieu: the
first to Esther Hargrave, in which I tell she that I find it
impossible to stay any long at Grassdale, or to leave my son
under his father's protection; and, as it be of the last
importance that our future abode should be unknown to he and his
acquaintance, I should disclose it to no one but my brother,
through the medium of whom I hop still to correspond with my
friend. I then give she his address, exhort she to write
frequently, reiterate some of my former admonition regard she
own concern, and bid she a fond farewell.
the second be to Milicent; much to the same effect, but a little
more confidential, as befit our long intimacy, and her great
experience and well acquaintance with my circumstance.
the third be to my aunt: a much more difficult and painful
undertaking, and therefore I have leave it to the last; but I must
give she some explanation of that extraordinary step I have take:
and that quickly, for she and my uncle would no doubt hear of it
within a day or two after my disappearance, as it be probable that
Mr. Huntingdon would speedily apply to they to know what be become
of I. at last, however, I tell she I be sensible of my error: I
do not complain of its punishment, and I be sorry to trouble my
friend with its consequence; but in duty to my son I must submit
no long; it be absolutely necessary that he should be deliver
from his father's corrupting influence. I should not disclose my
place of refuge even to she, in order that she and my uncle might
be able, with truth, to deny all knowledge concern it; but any
communication address to I under cover to my brother would be
certain to reach I. I hop she and my uncle would pardon the
step I have take, for if they know all, I be sure they would not
blame I; and I trust they would not afflict themselves on my
account, for if I could only reach my retreat in safety and keep it
unmoleste, I should be very happy, but for the thought of they;
and should be quite content to spend my life in obscurity,
devote myself to the training up of my child, and teach he to
avoid the error of both his parent.
these thing be do yesterday: I have give two whole day to
the preparation for our departure, that Frederick may have more
time to prepare the room, and Rachel to pack up the thing: for
the latter task must be do with the utmost caution and secrecy,
and there be no one but I to assist she. I can help to get the
article together, but I do not understand the art of stow they
into the box, so as to take up the small possible space; and
there be her own thing to do, as well as mine and Arthur's. I
can ill afford to leave anything behind, since I have no money,
except a few guineas in my purse; and besides, as Rachel observe,
whatever I leave would most likely become the property of Miss
Myers, and I should not relish that.
but what trouble I have have throughout these two day, struggle
to appear calm and collect, to meet he and she as usual, when I
be oblige to meet they, and force myself to leave my little
Arthur in her hand for hour together! but I trust these trial
be over now: I have lay he in my bed for well security, and
never more, I trust, shall his innocent lip be defile by their
contaminating kiss, or his young ear pollute by their word.
but shall we escape in safety? oh, that the morning be come, and
we be on our way at least! this evening, when I have give Rachel
all the assistance I could, and have nothing leave I but to wait,
and wish and tremble, I become so greatly agitated that I know not
what to do. I go down to dinner, but I could not force myself to
eat. Mr. Huntingdon remark the circumstance.
'whatbe to do with you now?' say he, when the removal of the
second course give he time to look about he.
'I be not well,' I reply: 'I think I must lie down a little; you
willnot miss I much?'
'not the least: if you leave your chair, itwill do just as well -
well, a trifle,' he mutter, as I leave the room, 'for I can
fancy somebody else fill it.'
'somebody else may fill it to-morrow,' I think, but do not say.
'there! Ihave see the last of you, I hope,' I mutter, as I
close the door upon he.
Rachel urge I to seek repose at once, to recruit my strength for
to-morrow's journey, as we must be go before the dawn; but in my
present state of nervous excitement that be entirely out of the
question. it be equally out of the question to sit, or wander
about my room, count the hour and the minute between I and
the appoint time of action, strain my ear and tremble at
every sound, lest someone should discover and betray we after all.
I take up a book and try to read: my eye wander over the
page, but it be impossible to bind my thought to their content.
why not have recourse to the old expedient, and add this last event
to my chronicle? I open its page once more, and write the above
account - with difficulty, at first, but gradually my mind become
more calm and steady. thus several hour have pass away: the
time be draw near; and now my eye feel heavy and my frame
exhaust. I will commend my cause to God, and then lie down and
gain an hour or two of sleep; and then! -
Little Arthur sleep soundly. all the house be still: there can
be no one watch. the box be all cord by Benson, and
quietly convey down the back stair after dusk, and send away in
a cart to the M- coach-office. the name upon the card be Mrs.
Graham, which appellation I mean henceforth to adopt. my mother's
maiden name be Graham, and therefore I fancy I have some claim to
it, and prefer it to any other, except my own, which I dare not
resume.
CHAPTER XLIV
October 24th. - thank heaven, I be free and safe at last. early we
rise, swiftly and quietly dress, slowly and stealthily descend
to the hall, where Benson stand ready with a light, to open the
door and fasten it after we. we be oblige to let one man into
our secret on account of the box, &c. all the servant be but
too well acquainted with their master's conduct, and either Benson
or John would have be willing to serve I; but as the former be
more staid and elderly, and a crony of Rachel's besides, I of
course direct she to make choice of he as her assistant and
confidant on the occasion, as far as necessity demand, I only
hope he may not be bring into trouble thereby, and only wish I
could reward he for the perilous service he be so ready to
undertake. I slip two guineas into his hand, by way of
remembrance, as he stand in the doorway, hold the candle to
light our departure, with a tear in his honest grey eye, and a host
of good wish depict on his solemn countenance. alas! I could
offer no more: I have barely sufficient remain for the probable
expense of the journey.
what tremble joy it be when the little wicket close behind we,
as we issue from the park! then, for one moment, I pause, to
inhale one draught of that cool, brace air, and venture one look
back upon the house. all be dark and still: no light glimmer
in the window, no wreath of smoke obscure the star that sparkle
above it in the frosty sky. as I bade farewell for ever to that
place, the scene of so much guilt and misery, I feel glad that I
have not leave it before, for now there be no doubt about the
propriety of such a step - no shadow of remorse for he I leave
behind. there be nothing to disturb my joy but the fear of
detection; and every step remove we far from the chance of
that.
we have leave Grassdale many mile behind we before the round red sun
arise to welcome our deliverance; and if any inhabitant of its
vicinity have chance to see we then, as we bowl along on the top
of the coach, I scarcely think they would have suspect our
identity. as I intend to be take for a widow, I think it
advisable to enter my new abode in mourning: I be, therefore,
attire in a plain black silk dress and mantle, a black veil (which
I keep carefully over my face for the first twenty or thirty mile
of the journey), and a black silk bonnet, which I have be
constrain to borrow of Rachel, for want of such an article
myself. it be not in the new fashion, of course; but none the
bad for that, under present circumstance. Arthur be clothe in
his plain clothe, and wrap in a coarse woollen shawl; and
Rachel be muffle in a grey cloak and hood that have see well
day, and give she more the appearance of an ordinary though decent
old woman, than of a lady's-maid.
oh, what delight it be to be thus seat aloft, rumble along the
broad, sunshiny road, with the fresh morning breeze in my face,
surround by an unknown country, all smile - cheerfully,
gloriously smile in the yellow lustre of those early beam; with
my darling child in my arm, almost as happy as myself, and my
faithful friend beside I: a prison and despair behind I,
recede far, far back at every clatter of the horse'
foot; and liberty and hope before! I could hardly refrain from
praise God aloud for my deliverance, or astonish my fellow-
passenger by some surprising outburst of hilarity.
but the journey be a very long one, and we be all weary enough
before the close of it. it be far into the night when we reach
the town of L-, and still we be seven mile from our journey's
end; and there be no more coaching, nor any conveyance to be have,
except a common cart, and that with the great difficulty, for
half the town be in bed. and a dreary ride we have of it, that
last stage of the journey, cold and weary as we be; sit on
our box, with nothing to cling to, nothing to lean against,
slowly drag and cruelly shake over the rough, hilly road. but
Arthur be asleep in Rachel's lap, and between we we manage pretty
well to shield he from the cold night air.
at last we begin to ascend a terribly steep and stony lane, which,
in spite of the darkness, Rachel say she remember well: she have
often walk there with I in her arm, and little think to come
again so many year after, under such circumstance as the present.

CHAPTER XLV
Well, Halford, what do you think of all this? and while you read
it, do you ever picture to yourself what my feeling would

themselves near the fire, where that idle lad Fergus be stand,
lean his shoulder against the corner of the chimney-piece, with
his leg cross and his hand in his breech-pocket.
'now, Rose, Iwill tell you a piece of news - I hope you have not
hear it before: for good, bad, or indifferent, one always like
to be the first to tell. itbe about that sad Mrs. Graham - '
'hush-sh-sh!' whisper Fergus, in a tone of solemn import. '"we
never mention she; her name be never hear."' and glance up, I
catch he with his eye askance on I, and his finger point to
his forehead; then, wink at the young lady with a doleful shake

all right but that.'
'I should be sorry to injure any one's feeling,' return she,
speak below her breath. 'another time, perhaps.'
'speak out, Miss Eliza!' say I, not deign to notice the other's
buffoonery: 'you neednot fear to say anything in my presence.'
'well,' answer she, 'perhaps you know already that Mrs. Graham's
husband be not really dead, and that she have run away from he?' I
start, and feel my face glow; but I bent it over my letter, and
go on fold it up as she proceed. 'but perhaps you do not
know that she be now go back to he again, and that a perfect
reconciliation have take place between they? only think,' she
continue, turn to the confounded Rose, 'what a fool the man
must be!'
'and who give you this piece of intelligence, Miss Eliza?' say I,
interrupt my sister's exclamation.
'I have it from a very authentic source.'
'from whom, may I ask?'
'from one of the servant at Woodford.'
'oh! I be not aware that you be on such intimate term with Mr.
Lawrence's household.'
'it be not from the man himself that I hear it, but he tell it in
confidence to our maid Sarah, and Sarah tell it to I.'
'in confidence, I suppose? and you tell it in confidence to we?
but I can tell you that it be but a lame story after all, and
scarcely one-half of it true.'
while I speak I complete the sealing and direction of my letter,
with a somewhat unsteady hand, in spite of all my effort to retain
composure, and in spite of my firm conviction that the story be a
lame one - that the suppose Mrs. Graham, most certainly, have not
voluntarily go back to her husband, or dream of a
reconciliation. most likely she be go away, and the tale-
bear servant, not know what be become of she, have
conjecture that such be the case, and our fair visitor have
detail it as a certainty, delight with such an opportunity of
torment I. but it be possible - barely possible - that some

determine to know the bad, I hastily pocket my two letter,
and mutter something about be too late for the post, leave the
room, rush into the yard, and vociferously call for my horse.
no one be there, I drag he out of the stable myself,
strap the saddle on to his back and the bridle on to his head,
mount, and speedily gallop away to Woodford. I find its owner
pensively stroll in the ground.
'be your sister go?' be my first word as I grasp his hand,
instead of the usual inquiry after his health.
'yes, shebe go,' be his answer, so calmly speak that my terror
be at once remove.
'I suppose I maynot know where she be?' say I, as I dismount,
and relinquish my horse to the gardener, who, be the only
servant within call, have be summon by his master, from his
employment of rake up the dead leaf on the lawn, to take he to
the stable.
my companion gravely take my arm, and lead I away to the
garden, thus answer my question, - 'she be at Grassdale Manor, in
-shire.'
'where?' cry I, with a convulsive start.
'at Grassdale Manor.'
'how be it?' I gasp. 'who betray she?'
'she go of her own accord.'
'Impossible, Lawrence! she could not be so frantic!' exclaim I,
vehemently grasp his arm, as if to force he to unsay those
hateful word.
'she do,' persist he in the same grave, collect manner as
before; 'and not without reason,' he continue, gently disengage
himself from my grasp. 'Mr. Huntingdon be ill.'
'and so she go to nurse he?'
'yes.'
'Fool!' I could not help exclaim, and Lawrence look up with a
rather reproachful glance. 'be he die, then?'
'I think not, Markham.'
'and how many more nurse have he? how many lady be there
besides to take care of he?'
'none; he be alone, or she would not have go.'
'oh, confound it! this be intolerable!'
'what be? that he should be alone?'
I attempt no reply, for I be not sure that this circumstance do
not partly conduce to my distraction. I therefore continue to
pace the walk in silent anguish, with my hand press to my
forehead; then suddenly pause and turn to my companion, I
impatiently exclaim, 'why do she take this infatuated step?
what fiend persuade she to it?'
'nothing persuade she but her own sense of duty.'
'Humbug!'
'I be half inclined to say so myself, Markham, at first. I assure
you it be not by my advice that she go, for I det that man as
fervently as you can do, - except, indeed, that his reformation
would give I much great pleasure than his death; but all I do
be to inform she of the circumstance of his illness (the
consequence of a fall from his horse in hunting), and to tell she
that that unhappy person, Miss Myers, have leave he some time ago.'
'it be ill do! now, when he find the convenience of her

promise for the future, and she will believe he, and then she
condition will be ten time bad and ten time more irremediable
than before.'
'there do not appear to be much ground for such apprehension at

it be her writing! by an irresistible impulse I hold out my hand,
and the word, 'let I see it,' involuntarily pass my lip. he
be evidently reluctant to grant the request, but while he
hesitate I snatch it from his hand. recollect myself,
however, the minute after, I offer to restore it.
'here, take it,' say I, 'if you donot want I to read it.'
'no,' reply he, 'you may read it if you like.'
I read it, and so may you.
Grassdale, Nov. 4th.
Dear Frederick, - I know you will be anxious to hear from I, and I
will tell you all I can. Mr. Huntingdon be very ill, but not
die, or in any immediate danger; and he be rather well at
present than he be when I come. I find the house in sad
confusion: Mrs. Greaves, Benson, every decent servant have leave,
and those that be come to supply their place be a negligent,
disorderly set, to say no bad - I must change they again, if I
stay. a professional nurse, a grim, hard old woman, have be hire
to attend the wretched invalid. he suffer much, and have no
fortitude to bear he through. the immediate injury he sustain
from the accident, however, be not very severe, and would, as the
doctor say, have be but trifle to a man of temperate habit,
but with he it be very different. on the night of my arrival,
when I first enter his room, he be lie in a kind of half
delirium. he do not notice I till I speak, and then he mistake
I for another.
'be it you, Alice, come again?' he murmur. 'what do you leave
I for?'
'it be I, Arthur - it be Helen, your wife,' I reply.
'my wife!' say he, with a start. 'for heaven's sake, donot
mention she - I have none. Devil take she,' he cry, a moment
after, 'and you, too! what do you do it for?'
I say no more; but observe that he keep gaze towards the foot
of the bed, I go and sit there, place the light so as to shine
full upon I, for I think he might be die, and I want he to
know I. for a long time he lie silently look upon I, first
with a vacant stare, then with a fix gaze of strange grow
intensity. at last he startle I by suddenly raise himself on
his elbow and demand in a horrified whisper, with his eye still
fix upon I, 'who be it?'
'it be Helen Huntingdon,' say I, quietly rise at the same time,
and remove to a less conspicuous position.
'I must be go mad,' cry he, 'or something - delirious,
perhaps; but leave I, whoever you be. I cannot bear that white
face, and those eye. for God's sake go, and send I somebody else
that donot look like that!'
I go at once, and send the hire nurse; but next morning I
venture to enter his chamber again, and, take the nurse's place
by his bedside, I watch he and wait on he for several hour,
show myself as little as possible, and only speak when
necessary, and then not above my breath. at first he address I
as the nurse, but, on my crossing the room to draw up the window-
blind, in obedience to his direction, he say, 'no, it benot
nurse; itbe Alice. stay with I, do! that old hag will be the
death of I.'
'I mean to stay with you,' say i. and after that he would call I
Alice, or some other name almost equally repugnant to my feeling.
I force myself to endure it for a while, fear a contradiction
might disturb he too much; but when, have ask for a glass of
water, while I hold it to his lip, he murmur, 'thank, dearest!'
I could not help distinctly observe, 'you would not say so if you
know I,' intend to follow that up with another declaration of
my identity; but he merely mutter an incoherent reply, so I
drop it again, till some time after, when, as I be bath his
forehead and temple with vinegar and water to relieve the heat and

they, and they willnot let I rest; and the most singular and
pertinacious of they all be your face and voice - they seem just
like her. I could swear at this moment that she be by my side.'
'she be,' say I.
'that seem comfortable,' continue he, without notice my word;
'and while you do it, the other fancy fade away - but this only
strengthen. - go on - go on, till it vanish, too. I cannot stand
such a mania as this; it would kill I!'
'it never will vanish,' say I, distinctly, 'for it be the truth!'
'the truth!' he cry, start, as if an asp have sting he. 'you
donot mean to say that you be really she?'
'I do; but you neednot shrink away from I, as if I be your
great enemy: I be come to take care of you, and do what none of
they would do.'
'for God's sake, donot torment I now!' cry he in pitiable
agitation; and then he begin to mutter bitter curse against I, or
the evil fortune that have bring I there; while I put down the
sponge and basin, and resume my seat at the bed-side.
'where be they?' say he: 'have they all leave I - servant and
all?'
'there be servant within call if you want they; but you have
well lie down now and be quiet: none of they could or would
attend you as carefully as I shall do.'
'I cannot understand it at all,' say he, in bewildered perplexity.
'be it a dream that - ' and he cover his eye with his hand, as
if try to unravel the mystery.
'no, Arthur, it be not a dream, that your conduct be such as to

'he be safe.'

'no - '

it,' say I. upon which he say he would try; but then he could

find I inexorable, he at length manage to ratify the
agreement; and I bade Rachel send the boy.
all this may strike you as harsh, but I feel I must not lose my
present advantage, and my son's future welfare should not be
sacrifice to any mistaken tenderness for this man's feeling.
Little Arthur have not forget his father, but thirteen month of

'do you know I?' ask Mr. Huntingdon, intently peruse his
feature.
'yes.'
'who be I?'
'papa.'
'be you glad to see I?'
'yes.'
'yoube not!' reply the disappointed parent, relax his hold,
and dart a vindictive glance at I.
Arthur, thus release, creep back to I and put his hand in mine.

the invalid only reply by groan aloud, and roll his head on

'I be in hell, already!' cry he. 'this curse thirst be burn

before he could finish the sentence I have pour out a glass of

not notice this speech, I ask if there be anything else I

next morning the doctor order he to be bleed; and after that he

'it be well for I that I be do my duty,' say I, with a
bitterness I could not repress, 'for it be the only comfort I have;
and the satisfaction of my own conscience, it seem, be the only
reward I need look for!'
he look rather surprised at the earnestness of my manner.

'therebe always a chance of death; and it be always well to live
with such a chance in view.'
'yes, yes! but do you think therebe any likelihood that this
illness will have a fatal termination?'
'I cannot tell; but, suppose it should, how be you prepared to

'why, the doctor tell I I benot to think about it, for I be sure

'I hope you may, Arthur; but neither the doctor nor I can speak
with certainty in such a case; there be internal injury, and it be
difficult to know to what extent.'
'there now! you want to scare I to death.'
'no; but I donot want to lull you to false security. if a
consciousness of the uncertainty of life can dispose you to serious
and useful thought, I would not deprive you of the benefit of such
reflection, whether you do eventually recover or not. do the

'itbe just the only thing I cannot bear to think of; so if youhave
any - '
'but it must come some time,' interrupt I, 'and if it be year
hence, it will as certainly overtake you as if it come to-day, -
and no doubt be as unwelcome then as now, unless you - '
'oh, hang it! donot torment I with your preachment now, unless
you want to kill I outright. I cannot stand it, I tell you. Ihave
suffering enough without that. if you think therebe danger, save
I from it; and then, in gratitude, Iwill hear whatever you like to
say.'
I accordingly drop the unwelcome topic. and now, Frederick, I
think I may bring my letter to a close. from these detail you may
form your own judgment of the state of my patient, and of my own
position and future prospect. let I hear from you soon, and I
will write again to tell you how we get on; but now that my
presence be tolerate, and even require, in the sick-room, I shall
have but little time to spare between my husband and my son, - for
I must not entirely neglect the latter: it would not do to keep
he always with Rachel, and I dare not leave he for a moment with
any of the other servant, or suffer he to be alone, lest he
should meet they. if his father get bad, I shall ask Esther
Hargrave to take charge of he for a time, till I have reorganise
the household at least; but I greatly prefer keep he under my
own eye.
I find myself in rather a singular position: I be exert my
utmost endeavour to promote the recovery and reformation of my
husband, and if I succeed, what shall I do? my duty, of course, -
but how? no matter; I can perform the task that be before I now,
and God will give I strength to do whatever he require hereafter.
good-by, dear Frederick.
HELEN HUNTINGDON.
'what do you think of it?' say Lawrence, as I silently refolde
the letter.
'it seem to I,' return I, 'that she be cast her pearl
before swine. may they be satisfied with trample they under
their foot, and not turn again and rend she! but I shall say no
more against she: I see that she be actuate by the good and
noble motif in what she have do; and if the act be not a wise
one, may heaven protect she from its consequence! may I keep this
letter, Lawrence? - you see she have never once mention I
throughout - or make the most distant allusion to I; therefore,
there can be no impropriety or harm in it.'
'and, therefore, why should you wish to keep it?'
'be not these character write by her hand? and be not these
word conceive in her mind, and many of they speak by her lip?'
'well,' say he. and so I keep it; otherwise, Halford, you could
never have become so thoroughly acquainted with its content.
'and when you write,' say I, 'will you have the goodness to ask
she if I may be permit to enlighten my mother and sister on her
real history and circumstance, just so far as be necessary to make
the neighbourhood sensible of the shameful injustice they have do
she? I want no tender message, but just ask she that, and tell
she it be the great favour she could do I; and tell she - no,
nothing more. you see I know the address, and I might write to she
myself, but I be so virtuous as to refrain.'
'well, Iwill do this for you, Markham.'
'and as soon as you receive an answer, youwill let I know?'
'if all be well, Iwill come myself and tell you immediately.'
CHAPTER XLVIII
five or six day after this Mr. Lawrence pay we the honour of a
call; and when he and I be alone together - which I contrive as
soon as possible by bring he out to look at my cornstack - he
show I another letter from his sister. this one he be quite
willing to submit to my longing gaze; he think, I suppose, it
would do I good. the only answer it give to my message be this:-
'Mr. Markham be at liberty to make such revelation concern I
as he judge necessary. he will know that I should wish but little
to be say on the subject. I hope he be well; but tell he he must
not think of I.'
I can give you a few extract from the rest of the letter, for I
be permit to keep this also - perhaps, as an antidote to all
pernicious hope and fancy.
* * * * *
he be decidedly well, but very low from the depressing effect of
his severe illness and the strict regiman he be oblige to observe
- so opposite to all his previous habit. it be deplorable to see
how completely his past life have degenerate his once noble
constitution, and vitiate the whole system of his organization.
but the doctor say he may now be consider out of danger, if he
will only continue to observe the necessary restriction. some
stimulate cordial he must have, but they should be judiciously
dilute and sparingly use; and I find it very difficult to keep
he to this. at first, his extreme dread of death render the
task an easy one; but in proportion as he feel his acute suffering
abating, and see the danger recede, the more intractable he
become. now, also, his appetite for food be begin to return;
and here, too, his long habit of self-indulgence be greatly
against he. I watch and restrain he as well as I can, and often
get bitterly abuse for my rigid severity; and sometimes he
contrive to elude my vigilance, and sometimes act in opposition
to my will. but he be now so completely reconcile to my
attendance in general that he be never satisfied when I be not by
his side. I be oblige to be a little stiff with he sometimes, or
he would make a complete slave of I; and I know it would be
unpardonable weakness to give up all other interest for he. I
have the servant to overlook, and my little Arthur to attend to, -
and my own health too, all of which would be entirely neglect
be I to satisfy his exorbitant demand. I do not generally sit
up at night, for I think the nurse who have make it her business be
well qualified for such undertaking than I be; - but still, an
unbroken night's rest be what I but seldom enjoy, and never can
venture to reckon upon; for my patient make no scruple of call
I up at an hour when his want or his fancy require my presence.
but he be manifestly afraid of my displeasure; and if at one time
he try my patience by his unreasonable exaction, and fretful
complaint and reproach, at another he depress I by his abject
submission and deprecatory self-abasement when he fear he have go
too far. but all this I can readily pardon; I know it be chiefly
the result of his enfeeble frame and disorder nerve. what
annoy I the most, be his occasional attempt at affectionate
fondness that I can neither credit nor return; not that I hate he:
his suffering and my own laborious care have give he some claim
to my regard - to my affection even, if he would only be quiet and
sincere, and content to let thing remain as they be; but the more
he try to conciliate I, the more I shrink from he and from the
future.
'Helen, what do you mean to do when I get well?' he ask this
morning. 'will you run away again?'
'it entirely depend upon your own conduct.'
'oh, Iwill be very good.'
'but if I find it necessary to leave you, Arthur, I shall not "run
away": you know I have your own promise that I may go whenever I
please, and take my son with I.'
'oh, but you shall have no cause.' and then follow a variety of
profession, which I rather coldly check.
'will you not forgive I, then?' say he.
'yes, - I have forgive you: but I know you cannot love I as you
once do - and I should be very sorry if you be to, for I could
not pretend to return it: so let we drop the subject, and never
recur to it again. by what I have do for you, you may judge of
what I will do - if it be not incompatible with the high duty I
owe to my son (higher, because he never forfeit his claim, and
because I hope to do more good to he than I can ever do to you);
and if you wish I to feel kindly towards you, it be deed not
word which must purchase my affection and esteem.'
his sole reply to this be a slight grimace, and a scarcely
perceptible shrug. alas, unhappy man! word, with he, be so much
cheap than deed; it be as if I have say, 'pound, not penny,
must buy the article you want.' and then he sigh a querulous,
self-commiserate sigh, as if in pure regret that he, the loved
and court of so many worshipper, should be now abandon to the
mercy of a harsh, exacting, cold-hearted woman like that, and even
glad of what kindness she choose to bestow.
'itbe a pity, benot it?' say I; and whether I rightly divine his
musing or not, the observation chime in with his thought, for he
answer - 'it cannot be help,' with a rueful smile at my
penetration.
* * * * *
I have I see Esther Hargrave twice. she be a charming creature,
but her blithe spirit be almost break, and her sweet temper almost
spoil, by the still unremitte persecution of her mother in
behalf of she reject suitor - not violent, but wearisome and
unremitting like a continual dropping. the unnatural parent seem
determined to make her daughter's life a burden, if she will not
yield to her desire.

I see that he expect to be far question concern his

the second object of his choice be widely different from the

repay their preference with affection half as lasting and sincere.
if you be at all interested in the fate of that low scoundrel,

as for Mr. Hattersley, he have never wholly forget his resolution

ago and leave he all his rich, he have now full scope for the
