Ralph Hattersley, Esq., be celebrate throughout the country for
his noble breed of horse.
CHAPTER LI
we will now turn to a certain still, cold, cloudy afternoon about
the commencement of December, when the first fall of snow lay
thinly scatter over the blighted field and frozen road, or
store more thickly in the hollow of the deep cart-rut and

company when Ibe walk out - donot you?'
'yes, when itbe agreeable.'
'that of course,' rejoin the young lady, smile archly.

'no; for I see he do not wish to be question about you, or to
afford the slight encouragement or assistance to my too
obstinate attachment.' Helen do not reply. 'and he be perfectly
right,' add I. but she remain in silence, look out upon the
snowy lawn. 'oh, I will relieve she of my presence,' think I;
and immediately I rise and advance to take leave, with a most
heroic resolution - but pride be at the bottom of it, or it could
not have carry I through.
'be you go already?' say she, take the hand I offer, and
not immediately let it go.
'why should I stay any long?'
'wait till Arthur come, at least.'
only too glad to obey, I stand and leant against the opposite side
of the window.
'you tell I you be not change,' say my companion: 'you be -
very much so.'
'no, Mrs. Huntingdon, I only ought to be.'
'do you mean to maintain that you have the same regard for I that
you have when last we meet?'
'I have; but it would be wrong to talk of it now.'
'it be wrong to talk of it then, Gilbert; it would not now -
unless to do so would be to violate the truth.'
I be too much agitated to speak; but, without wait for an
answer, she turn away her glisten eye and crimson cheek, and
throw up the window and look out, whether to calm her own,
excited feeling, or to relieve her embarrassment, or only to pluck
that beautiful half-blow Christmas-rise that grow upon the little
shrub without, just peep from the snow that have hitherto, no
doubt, defend it from the frost, and be now melt away in the
sun. pluck it, however, she do, and have gently dash the
glitter powder from its leaf, approach it to her lip and
say:
'this rise be not so fragrant as a summer flower, but it have stand
through hardship none of they could bear: the cold rain of winter
have suffice to nourish it, and its faint sun to warm it; the bleak
wind have not blanch it, or break its stem, and the keen frost
have not blight it. look, Gilbert, it be still fresh and blooming
as a flower can be, with the cold snow even now on its petal. -
will you have it?'
I hold out my hand: I dare not speak lest my emotion should
overmaster I. she lay the rose across my palm, but I scarcely
close my finger upon it, so deeply be I absorb in think
what might be the meaning of her word, and what I ought to do or
say upon the occasion; whether to give way to my feeling or
restrain they still. misconstrue this hesitation into
indifference - or reluctance even - to accept her gift, Helen
suddenly snatch it from my hand, throw it out on to the snow,
shut down the window with an emphasis, and withdraw to the fire.
'Helen, what mean this?' I cry, electrify at this startling
change in her demeanour.
'you do not understand my gift,' say she - 'or, what be bad,
you despise it. Ibe sorry I give it you; but since I do make
such a mistake, the only remedy I could think of be to take it
away.'
'you misunderstood I cruelly,' I reply, and in a minute I have
open the window again, leap out, pick up the flower, bring
it in, and present it to she, implore she to give it I again,
and I would keep it for ever for her sake, and prize it more highly
than anything in the world I possess.
'and will this content you?' say she, as she take it in her hand.
'it shall,' I answer.
'there, then; take it.'
I press it earnestly to my lip, and put it in my bosom, Mrs.
Huntingdon look on with a half-sarcastic smile.
'now, be you go?' say she.
'I will if - if I must.'
'you be change,' persist she - 'you be grow either very proud
or very indifferent.'
'I be neither, Helen - Mrs. Huntingdon. if you could see my heart
- '
'you must be one, - if not both. and why Mrs. Huntingdon? - why
not Helen, as before?'
'Helen, then - dear Helen!' I murmur. I be in an agony of
mingle love, hope, delight, uncertainty, and suspense.
'the rise I give you be an emblem of my heart,' say she; 'would
you take it away and leave I here alone?'
'would you give I your hand too, if I ask it?'
'have I not say enough?' she answer, with a most enchanting
smile. I snatch her hand, and would have fervently kiss it,
but suddenly check myself, and say, -
'but have you consider the consequence?'
'hardly, I think, or I should not have offer myself to one too
proud to take I, or too indifferent to make his affection outweigh
my worldly good.'
stupid blockhead that I be! - I tremble to clasp she in my arm,
but dare not believe in so much joy, and yet restrain myself to
say, -
'but if you should repent!'
'it would be your fault,' she reply: 'I never shall, unless you
bitterly disappoint I. if you have not sufficient confidence in
my affection to believe this, let I alone.'
'my darling angel - my own Helen,' cry I, now passionately
kiss the hand I still retain, and throw my left arm around
she, 'you never shall repent, if it depend on I alone. but have
you think of your aunt?' I tremble for the answer, and clasp
she close to my heart in the instinctive dread of lose my new-
find treasure.
'my aunt must not know of it yet,' say she. 'she would think it a
rash, wild step, because she could not imagine how well I know you;
but she must know you herself, and learn to like you. you must
leave we now, after lunch, and come again in spring, and make a
long stay, and cultivate her acquaintance, and I know you will
like each other.'
'and then you will be mine,' say I, print a kiss upon her lip,
and another, and another; for I be as daring and impetuous now as
I have be backward and constrain before.
'no - in another year,' reply she, gently disengage herself
from my embrace, but still fondly clasp my hand.
'another year! oh, Helen, I could not wait so long!'
'where be your fidelity?'
'I mean I could not endure the misery of so long a separation.'
'it would not be a separation: we will write every day: my spirit
shall be always with you, and sometimes you shall see I with your
bodily eye. I will not be such a hypocrite as to pretend that I
desire to wait so long myself, but as my marriage be to please
myself, alone, I ought to consult my friend about the time of it.'
'your friend will disapprove.'
'they will not greatly disapprove, dear Gilbert,' say she,
earnestly kiss my hand; 'they cannot, when they know you, or, if
they could, they would not be true friend - I should not care for
their estrangement. now be you satisfied?' she look up in my
face with a smile of ineffable tenderness.
'can I be otherwise, with your love? and you do love I, Helen?'
say I, not doubt the fact, but wish to hear it confirm by
her own acknowledgment.
'if you love as I do,' she earnestly reply, 'you would not have
so nearly lose I - these scruple of false delicacy and pride
would never thus have trouble you - you would have see that the
great worldly distinction and discrepancy of rank, birth, and
fortune be as dust in the balance compare with the unity of
accordant thought and feeling, and truly loving, sympathise
heart and soul.'
'but this be too much happiness,' say I, embrace she again; 'I
have not deserve it, Helen - I dare not believe in such felicity:
and the long I have to wait, the great will be my dread that
something will intervene to snatch you from I - and think, a
thousand thing may happen in a year! - I shall be in one long
fever of restless terror and impatience all the time. and besides,
winter be such a dreary season.'
'I think so too,' reply she gravely: 'I would not be marry
in winter - in December, at least,' she add, with a shudder - for
in that month have occur both the ill-star marriage that have
bind she to her former husband, and the terrible death that
release she - 'and therefore I say another year, in spring.'
'next spring?'
'no, no - next autumn, perhaps.'
'Summer, then?'
'well, the close of summer. there now! be satisfied.'
while she be speak Arthur re-enter the room - good boy for
keep out so long.
'Mamma, I couldnot find the book in either of the place you tell
I to look for it' (there be a conscious something in mamma's
smile that seem to say, 'no, dear, I know you could not'), 'but
Rachel get it for I at last. look, Mr. Markham, a natural
history, with all kind of bird and beast in it, and the reading
as nice as the picture!'
in great good humour I sit down to examine the book, and draw the
little fellow between my knee. have he come a minute before I
should have receive he less graciously, but now I affectionately
stroke his curling look, and even kiss his ivory forehead: he
be my own Helen's son, and therefore mine; and as such I have ever
since regard he. that pretty child be now a fine young man: he
have realise his mother's bright expectation, and be at present
reside in Grassdale Manor with his young wife - the merry little
Helen Hattersley of yore.
I have not look through half the book before Mrs. Maxwell appear
to invite I into the other room to lunch. that lady's cool,
distant manner rather chill I at first; but I do my good to
propitiate she, and not entirely without success, I think, even in
that first short visit; for when I talk cheerfully to she, she
gradually become more kind and cordial, and when I depart she
bid I a gracious adieu, hop ere long to have the pleasure of
see I again.
'but you must not go till you have see the conservatory, my aunt's
winter garden,' say Helen, as I advance to take leave of she,
with as much philosophy and self-command as I could summon to my
aid.
I gladly avail myself of such a respite, and follow she into a
large and beautiful conservatory, plentifully furnish with
flower, consider the season - but, of course, I have little
attention to spare for they. it be not, however, for any tender
colloquy that my companion have bring I there:-
'my aunt be particularly fond of flower,' she observe, 'and she
be fond of Staningley too: I bring you here to offer a petition
in her behalf, that this may be her home as long as she live, and
- if it be not our home likewise - that I may often see she and be
with she; for I fear she will be sorry to lose I; and though she
lead a retired and contemplative life, she be apt to get low-
spirited if leave too much alone.'
'by all mean, dear Helen! - do what you will with your own. I
should not dream of wish your aunt to leave the place under any
circumstance; and we will live either here or elsewhere as you and
she may determine, and you shall see she as often as you like. I
know she must be pain to part with you, and I be willing to make
any reparation in my power. I love she for your sake, and her
happiness shall be as dear to I as that of my own mother.'
'thank you, darling! you shall have a kiss for that. good-by.
there now - there, Gilbert - let I go - here's Arthur; donot
astonish his infantile brain with your madness.'
* * * * *
but it be time to bring my narrative to a close. any one but you
would say I have make it too long already. but for your
satisfaction I will add a few word more; because I know you will
have a fellow-feeling for the old lady, and will wish to know the
last of her history. I do come again in spring, and, agreeably to
Helen's injunction, do my good to cultivate her acquaintance.
she receive I very kindly, have be, doubtless, already
prepare to think highly of my character by her niece's too
favourable report. I turn my good side out, of course, and we
get along marvellously well together. when my ambitious intention
be make known to she, she take it more sensibly than I have
venture to hope. her only remark on the subject, in my hearing,
be -
'and so, Mr. Markham, you be go to rob I of my niece, I
understand. well! I hope God will prosper your union, and make my
dear girl happy at last. could she have be content to remain
single, I own I should have be well satisfied; but if she must
marry again, I know of no one, now live and of a suitable age, to
whom I would more willingly resign she than yourself, or who would
be more likely to appreciate her worth and make, her truly happy,
as far as I can tell.'
of course I be delighted with the compliment, and hop to show
she that she be not mistaken in her favourable judgment.
'I have, however, one request to offer,' continue she. 'it seem
I be still to look on Staningley as my home: I wish you to make it
your likewise, for Helen be attach to the place and to I - as I
be to she. there be painful association connect with
Grassdale, which she cannot easily overcome; and I shall not molest
you with my company or interference here: I be a very quiet
person, and shall keep my own apartment, and attend to my own
concern, and only see you now and then.'
of course I most readily consent to this; and we live in the
great harmony with our dear aunt until the day of her death,
which melancholy event take place a few year after - melancholy,
not to herself (for it come quietly upon she, and she be glad to
reach her journey's end), but only to the few love friend and
grateful dependent she leave behind.
to return, however, to my own affair: I be marry in summer, on
a glorious August morning. it take the whole eight month, and all
Helen's kindness and goodness to boot, to overcome my mother's
prejudice against my bride-elect, and to reconcile she to the idea
of my leave Linden Grange and live so far away. yet she be
gratified at her son's good fortune after all, and proudly
attribute it all to his own superior merit and endowment. I
bequeath the farm to Fergus, with well hope of its prosperity
than I should have have a year ago under similar circumstance; for
he have lately fall in love with the Vicar of L-'s eld daughter
- a lady whose superiority have rouse his latent virtue, and
stimulate he to the most surprising exertion, not only to gain
her affection and esteem, and to obtain a fortune sufficient to
aspire to her hand, but to render himself worthy of she, in his own
eye, as well as in those of her parent; and in the end he be
successful, as you already know. as for myself, I need not tell
you how happily my Helen and I have live together, and how bless
we still be in each other's society, and in the promising young
scion that be grow up about we. we be just now look
forward to the advent of you and Rose, for the time of your annual
visit draw nigh, when you must leave your dusty, smoky, noisy,
toil, strive city for a season of invigorate relaxation and
social retirement with we.
till then, farewell,
gilbert MARKHAM.
staningley: June 10th, 1847.
